Innovation Ranking Criteria

Papers are ranked from most to least innovative based on five criteria: (1) novelty of problem formulation or paradigm shift, (2) creativity of methodology, (3) surprisingness of results or insights, (4) potential impact on the field, and (5) degree of departure from existing approaches. Each paper includes a justification explaining its placement.

≥90 Groundbreaking
≥75 Highly Innovative
≥60 Innovative
≥40 Moderately Innovative
≥20 Incremental
<20 Routine

Innovation Rankings

#1 — Highly Innovative (87)

A Built-in Crypto Expert for Artificial Intelligence: How Far is the Horizon?

eprint.iacr.org · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: This paper introduces a genuinely novel paradigm: embedding cryptographic operations natively within an LLM's differentiable computation graph rather than bolting them on externally. The algebraic design of SoftXOR, SoftLUT (as key-value attention), and GF-conv primitives is elegant and deeply creative, achieving a 10.4× neuron reduction and ~160× latency improvement over ReLU baselines while provably converging to exact discrete AES at inference. The demonstrated resistance to continuous differential attacks that completely break prior ReLU-based approaches represents a meaningful security advance. While still at proof-of-concept scale, this work opens a fundamentally new design space for AI-native cryptographic security.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Modern LLMs and agentic AI systems face a fundamental security gap: cryptographic protections are bolted on externally rather than embedded structurally, leaving models vulnerable to prompt injection, sensitive-data leakage, and supply-chain attacks. Prior work (Gerault et al.) showed that naively implementing AES as a ReLU-based deep neural network is both parameter-heavy and vulnerable to continuous differential attacks — adversaries can recover secret keys in near-linear time by exploiting the piecewise-linear structure of ReLU activations. This paper asks whether a cryptographic module can be embedded *natively* inside an LLM's computation graph in a way that is fully differentiable during training, provably correct at inference, and resistant to differential attacks. Methodology: The authors design a differentiable AES proxy using three custom neuron primitives tailored to AES's algebraic structure. SoftXOR implements bitwise XOR as a probabilistic polynomial over soft-bit probabilities using scaled sigmoids with a sharpness hyperparameter γ, providing smooth non-zero gradients everywhere. SoftLUT implements the AES S-box (SubBytes) as a key-value query (KVQ) attention mechanism: each input byte is encoded as an 8-dimensional bipolar binary key, matched against all 256 possible AES byte values via scaled dot-product attention with temperature τ, and the output is a weighted sum of S-box outputs — recovering exact discrete lookup as τ→0. GF-conv implements MixColumns as a 1D convolution with precomputed 8×8 binary transformation matrices over GF(2⁸), with field additions relaxed to SoftXOR reductions via a binary tree that reduces backpropagation depth from O(n) to O(log n). ShiftRows is a fixed sparse permutation matrix. These primitives share a unified soft-bit tensor interface and use curriculum-scheduled annealing of γ and τ to trade gradient smoothness for discreteness during training. The differentiable AES module is inserted as a specialized expert in a Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) LLM (MiniMind architecture). A RouterNet learns to identify tokens tagged as sensitive payloads and route them to the AES Expert; at inference the differentiable simulator is replaced wholesale by a standard discrete AES implementation, with an Embedder trained during calibration to align the simulator's continuous representations with real AES ciphertext embeddings. Key Results: On neuron count, the proposed differentiable AES requires 4,864 neurons per round vs. 50,816 for the ReLU baseline — a 10.4× reduction overall (3.0× for AddRoundKey, 3.9× for SubBytes, 66.2× for MixColumns). On inference latency at batch size 1, the approach takes ~372–385 ms vs. 4,395 ms for the ReLU baseline; at batch size 10 the gap widens to ~393 ms vs. 63,208 ms (~160× faster). Crucially, the continuous differential attack that completely breaks ReLU-based AES-DNN yields exactly 256 ciphertext collisions per byte guess against the proposed system — meaning the attacker cannot distinguish the correct key from any other, so the attack fails completely. This resistance stems from the SoftLUT's near-zero temperature causing aggressive rounding of any continuous perturbation to the nearest AES S-box entry. The integrated MiniMind+AES Expert system achieves ~410 tokens/second throughput and correctly encrypts sensitive spans inline during generation. Strengths: - Elegant algebraic alignment: each differentiable primitive (SoftXOR, SoftLUT, GF-conv) directly mirrors the mathematical structure of the corresponding AES operation, rather than treating AES as a black-box Boolean function to memorize, yielding dramatic parameter efficiency. - Provable train-to-inference correctness: formal convergence proofs show each primitive recovers exact discrete AES behavior as γ→∞ and τ→0, and the simulator-to-real swap guarantees standard AES semantics at deployment. - Resistance to the specific differential attack class that defeats ReLU-based constructions, with experimental validation producing uniform ciphertext collisions across all 256 key guesses. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The security argument assumes a *trusted trainer*: during training, the differentiable simulator is exposed and could itself be subject to continuous differential attacks if the training environment is compromised; the authors acknowledge this requires TEE deployment for the AES Expert. - The routing mechanism introduces a failure mode: if the RouterNet misclassifies sensitive tokens, those spans are transmitted in plaintext without any fallback; the paper does not quantify routing accuracy or false-negative rates in adversarial prompt settings. - The evaluation is limited to a small proof-of-concept model (MiniMind) and does not demonstrate integration with production-scale LLMs; generalization of the Embedder calibration step and downstream task degradation at scale remain open questions.

#2 — Highly Innovative (83)

Evo: Autoregressive-Diffusion Large Language Models with Evolving Balance

Junde Wu, Minhao Hu, Jiayuan Zhu, Yuyuan Liu, Tianyi Zhang, Kang Li, Jingkun Chen, Jiazhen Pan, Min Xu, Yueming Jin · arxiv.org · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: Evo scores 83 for its theoretically principled unification proving that AR and diffusion models are discretizations of the same probability flow ODE — a genuinely surprising and elegant result that reframes the field's understanding of these two paradigms. The learned per-token progression variable that adaptively allocates refinement effort is a creative methodological contribution that solves a real practical problem. The empirical results are exceptionally strong, with massive gains on reasoning and code benchmarks (+33.7 on GSM8K over LLaMA3) while maintaining near-AR inference speed, demonstrating that the theoretical insight translates into practical advantage. The broad applicability across 15 benchmarks and clear superiority over prior hybrid approaches suggest significant potential impact on how future LLMs are designed.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Autoregressive (AR) LLMs like GPT and Claude dominate language generation but are limited by strictly left-to-right decoding and compounding errors from greedy/approximate decoding heuristics. Diffusion-based LLMs offer global planning and iterative self-correction but suffer from high inference cost, poor perplexity, and lack of strategic sequential planning. Existing AR–diffusion hybrids (e.g., ARD, BD3-LM) apply diffusion in a fixed autoregressive order or at rigid block boundaries, preventing adaptive allocation of refinement effort based on semantic uncertainty. The gap is a unified framework that fluidly interpolates between AR-style committed generation and diffusion-style global planning at the token level. Methodology: Evo introduces a duality latent trajectory framework grounded in a theoretical unification: it proves that AR models and score-based diffusion models are both discretizations of the same underlying continuous-time probability flow ODE, differing only in time parametrization and stochasticity. Building on this, Evo models text generation as a continuous latent flow where each token $x_i$ is associated with a latent vector $z_i \in \mathbb{R}^d$ that evolves along a shared vector field $\mathcal{F}_\theta$ governed by a per-token progression variable $t_i \in [0,1]$. Low $t_i$ signals high semantic maturity (AR-like committed refinement); high $t_i$ signals uncertainty (diffusion-style planning). The $t_i$ values are predicted jointly with the latent codes by a learned posterior via backpropagation through a variational ELBO, rather than fixed by a heuristic schedule. The model is a single decoder-only Transformer augmented with sinusoidal time embeddings, trained end-to-end. During inference, it samples initial latent states and progression steps, then deterministically refines each token's latent trajectory over $K_{\max}=20$ steps, with per-token update depth $K_i = \lfloor K_{\max} \cdot t_i \rfloor$, enabling early stopping for confident tokens. Pretraining uses 3.4T tokens; the main reported model is Evo 8.5B (XL configuration). Key Results: Evo 8B is evaluated against AR-only (LLaMA3 8B, Qwen2.5 7B), diffusion-only (LLaDA 8B, MDLM 7B), and AR+diffusion hybrid (BD3-LM 7B, ARD 7B) baselines on 15 benchmarks. In the pre-trained setting, Evo leads on GSM8K (86.4 vs. LLaMA3's 52.7, +33.7), MATH (54.9 vs. 15.4), GPQA (38.4 vs. 26.5), HumanEval (60.6 vs. 34.6, +26.0), and MBPP (77.4 vs. 47.1). Compared to the best AR+diffusion hybrid BD3-LM, Evo improves GSM8K by +6.6, MATH by +12.0, GPQA by +7.9, and HumanEval by +10.0. Post-training gains persist: Evo 8B reaches 92.5 on ARC-C, 78.8 on MATH, and 86.7 on HumanEval. Crucially, Evo achieves near-AR inference speed (52 tokens/s, 8.6s latency), matching LLaMA3 (58 tokens/s, 7.4s) and dramatically outperforming BD3-LM (28 tokens/s, 14.2s) and ARD (12 tokens/s, 32.5s). Ablations confirm that removing time conditioning drops task accuracy from 81.6 to 69.1, and that learned $t_i$ outperforms all fixed or entropy-heuristic alternatives. Strengths: - Theoretically principled unification: formally proves AR and diffusion are discretizations of the same probability flow ODE, giving the hybrid a rigorous foundation rather than an ad hoc combination. - Adaptive, token-wise computation: learned progression times $t_i$ dynamically allocate refinement effort to uncertain tokens only, achieving near-AR speed while preserving diffusion's global planning benefits. - Strong and broad empirical gains: outperforms both AR-only and prior AR+diffusion hybrids across reasoning, math, code, and language understanding, without sacrificing inference throughput. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Higher training cost than pure AR or diffusion models: jointly learning latent flows and time-step predictors requires more compute, with wall-clock pretraining up to seven weeks for the largest scale. - Reduced controllability and interpretability: generating via a shared semantic scaffold rather than strict left-to-right token commitment makes fine-grained length control and output steering harder, especially for long-form generation; guiding scaffold evolution remains an open problem.

#3 — Highly Innovative (83)

Shared Spotlight Meridian: Sparse Distributed Pseudorandom Functions for Scalable Federated Learning

staff.ie.cuhk.edu.hk · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: This paper defines a new cryptographic primitive (Distributed Sparse PRFs) that elegantly resolves a long-standing incompatibility between gradient sparsification and secure aggregation in federated learning — two optimizations that previously could not coexist. The 'shared spotlight' mechanism is a creative co-design insight, and the construction achieves near-plaintext communication cost (1 extra bit per nonzero element) with rigorous security proofs under standard assumptions. The comprehensive evaluation across vision, NLP, and recommendation domains with models up to 70B parameters demonstrates practical viability. The multi-server trust assumption is a real constraint but does not diminish the theoretical contribution.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Secure federated learning (FL) requires clients to collaboratively train models while keeping raw data private. Two natural optimizations—gradient sparsification (transmitting only top-k gradient entries to reduce communication) and secure aggregation (cryptographic protocols to hide individual updates)—are fundamentally incompatible with existing approaches. Current secure aggregation protocols must pad every coordinate even when gradients are sparse, negating all communication savings. Conversely, quantization and sparsification methods that do reduce communication forfeit cryptographic privacy. The gap between ML-side communication optimization and cryptography-side privacy has remained largely unaddressed. Methodology: The paper introduces Distributed Sparse Pseudorandom Functions (DSPRFs), a new cryptographic primitive that resolves the sparsification–privacy tension through a "security–ML co-design" philosophy. The key insight is a "shared spotlight" mechanism: each client secretly shares knowledge of a single gradient coordinate (the spotlight/meridian) among servers via distributed keys. This hidden coordinate serves as an implicit alignment reference, enabling servers to recover the sum of sparse gradients without learning which coordinate it is. Two variants are constructed: (1) Static Distributed Sparse PRFs (StaF) for clients with a fixed, pre-known sparse support (e.g., embedding layers), built on Distributed Point Functions (DPFs) and the Learning With Errors (LWE) assumption; and (2) Dynamic Distributed Sparse PRFs (DynF) for clients whose sparse support varies per round (e.g., top-k attention gradients), built on low-degree Random Sparse PRGs (RSPRGs) and Homomorphic Secret Sharing (HSS). To support an unbounded number of FL rounds despite DynF's polynomial-size input domain, a pipelining trick streams distributed key bits to servers one bit per round, refreshing keys seamlessly. The resulting FL protocol requires ≥2 non-colluding servers; privacy holds unless all servers collude. For each nonzero gradient element, the client communication cost is at most log N + log q + 1 bits (one bit more than plaintext), compared to Ω(N · log q) for Flamingo and Ω(λ · log q) for 2-Party DPF approaches. Client computation overhead is one AES + one LWE inner product per gradient element for static sparsity, plus one local-PRG invocation for dynamic sparsity. Key Results: Experiments span image classification (ResNet-18/50 on CIFAR-10, CIFAR-100, Tiny ImageNet), language understanding (RoBERTa fine-tuned on CoLA and MRPC with LoRA), and recommendation systems (MF and NCF on MovieLens/Yelp; Caser and SASRec on Amazon datasets). For image classification with dynamic sparse gradients, the protocol achieves 5×–200× communication reduction over Flamingo and 90× average reduction over 2-server DPF at sparsification ratios from 0.2 to 0.005. For language understanding at ρ=0.1, communication cost matches plaintext (e.g., 0.83 MB vs. 0.83 MB for DistillBERT) while Flamingo costs 96.72 MB and DPF costs 4.10 MB. For recommendation with static sparsity, communication matches plaintext exactly (e.g., 0.04 MB vs. 0.04 MB for MF on ML1M) while Flamingo costs 0.99 MB. Against lossy compression baselines (Bit8Quant, TernQuant), the protocol achieves 13×–26× communication reduction while maintaining equivalent or slightly better accuracy (e.g., CoLA ACC: 0.841 vs. 0.840/0.839; MRPC ACC: 0.864 vs. 0.842/0.843). Accuracy loss from sparsification at ρ=0.2 is only ~0.7% for image classification, and ≤1% for language understanding at ρ≥0.3. Client computation overhead is 0.87% extra for recommendation and 0.29% for language understanding tasks. Strengths: - Achieves near-plaintext communication cost (at most 1 bit overhead per nonzero gradient element) while providing cryptographic privacy, closing a long-standing gap between FL communication efficiency and security - Novel cryptographic primitive (DSPRF) with rigorous security proofs under standard assumptions (LWE hardness, DPF security), applicable beyond FL - Comprehensive empirical evaluation across three distinct ML domains (vision, NLP, recommendation) with models ranging from 67M to 70B parameters, demonstrating consistent plaintext-level communication with negligible accuracy loss Weaknesses / Limitations: - Requires ≥2 non-colluding servers; the multi-server trust model is a deployment constraint not present in single-server approaches like Flamingo, and extension to the malicious adversary setting is left as future work - Server computation is not optimized: decrypting dynamic sparse gradients involves AES, LWE operations, and low-degree HSS evaluation, with estimated server-side time of M/3 seconds per core per FL round (where M is the total gradient elements from participating clients), which may be non-trivial at large scale

#4 — Highly Innovative (82)

Matching Features, Not Tokens: Energy-Based Fine-Tuning of Language Models

Samy Jelassi, Mujin Kwun, Rosie Zhao, Yuanzhi Li, Nicolo Fusi, Yilun Du, Sham M. Kakade, Carles Domingo-Enrich · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: This paper introduces a genuinely novel training objective (Energy-Based Fine-Tuning) that addresses the fundamental train/test distribution mismatch of teacher-forced SFT through feature-matching in embedding space, with elegant theoretical connections to energy-based models and proper scoring rules. The key innovation is eliminating the need for any task-specific reward or verifier while simultaneously improving accuracy, cross-entropy, and distributional calibration — breaking the accuracy/calibration tradeoff inherent to RLVR. The strided block-parallel rollout generation and feature whitening via pseudoinverse are creative engineering contributions. Results matching RLVR without any correctness signal on coding tasks, and outperforming it on translation, are surprising and practically significant.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Standard supervised fine-tuning (SFT) via cross-entropy (CE) under teacher forcing optimizes next-token prediction on ground-truth prefixes, but at deployment the model must condition on its own generated outputs. This train/test distribution mismatch causes compounding errors over long sequences — empirically, a conditional feature-matching (CFM) loss (measuring how well a model's rollout distribution matches the data distribution in embedding space) grows monotonically with completion length even for low-perplexity SFT models. Reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR) addresses behavioral calibration but requires a task-specific correctness signal and degrades validation cross-entropy, representing a fundamental accuracy/calibration tradeoff. The paper targets the gap left by both: a fine-tuning objective that improves sequence-level distributional calibration without requiring any reward or verifier. Methodology: The authors introduce Energy-Based Fine-Tuning (EBFT), which directly optimizes a feature-matching loss: the squared L2 distance between the mean feature embedding of model rollouts and that of ground-truth completions, where features are extracted from a frozen copy of the base model (concatenating intermediate activations at depths 25%, 50%, 75%, normalized per block). Because this loss cannot be estimated directly from (context, ground-truth) pairs alone, they derive an unbiased REINFORCE estimator by sampling n completions per context and computing per-rollout rewards with an RLOO (leave-one-out) baseline. Each reward has an alignment term (inner product of rollout features with ground-truth features) and a diversity term (negative mean inner product across rollout pairs). Training efficiency is achieved via strided block-parallel rollout generation using a custom attention mask (from Quiet-STaR), which amortizes prefix computation and enables batched feature extraction across multiple anchored contexts from a single sequence. Feature whitening via the Moore-Penrose pseudoinverse of the empirical second-moment matrix is applied to handle anisotropy and improve conditioning. The practical objective mixes the feature-matching loss with a small CE regularization coefficient γ ≥ 0 to stabilize cross-entropy. Under KL regularization, EBFT is shown to implicitly define an energy-based model whose optimal policy takes the form of an exponential tilt of the base distribution — connecting feature matching to classical energy-based modeling and proper scoring rules. Key Results: EBFT is evaluated on Q&A coding (OpenCodeInstruct, Qwen2.5-1.5B), unstructured coding (SwallowCode), and translation (ALMA, Llama-3.2-1B). On Q&A coding, EBFT achieves greedy HumanEval 0.548 vs. SFT 0.483 and pass@16 0.771 vs. 0.728 for SFT, matching RLVR (0.535 greedy, 0.752 pass@16) without any correctness signal. EBFT achieves the best feature-matching loss across all methods (0.258 vs. 0.315 SFT and 0.442 RLVR), and the best validation CE (0.207 vs. 0.289 SFT; RLVR degrades to 0.774). On unstructured coding where RLVR is inapplicable, EBFT outperforms SFT on all pass@k metrics (pass@16: 0.769 vs. 0.747). On translation, EBFT achieves the highest COMET scores (greedy WMT22: 0.725 vs. SFT 0.717 and RLVR 0.697) and the lowest CE (1.670 vs. SFT 1.782). Feature-matching improvements generalize beyond the 8-token training horizon to completion lengths up to 64 tokens. Ablations confirm that last-token pooling with whitening is critical; random-weight feature networks hurt only modestly; scaling the feature network from 1.5B to 7B yields no additional gains. EBFT improvements are consistent across model scales (1.5B, 3B, 7B) with no sign of diminishing returns. Strengths: - Addresses a genuine fundamental limitation of teacher-forced training (distribution shift at inference) with a principled, theoretically grounded objective connected to proper scoring rules and energy-based models. - Requires no task-specific reward, verifier, or preference data, making it applicable in non-verifiable settings where RLVR is unavailable — demonstrated concretely on raw-code continuation. - Simultaneously improves downstream task accuracy, validation cross-entropy (better than SFT despite not optimizing CE directly), and distributional calibration — avoiding the accuracy/calibration tradeoff inherent to RLVR. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Significantly higher compute cost than SFT: a single EBFT epoch takes ~36 hours on two 80GB H100s (unoptimized, without vLLM), compared to 0.5 hours for SFT, due to on-policy rollout generation and feature network forward passes. - Evaluated only on models up to 7B parameters with short rollout horizons (G=4–8 tokens); scaling behavior for longer rollouts or much larger models is unknown, and the frozen feature network may become a bottleneck at larger scales.

#5 — Highly Innovative (82)

Automated Tensor-Relational Decomposition for Large-Scale Sparse Tensor Computation

Yuxin Tang, Zhiyuan Xin, Zhimin Ding, Xinyu Yao, Daniel Bourgeois, Tirthak Patel, Chris Jermaine · arxiv.org · karypis - new results
Why this rank: This paper scores 82 for introducing a principled, formally grounded unification of relational and tensor computation through the upper-case-lower-case EinSum notation—a genuinely novel abstraction that makes the sparsity-density decomposition decision explicit and automatically optimizable. The SPARSEEINSUM compiler, with its sparsity-aware cost model and dynamic programming search over decomposition choices, represents a creative methodology that bridges two traditionally separate computational paradigms. The practical impact is substantial: enabling GCN training on 111M-node graphs where DGL/PyTorch simply runs out of memory, and achieving 30–100× speedups over pure relational systems, demonstrates that this formalism unlocks workloads previously infeasible in either paradigm alone. The work is tempered by the greedy heuristic for shared intermediates and sensitivity to cost model calibration, but the core contribution—making tensor-relational decomposition a first-class compiler optimization—could significantly influence how sparse ML workloads are compiled and executed.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Large-scale machine learning workloads (graph neural networks, attention mechanisms, quantum circuit simulation) involve tensor computations over highly sparse data. Pure tensor frameworks (PyTorch/DGL) require storing full dense tensors in GPU RAM—infeasible for graphs with 100M+ vertices—while pure relational implementations miss opportunities to use high-performance dense kernels, leaving up to 99.9% of GPU compute unused on sparse data. The gap is that no existing system automatically decides which parts of a tensor computation should be handled relationally (exploiting sparsity) versus via efficient numerical kernels (exploiting density). Methodology: The paper introduces upper-case-lower-case EinSum (a tensor-relational variant of Einstein Summation Notation) and the SPARSEEINSUM compiler that automatically rewrites a DAG of standard EinSum expressions into optimized upper-case-lower-case EinSum. In this notation, indices written in upper case are "promoted" to be handled by relational joins/aggregations, while lower-case indices are "demoted" into tensor kernels. Any EinSum expression can be translated to SQL using this decomposition. SPARSEEINSUM consists of two main components: (1) a sparsity-aware cost model that estimates the number of intermediate tuples using tensor statistics T(U) (non-zero count) and V(l, U) (distinct values per label), computing costs for join, aggregation, and repartition operations; and (2) a dynamic programming algorithm (Algorithm 1: ComputeMinCostForTree) that searches over all possible decompositions of each EinSum expression, propagating optimal promoted-label sets through a topologically-sorted DAG. TACO-generated kernels handle the dense inner computations; PlinyCompute serves as the distributed relational execution engine. Key Results: On distributed GCN training (ogbn-papers100M, 111M nodes, 1.6B edges), DGL/PyTorch runs out of memory on all cluster sizes, while SPARSEEINSUM completes in 42.4–289.5 seconds on 1–8 machines, achieving 5.0× speedup from 1 to 8 machines. On ogbn-products with 8 machines, SPARSEEINSUM is 8.6%–40% faster than DGL. For single-machine sparse attention computation, SPARSEEINSUM is 30×–100× faster than pure relational implementations (Hyper, PostgreSQL) and handles cases where pure relational systems run out of disk. For graph convolution (single machine), SPARSEEINSUM is up to 10× faster than pure relational+Hyper on larger datasets. The dynamic programming search outperforms a greedy baseline significantly (e.g., 4.5s vs. 21s on Cora for sparse attention). Compilation overhead (schema computation, SQL generation, kernel generation) is dominated by SQL data loading and is negligible relative to repeated execution cost. Strengths: - Principled unification of relational and tensor computation via a formal notation that makes decomposition choices explicit and automatically optimizable - Cost model handles sparsity estimation correctly using information-theoretic tuple-count formulas, and is shown robust to moderate cost parameter perturbations - Demonstrated end-to-end on diverse workloads (GCN, sparse/dense attention, quantum circuit simulation) with substantial speedups over both pure tensor and pure relational baselines Weaknesses / Limitations: - The dynamic programming algorithm is not globally optimal when tensors are consumed by multiple EinSum expressions (shared intermediates); the paper uses a greedy tree-decomposition heuristic in those cases, which may miss globally optimal solutions - Cost model accuracy degrades with large cost parameter perturbations (Γ(1/2, 3) or Γ(1/10, 10) errors cause up to 22× slowdowns), meaning inaccurate hardware calibration can substantially hurt performance - Higher-degree (non-binary) EinSum expressions require an external contraction-path optimizer as a preprocessing step, and are noted as future work for deeper integration

#6 — Highly Innovative (82)

Understanding by Reconstruction: Reversing the Software Development Process for LLM Pretraining

Zhiyuan Zeng, Yichi Zhang, Yong Shan, Kai Hua, Siyuan Fang, Zhaiyu Liu, Jiaheng Liu, Haozhe Wang, Yining Zheng, Ming Ding, Ke Shen, Ge Zhang, Wenhao Huang, Xipeng Qiu · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: This paper scores 82 for its elegant insight that static code repositories are lossy compressions of rich reasoning processes, and that reconstructing those processes yields superior pretraining data. The reverse-engineering paradigm—using multi-agent trajectory curation grounded in real structural data (ASTs, dependency graphs)—is a creative and well-motivated departure from standard code pretraining. The search-based CoT optimization, which treats optimal reasoning as the thought minimizing ground-truth code perplexity, is a lightweight yet principled alternative to RL. Most impressively, gains transfer beyond coding to long-context retrieval and general reasoning, suggesting the structured causal data provides broadly useful supervision.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: LLMs excel at code generation for short snippets but struggle with long-horizon reasoning required for complex software engineering. The core issue is that static code repositories—the dominant form of pretraining data—represent only the terminal state of a rich intellectual process. They abstract away the intermediate steps of requirement analysis, architectural planning, trial-and-error debugging, and iterative refinement. Training on static code thus teaches models to mimic surface-level structural patterns rather than mastering the generative reasoning required to derive them. This mismatch explains why models that generate short snippets well often fail at constructing and maintaining complex software systems. Methodology: The paper proposes "understanding via reconstruction"—a pretraining data synthesis paradigm that reverse-engineers the latent agentic trajectories hidden behind static repositories. The framework has two stages. First, Multi-Agent Trajectory Curation: a powerful LLM (Qwen3-30B-A3B-Instruct-2507) simulates a software development workflow over ~300k GitHub repositories. A Main Agent generates high-level project requirements and dependency-aware implementation plans; Sub-Agents then handle individual file creation via Think→Read→Write tool-use sequences. Crucially, hallucination is controlled by injecting ground-truth structural data extracted from the real repository (file hierarchy, inter-file dependency graphs, AST-derived intra-file structure), and tool-call responses are replaced with actual file contents. Second, LongCoT Optimization via Search: the initial synthetic Chain-of-Thought reasoning may be suboptimal. The paper refines it by treating high-quality reasoning z* as argmax_z log p(x|z)—i.e., the thought that minimizes the perplexity of the ground-truth code. A tree-search-inspired algorithm iteratively samples k=2 candidate refinements per CoT step, keeps any that lower PPL on the target code, and repeats for 3 rounds. This converts causally incomplete thoughts into logically rigorous ones. The resulting 4B-token dataset of flattened hierarchical trajectories is used for continuous pretraining of Llama-3-8B-Instruct with targeted loss masking: Observation tokens (tool responses) are masked, forcing the model to learn only Think and Action generation. Key Results: Continuous pretraining on Repo2Agent-Search trajectories consistently outperforms training on raw repository code (Raw-Repos) and matches or exceeds the strong Prolong external baseline across diverse benchmarks. On long-context understanding (Ruler benchmark), Repo2Agent-Search achieves 61.80 at 64k context vs. 61.00 for Raw-Repos and 57.10 for Prolong—most pronounced on NIAH-Multi (80.40 vs. 76.30 for Raw-Repos). On Helmet at 32k context, Repo2Agent-Search scores 62.65 vs. 60.98 for Raw-Repos. On coding, HumanEval improves from 34.76 (Raw-Repos) to 37.20 (Repo2Agent-Search); LongCodeBench-32k improves from 34.16 to 36.46. On general reasoning (BBH), Repo2Agent-Search scores 67.03 vs. 66.27 for Raw-Repos. On APTBench (agentic capabilities), Repo2Agent achieves 30.10 overall vs. 29.02 for Raw-Repos, with particular strength in Issue-Fix planning (34.84%). CoT optimization analysis confirms that more search iterations increase CoT length (from ~200 to ~1400 tokens over 10 steps) while steadily decreasing target code perplexity, validating the quality-improvement mechanism. Strengths: - Elegant insight that static code is a lossy compression of a reasoning process, and that reconstructing the process provides richer supervision than next-token prediction on code alone - Search-based CoT optimization is lightweight compared to RL, yet demonstrably improves reasoning quality as measured by ground-truth code perplexity - Grounding the LLM simulation with real structural data (file trees, dependency graphs, ASTs) substantially reduces hallucination risk—a key failure mode of pure LLM-simulated trajectories - Benefits generalize beyond coding to long-context retrieval and general reasoning, suggesting the structured causal data transfers broadly Weaknesses / Limitations: - Experiments are limited to Llama-3-8B; it is unclear whether gains hold for larger models where raw code pretraining may already provide sufficient signal - The trajectory synthesis pipeline itself depends on a large, capable LLM (Qwen3-30B), making data generation computationally expensive and subject to the quality ceiling of that model - Repo2Agent-Search underperforms Prolong at the 65k context length on Helmet LongQA (57.84 vs. 58.10), and the Prolong baseline uses a more carefully curated data mixture, making direct comparisons partially confounded
Recommended 2603.11103 PDF
#7 — Highly Innovative (79)

The Model Knows Which Tokens Matter: Automatic Token Selection via Noise Gating

Landi He, Xiaoyu Yang, Lijian Xu · arxiv.org · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: AutoSelect earns 79 for its theoretically principled reframing of visual token pruning as capacity-constrained representation learning via variance-preserving noise gating—a genuinely creative departure from heuristic pruning methods. The VP noise gate elegantly solves the gradient-flow problem of discrete token selection while providing a continuous surrogate provably equivalent to hard Top-K at convergence. The diagonal-attention Denoiser design insight (preventing information leakage) and the dramatic efficiency advantage (0.69ms vs 43ms for comparable methods) demonstrate deep architectural thinking. Cross-architecture generalization to Qwen2.5-VL, which breaks many prior methods, further validates the approach's robustness.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Visual tokens dominate inference cost in vision-language models (VLMs) due to the quadratic scaling of self-attention with sequence length. While visual redundancy is well-documented—attention distributions are highly concentrated on a small token subset—existing pruning methods rely on heuristic proxy signals (attention magnitude, CLS-to-patch similarity, diversity scores) and frame the problem as identifying and discarding unimportant tokens. This framing misses a more fundamental question: given a fixed computational budget K, how should representational capacity be globally allocated across all visual tokens to maximize downstream task performance? Methodology: AutoSelect recasts visual token pruning as capacity-constrained representation learning. Two lightweight modules—a Scorer and a Denoiser—are inserted between the frozen vision encoder and the frozen LLM projector; all base VLM weights remain frozen. During training, the Scorer assigns per-token importance scores, which are polarized by a differentiable Soft Top-K operator (temperature-annealed on a cosine schedule from diffuse to binary). Instead of dropping low-scoring tokens (which would break gradient flow), a variance-preserving (VP) noise gate modulates each token's information throughput: high-scoring tokens pass through cleanly while low-scoring tokens are replaced with isotropic Gaussian noise scaled to preserve marginal variance (x̃ᵢ = √αᵢ xᵢ + √(1−αᵢ) εᵢ). A lightweight Denoiser with diagonal (per-token-only) attention then maps the noise-perturbed representations back into the LLM's expected input distribution—diagonal attention is critical to prevent high-importance tokens from leaking information to suppressed ones. The entire framework is trained end-to-end with only the standard next-token prediction loss, requiring no auxiliary losses or external annotations. At inference, the Denoiser and noise injection are discarded entirely; only the Scorer and hard Top-K selection remain, adding just 0.69 ms overhead. The Scorer is text-agnostic (operates on visual features only), enabling score reuse across dialogue turns. Key Results: On LLaVA-1.5-7B across ten benchmarks (GQA, MMBench, MME, POPE, ScienceQA, VQAv2, TextVQA, SEED-Bench, VizWiz), AutoSelect retains 96.5% of full-model accuracy at 88.9% pruning (64 of 576 tokens), surpassing the previous best (PRUNESID) by 1.4% at this aggressive budget. At 77.8% pruning (128 tokens), AutoSelect achieves 97.6% retention vs. PRUNESID's 96.9%. On LLaVA-NeXT-7B (2,880 tokens, 88.9% pruning to 320), AutoSelect reaches 96.1% retention, outperforming HoloV by 0.4%. On Qwen2.5-VL-7B with variable-length sequences, AutoSelect outperforms all baselines at all three pruning rates (e.g., 98.3% vs. 94.3% at 66.7% pruning). Efficiency analysis on a single A6000 GPU shows AutoSelect achieves 2.85× LLM prefill acceleration with only 0.69 ms pruning module overhead (TTFT: 72.73 ms vs. 149.51 ms for full LLaVA), while PRUNESID—despite comparable accuracy at light pruning—requires 43.39 ms for its module alone (TTFT: 115.84 ms). An LLM-free ImageNet-1K classification experiment confirms the accuracy gains originate from better token selection, not LLM adaptation: AutoSelect leads HoloV by ~10 percentage points in Top-1 accuracy at K=6 (3% of tokens). Strengths: - Theoretically principled reframing: VP noise gating provides a continuous, gradient-friendly surrogate that is empirically validated to impose information constraints equivalent to hard Top-K pruning, enabling end-to-end training without discrete operations. - Minimal overhead and architectural agnosticism: 0.69 ms inference overhead, ~84M additional parameters, no modifications to any base VLM component, and demonstrated transfer across LLaVA-1.5, LLaVA-NeXT, and the architecturally distinct Qwen2.5-VL (which lacks a CLS token, breaking many prior methods). - Strong performance under aggressive compression: The capacity-constrained formulation is especially advantageous at extreme budgets (K=64, 88.9% pruning) where heuristic methods fail to identify truly informative tokens. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The Scorer is text-agnostic by design, which enables score reuse but means it cannot adapt token selection to instruction content—text-rich or localization-sensitive queries may require different tokens than image-caption tasks. - Training requires ImageNet-1K captioning data (ImageNet-1K-VL-Enriched); while lightweight, this is not entirely annotation-free and introduces a dependency on an external calibration dataset. - Evaluation is limited to single-image benchmarks; the scalability and effectiveness of AutoSelect for multi-image or video settings—where the token count problem is most acute—is not demonstrated.

#8 — Highly Innovative (79)

Protecting Language Models Against Unauthorized Distillation through Trace Rewriting

Xinhang Ma, William Yeoh, Ning Zhang, Yevgeniy Vorobeychik · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: This paper scores 79 for its creative insight that reasoning trace rewriting can simultaneously achieve anti-distillation protection and robust watermarking while actually improving teacher model performance—resolving the core limitation of all prior anti-distillation work that degraded teacher quality. The OPRO-optimized rewriting instruction that arrives at 'esoteric, formal, densely technical lexicon' is an elegant automated discovery. The inherent attacker trade-off (filtering watermarks destroys the reasoning quality that motivated distillation) is a genuinely surprising and powerful result. The near-perfect watermark detection with zero false alarms using only 5 queries substantially outperforms all baselines.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Knowledge distillation—training a smaller student model by imitating a larger teacher model's outputs—is increasingly used to steal capabilities from proprietary frontier LLMs at a fraction of the development cost. This is especially acute for reasoning-capable models (e.g., DeepSeek-R1, o1) that produce explicit chain-of-thought traces, which provide rich supervision signals far beyond simple input-output pairs. Existing anti-distillation methods (ADS, DOGe) significantly degrade the teacher's own performance to achieve protection, and existing API watermarking schemes rely on token-level statistics leading to non-trivial false alarm rates. This paper addresses both problems simultaneously through a unified trace-rewriting framework. Methodology: The authors propose modifying teacher-generated reasoning traces online (per query, without access to the full distillation dataset) to achieve two goals: (1) anti-distillation—making traces poor training signal while preserving correct final answers and semantic coherence; and (2) API watermarking—embedding verifiable signatures into traces that survive distillation into student models. Two families of rewriting methods are developed. Instruction-based rewriting uses an auxiliary LLM (gpt-oss-120b) to transform traces via natural language prompts. Two variants are explored: semantic prompting (a simple high-level directive to make the trace unhelpful for distillation) and optimized prompting (OPT), which uses the OPRO framework with proxy student models to automatically search for the most effective rewrite instruction—arriving at an instruction that reformulates reasoning in an "esoteric, formal, and densely technical lexicon" to obfuscate clarity. Gradient-based rewriting operates directly in token embedding space, using Hessian-based (HB-Grad) or first-order (FO-Grad) approximations to maximize student training loss, followed by nearest-neighbor projection back to discrete tokens. For watermarking, the rewrite instruction embeds a trigger-target association (e.g., "x137 = 666") into 10% of traces; detection queries the suspect student model for the trigger and checks if it outputs the target. Key Results: The optimized prompting (OPT) method achieves the strongest anti-distillation effect across all evaluated student architectures (Llama-3.2-1B, Llama-3.2-3B, Qwen2.5-1.5B) and datasets (GSM8K, MATH, MMLU, MMLU-Pro). On GSM8K, OPT reduces student accuracy to below 20% (from ~30-75% clean baselines), representing up to ~61.3% accuracy reduction compared to distillation on clean traces. Crucially, OPT simultaneously improves teacher accuracy by ~3% on GSM8K and ~22% on MATH relative to clean traces—in stark contrast to ADS and DOGe which degrade teacher performance. The anti-distillation effect scales with student capability: stronger student models suffer greater degradation. Gradient-based methods are less effective than prompt-based ones in practice, likely due to the constrained number of token substitutions. For watermarking, the proposed approach achieves near-perfect true detection rate (TD ≈ 1.00) with essentially zero false alarm rate (FA ≈ 0.00) using as few as K=5 verification queries, outperforming all four baselines (He et al., GINSEW, KGW, VIA). The watermark remains robust to both regex-based filtering and paraphrase attacks—the latter actually amplifies the anti-distillation effect. Strengths: - Uniquely achieves both strong anti-distillation and reliable watermarking simultaneously, while improving (not degrading) teacher performance—resolving the core limitation of all prior anti-distillation work. - The two-stage pipeline (generate clean trace, then rewrite) is shown through ablation to be substantially more effective than directly prompting the teacher to produce anti-distillation outputs, validating the architectural choice. - Watermark robustness is rigorously tested against adaptive adversaries (regex filtering, paraphrasing), with the key insight that aggressive filtering sufficient to reduce detectability also destroys the reasoning quality that motivated distillation, creating an inherent trade-off for the attacker. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Gradient-based rewriting methods (HB-Grad, FO-Grad, RHB-Grad) are computationally expensive (requiring Hessian computation or iterative embedding updates) and empirically underperform the much simpler prompt-based approach, raising questions about their practical utility. - The optimization framework relies on proxy student models (different from actual victim students) to score rewrite instructions; while an ensemble is used to mitigate overfitting, there is an unquantified risk of poor transfer to unknown real-world student architectures. - Evaluation focuses exclusively on supervised fine-tuning (SFT)-based distillation; efficacy against other distillation paradigms (e.g., preference optimization, online distillation) is left for future work.

#9 — Highly Innovative (79)

Ensembling Language Models with Sequential Monte Carlo

Robin Shing Moon Chan, Tianyu Liu, Samuel Kiegeland, Clemente Pasti, Jacob Hoover Vigly, Timothy J. O'Donnell, Ryan Cotterell, Tim Vieira · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: This paper scores 79 for its principled unification of diverse LM ensembling strategies under the f-ensemble/generalized-means framework with clean variational justification via α-divergences. The byte-level SMC algorithm elegantly sidesteps the tokenization alignment problem that has dominated prior work, enabling combination of heterogeneous model families without ad-hoc vocabulary mapping. The empirical finding that consensus-seeking operators consistently outperform probability averaging—backed by rigorous correlation analysis showing approximation quality predicts task performance—is both surprising and actionable. The theoretical contribution of connecting ensembling operators to α-divergence minimization provides lasting conceptual value beyond the specific experiments.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Practitioners have access to many language models and prompting strategies, but model performance is highly sensitive to both model choice and prompt choice. Classical ensembling offers a principled way to aggregate predictions from multiple sources, yet applying ensembling to LMs during decoding is non-trivial: naively aggregating next-token probabilities yields samples from a locally normalized, biased approximation of the true global ensemble distribution over strings. Prior work on LM ensembling has largely focused on the vocabulary alignment problem (combining models with mismatching tokenizers) and has defaulted to local probability averaging—which produces samples biased toward the local ensemble rather than the global one. This paper asks a deeper question: beyond probability averaging, what ensembling strategies are effective, and what do we gain from consistently sampling from the *global* ensemble distribution over strings? Methodology: The authors introduce *f-ensembles*, a unified framework for composing K language models into ensemble distributions via a general aggregation function f: ℝ≥0^K → ℝ≥0. The f-ensemble Φ(x) = f(p1(x), ..., pK(x)) / Z defines a normalized distribution over strings. They show that the family of *generalized means* (parameterized by τ) arises naturally as minimizers of weighted α-divergences to the base models, recovering product-of-experts (τ→0), probability averaging/sum (τ=1), mixture-of-experts (τ→1), min, and max as special cases. To sidestep the vocabulary alignment problem, models are mapped to a shared character/byte-level space. For approximate inference from the intractable f-ensemble, the authors propose a byte-level Sequential Monte Carlo (SMC) algorithm. SMC maintains M particles (partial strings), extends them token by token from a proposal distribution, updates importance weights using a shaping function ψ̃(x'|x) ≈ f(p̃1(x'), ..., p̃K(x')), and resamples when the effective sample size falls below a threshold τ·M. The locally optimal proposal at each step is shown to be proportional to the shaping function. Experiments evaluate within-model ensembles (same model, different prompts) and cross-model ensembles (different model families) on three structured generation tasks: JSON Schema conformance (GlaiveAI), Big-Bench Hard word sorting, and SPIDER text-to-SQL. Models tested include Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct, Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct, and Phi-4 (14B). Key Results: (1) LMs can act synergistically—ensembles improve over base models when both prompts perform moderately; cross-model ensembles combining Qwen+Phi with the same prompt on BBH reach 41.1% vs. best single-model baseline. (2) Consensus-seeking f-ensembles (τ≤0: min, product) consistently outperform coverage-seeking ones (τ≥1: sum, max) and traditional local probability averaging. On JSON Schema with Llama, the best global f-ensemble (Token SMC, M=25) achieves 89.1% vs. 86.3% for the best single-prompt baseline and 83.4% for local probability averaging. On SPIDER with Qwen, the best cross-model ensemble reaches 55.5% vs. 53.1% best single prompt. (3) Approximation quality correlates positively with task accuracy for consensus-seeking ensembles (Spearman ρ≈0.235–0.309 for min/product on BBH with Llama), but weakly negatively for coverage-seeking ones—confirming that better SMC posteriors translate to measurably better task performance for consensus operators. Runtime overhead is 6-7x for Token SMC (M=10) and 60-220x for Byte SMC (M=10) relative to single-model inference. Strengths: - Principled unification of diverse ensembling strategies (product, average, min, max) under the f-ensemble/generalized-means framework with clean variational justification via α-divergences. - The byte-level SMC algorithm elegantly sidesteps the tokenization alignment problem that dominates most prior work, enabling principled combination of heterogeneous model families. - Strong empirical finding that consensus-seeking operators outperform probability averaging, with rigorous correlation analysis showing approximation quality predicts task performance for these operators. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Computational cost is substantial: Byte SMC is 60-220x slower per instance than a base model, limiting practical deployment outside high-stakes or offline settings. - Experiments are restricted to K=2 experts on structured tasks with binary correctness; generalization to K>2 models, open-ended generation, or reasoning-specialized models (e.g., chain-of-thought) is not evaluated. - The benefit of ensembling is contingent on models being complementary (both performing moderately); when one model dominates, ensembles provide little gain, limiting the general applicability.

#10 — Highly Innovative (79)

UltRAG: a Universal Simple Scalable Recipe for Knowledge Graph RAG

graphcore-research.github.io · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: UltRAG introduces a genuinely novel architectural insight: coupling LLMs with neural (not symbolic) query executors over fuzzy sets to handle both LLM noise and KG incompleteness simultaneously. The modular, training-free design that scales to Wikidata (116M entities, 1.6B relations) on a single GPU is a significant engineering and conceptual achievement. The fuzzy set formulation is theoretically principled and the 18-24% improvement of neural over symbolic execution on identical LLM queries validates the core thesis. The 19-167x speed advantage over prior KG-RAG methods while achieving state-of-the-art accuracy suggests this could become a standard recipe for KG-RAG.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Large language models (LLMs) frequently hallucinate—generating confident yet factually incorrect content. Retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) mitigates this for document-structured data, but adapting it to Knowledge Graphs (KGs) is non-trivial. KGs store information as subject-predicate-object triples distributed across millions of entities and relations, requiring multi-hop reasoning to answer many queries. Existing KG-RAG approaches either rely on symbolic query executors (brittle to both LLM-generated query noise and KG incompleteness) or offload all reasoning to the LLM (inefficient and unreliable for graph traversal). No prior framework successfully couples LLMs with neural query execution modules for KGQA without retraining either component. Methodology: UltRAG is a modular, iterative pipeline with four components: an LLM query generator, an entity linker, a neural query executor, and an arbitrator. The framework operates over fuzzy sets (membership functions in [0,1]^|V|) rather than crisp sets, enabling robustness to both LLM noise and KG incompleteness. Two key insights drive the design: (1) query executors must be neural rather than symbolic to handle real-world noise, and (2) LLMs are poor graph algorithm executors and should not be used for that role. The off-the-shelf instantiation, UltRAG-OTS, uses a custom infix DSL (reducing invalid query rates from 15-30% to <1% compared to BetaE-style tuples), ULTRAQUERY as the neural executor (a GNN-based foundation model for logical query reasoning over KGs), FAISS with IVFPQ indexing for scalable entity linking via E5-large embeddings, and a Personalized PageRank (SEPPR) algorithm to extract relevant subgraphs around seed entities. GPT-5 serves as both the LLM query generator and arbitrator. No retraining of any module is required. Key Results: On GTSQA (WikiKG2), UltRAG-OTS achieves 92.66% exact match hits, 91.05% recall, and 89.29% F1—surpassing the best baseline SubgraphRAG(200) by +8.32% hits, +9.39% recall, and +8.03% F1. On KGQAGen-10K (Wikidata-scale, 116M entities, 1.6B relations), it achieves 92.04% hits, 90.77% recall, and 88.82% F1, outperforming SubgraphRAG(200) by +2.28%, +5.25%, and +2.54% respectively. Neural query execution (ULTRAQUERY) provides a class-average improvement of ~18.58% MRR and ~24.09% Hit@10 over symbolic execution on identical LLM-generated queries. In efficiency, UltRAG-OTS is 19x/99x/167x faster than RoG/GNN-RAG/SubgraphRAG in non-API runtime on WikiKG2, with 94-96% prompt token cache hit rates reducing effective API costs. With GPT-5-mini for arbitration instead of GPT-5, performance drops only ~4% in Hits/F1 at $0.009/query vs $0.017/query. Strengths: - Universal and training-free: UltRAG requires no retraining of the LLM or neural executor, making it immediately applicable to new KGs and future model improvements - Demonstrated web-scale applicability: successfully operates on Wikidata (116M entities, 1.6B relations) on a single GH200 96GB GPU via FAISS and SEPPR subgraph extraction - Strong theoretical grounding: the fuzzy set formulation provides a principled way to handle both LLM query noise and KG incompleteness simultaneously, with clear ablation evidence for neural over symbolic execution Weaknesses / Limitations: - No support for temporal queries: UltRAG-OTS does not handle temporal knowledge graphs, which would require extending to frameworks like TFLEX - Potential underperformance on Hypergraphs: the reliance on ULTRA as the neural executor may be suboptimal for knowledge hypergraphs, as ULTRA has been shown to underperform in that setting - Input token overhead: UltRAG-OTS processes 25-62x more input tokens per query than baselines (due to relation types in the prompt and two API calls), though high cache hit rates (~94-96%) substantially mitigate the cost difference

#11 — Highly Innovative (79)

Revealing Behavioral Plasticity in Large Language Models: A Token-Conditional Perspective

Liyuan Mao, Le Yu, Jing Zhou, Chujie Zheng, Bowen Yu, Chang Gao, Shixuan Liu, An Yang, Weinan Zhang, JunYang Lin · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: This paper scores 79 for its genuinely surprising discovery that prepending just 6 tokens from an instruction-tuned model can fundamentally shift a reasoning model's behavioral mode at inference time—a finding that reframes how we think about latent capabilities in LLMs. The formalization of behavioral plasticity as a tractable RL objective via the KL-to-policy-gradient equivalence (Theorem 4.2) is theoretically elegant. Most compelling is the emergent recalibrative reasoning pattern, which was not explicitly trained for but arises naturally, representing a qualitatively new reasoning strategy distinct from both direct answering and chain-of-thought. The simultaneous improvement on both factual QA and math—a trade-off all baselines fail to navigate—demonstrates practical significance beyond theoretical novelty.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Large Reasoning Models (LRMs) excel at complex mathematics via step-by-step chain-of-thought reasoning but perform significantly worse than instruction-tuned counterparts on factual question answering—Qwen3-30B-A3B-2507-Thinking scores 18.9 on SimpleQA versus 22.1 for its Instruct version. The root cause is that extended exploratory reasoning introduces unnecessary associations and unverified information that hinder direct knowledge retrieval. Existing behavioral steering methods (SFT, RLHF, DPO) merely amplify behaviors already present in training data, while prompt engineering lacks stable control. This paper identifies an orthogonal phenomenon: LLMs possess intrinsic behavioral plasticity—latent capabilities that can be exposed at inference time without any parameter modification, and subsequently stabilized via RL. Methodology: The paper introduces two complementary contributions. First, token-conditional generation: by prepending a short prefix (k≈6 tokens) sampled from the initial tokens of an instruction-tuned model's direct answer to a reasoning model's generation context, the LRM seamlessly shifts from step-by-step reasoning to direct knowledge retrieval. This exploits the autoregressive property that initial tokens establish a behavioral trajectory the model naturally continues. Second, ToCoRL (Token-Conditional Reinforcement Learning) internalizes this transient adaptation into persistent model behavior. The RL objective is a KL-constrained REINFORCE update where the reference policy π_TC is the token-conditional induced policy (a mixture of the base policy and the token-guided policy). A key theoretical result (Theorem 4.2) shows that minimizing KL(π_TC‖π_θ) shares the same gradient as a weighted policy-gradient objective, making the approach tractable. During training, rollouts are sampled from a mixed policy π_mix that stochastically interleaves normal generation and token-conditional generation, reducing variance while ensuring the target behavior is continuously reinforced. Training uses 10K factual query-answer pairs plus 10K math queries jointly, with binary rewards from an LLM-as-a-judge (gpt-4o-2024-08-06 for SimpleQA, gemini-2.5-flash for AA-Omniscience). Key Results: Starting from Qwen3-30B-A3B-2507-Thinking, ToCoRL improves SimpleQA from 18.9 to 28.3 (+9.4) and AA-Omniscience from 16.1 to 18.6, while simultaneously preserving or improving math: AIME'24 84.8→89.0, AIME'25 80.5→81.5. All baselines fail to achieve this dual improvement: GRPO reaches 23.6/16.7 on SimpleQA/Omniscience but maintains math (89.5/81.4); Adaptive-Thinking reaches 23.9/13.8 with math degradation (87.8/78.9); Instruct-Tuning GRPO reaches 21.1/14.8 (87.8/80.4). The emergent behavior—dubbed recalibrative reasoning—has the model begin with a direct answer attempt then iteratively enumerate candidate answers using minimal focused context until reaching confidence, distinct from both pure direct answering and associative step-by-step reasoning. Training dynamics show average response length rapidly decreases initially (elimination of spurious reasoning), then minimum length stays low while mean increases as the model learns to extend reasoning only when the first attempt fails. Ablations confirm robustness: performance varies by less than 1 point across λ∈{0.5,1,2} and k∈{6,7,8}, and the emergent behavior is nearly identical across three different prefix providers (235A22B-Instruct, 30A3B-Instruct, 2.5-14B). Starting from the base model (Qwen3-30A3B-Base with SFT initialization), ToCoRL still achieves 28.1 on SimpleQA versus 22.6/24.8/21.7 for baselines. The discovered behavior transfers via SFT: using ToCoRL-generated responses as SFT data yields 29.1 on SimpleQA, outperforming SFT data from the instruct models (28.3 and 25.3). Strengths: - Elegant conceptual framing: formalizes behavioral plasticity as a latent property exposable via token-conditional generation, supported by rigorous theoretical proofs connecting KL divergence to policy gradient objectives - Achieves simultaneous improvement on both factual retrieval and complex math—a capability trade-off all competing baselines fail to navigate - Strong transferability: the discovered behavioral pattern can be distilled into SFT data, enabling other models to acquire the behavior without additional RL training - Robust ablations across hyperparameters, model sizes, and training starting points (Thinking model and base model) Weaknesses / Limitations: - Experimental scope is narrow: the paper explicitly focuses on a single test case (factual QA + math reasoning on Qwen3 series) and acknowledges it does not evaluate ToCoRL on broader task types - The recalibrative reasoning pattern can produce extremely long reasoning chains for hard factual problems (the enumeration process can be extensive), which may be computationally prohibitive at inference time for deployed systems - Reward signal depends on LLM-as-a-judge quality; the paper notes less capable judge models can misclassify long incorrect responses as correct, introducing reward hacking risk
Recommended 2603.08398 PDF
#12 — Highly Innovative (78)

Optimal Expert-Attention Allocation in Mixture-of-Experts: A Scalable Law for Dynamic Model Design

Junzhuo Li, Peijie Jiang, Changxin Tian, Jia Liu, Zhiqiang Zhang, Xuming Hu · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: This paper scores 78 because it identifies and rigorously characterizes a previously unmodeled first-order design variable — the expert-attention FLOPs ratio — in MoE architectures, filling a genuine gap in scaling law research. The controlled experimental sweep cleanly isolates this internal allocation effect across scales and sparsity regimes, yielding closed-form power-law guidance with demonstrated out-of-sample generalization to held-out sparsity levels. The practical implication is significant: MoE practitioners have been inheriting this ratio from dense designs without principled justification, and this work provides the missing theoretical and empirical foundation. The limitation to autoregressive LM and fixed routing patterns tempers the impact somewhat.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) architectures allow large language models to scale parameter count without proportionally increasing per-token computation. However, a critical design question has been largely ignored: how should compute be distributed *internally* between attention sublayers and expert (feed-forward) sublayers? In practice, this expert-attention FLOPs ratio is inherited from dense Transformer designs or tuned heuristically, despite expert layers often dominating compute budgets at scale. Existing scaling laws (Kaplan et al., Chinchilla) prescribe optimal balances between parameters and training tokens but implicitly assume a fixed internal allocation, leaving the expert–attention trade-off unmodeled. This paper addresses that gap. Methodology: The authors define r = C_E / C_A as the FLOPs ratio of expert compute to attention compute, and sparsity S = (E - E_act) / E as the fraction of inactive experts per token. They conduct controlled sweeps over r across multiple model scales (100M–5B total parameters, up to 1×10²¹ training FLOPs) and four sparsity levels (S ∈ {82.35%, 90.91%, 95.38%, 97.67%}, corresponding to E ∈ {17, 33, 65, 129} total experts), using GPT-style decoder-only MoE Transformers trained on a multilingual/multimodal dataset. By fixing per-token compute budget C_token = (C_A + C_E) / n_token and varying r in [0.2, 1.5], they isolate the internal allocation effect from confounding factors. The empirical optimal r* is extracted from loss minima, then fitted to a power-law r* = α_r · C^{β_r}, where the coefficients themselves follow power-law relationships with sparsity: α_r = 6.7×10⁻⁵(1−S)^{−1.23} and β_r = 0.24(1−S)^{0.21}. This allocation law is then incorporated into an extended Chinchilla-style loss model: L = a/N^α + b/D^β + c·e^R(1−S)^γ/N^λ + d·r/(r+1) + τ, fitted via L-BFGS with 9 parameters. Key Results: The optimal FLOPs ratio r* is empirically stable and well-defined (a clear valley in the loss surface, not noise), increases monotonically with total compute across all sparsity regimes, and is strongly modulated by sparsity. Specifically, low-sparsity models (more experts activated per token) favor higher r* at scale, while high-sparsity models grow r* more gradually. The fitted coefficients for the extended scaling law (Table 1: α=0.6288, β=0.0453, λ=0.4228, γ=0.0431, τ=13.74, a=15.12, b=18.62, c=39.55, d=0.0499) achieve strong agreement on held-out data, including a held-out sparsity level (S=97.67%) excluded entirely from fitting—demonstrating out-of-sample generalization. Compared to prior MoE scaling formulations (Wang et al. 2024, Abnar et al. 2025), the proposed law better captures loss trends across both small and large models and across high-sparsity regimes where prior methods fail. Strengths: - Identifies and rigorously characterizes a previously unmodeled first-order scaling variable (expert-attention FLOPs ratio) with practical, closed-form guidance for MoE design - Controlled experimental design (fixed per-token compute, sweep over r) cleanly isolates the internal allocation effect across a broad range of scales and sparsity regimes - Extended scaling law generalizes out-of-sample to held-out sparsity levels, validating the formulation's predictive power beyond the training distribution Weaknesses / Limitations: - Analysis is restricted to autoregressive language modeling; generalization to multimodal tasks, non-causal architectures, or adaptive (learned) routing is unvalidated - Hardware-level constraints such as communication costs in distributed training and memory bandwidth effects are not accounted for, which can substantially alter the practical optimal r in real deployments - Experiments use a fixed activation pattern (3 experts per token: 1 shared + 2 routed) and fixed context length (4096); the sensitivity of the discovered scaling laws to these architectural choices is unexplored
Recommended 2603.10379 PDF
#13 — Highly Innovative (78)

Coden: Efficient Temporal Graph Neural Networks for Continuous Prediction

Zulun Zhu, Siqiang Luo · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: CODEN scores 78 for its creative integration of State Space Models into temporal GNNs with rigorous theoretical backing. The RNN-Attention duality proof provides genuine insight into why the architecture works, going beyond empirical justification. The lazy-sampling strategy with closed-form approximation bounds is an elegant mechanism for controlling the accuracy-efficiency tradeoff via a single interpretable parameter. Scaling to billion-edge graphs where all attention- and RNN-based competitors fail with OOM errors demonstrates real practical impact, and the 44.8x speedup over state-of-the-art while maintaining accuracy is a compelling result.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Existing Temporal Graph Neural Networks (TGNNs) are designed primarily for one-time predictions over a fixed temporal span, but real-world applications (e-commerce, social platforms, recommendation systems) require continuous predictions as graph updates arrive frequently. Directly adapting existing TGNNs to continuous prediction is problematic: single-snapshot methods ignore historical context; RNN-based methods suffer from limited memory horizons and O(Km^(t)F) embedding recomputation costs per update; and attention-based methods incur O(T(n^(t))²F) quadratic time complexity as history grows. No existing TGNN achieves both accuracy and efficiency simultaneously in continuously evolving graph environments. Methodology: CODEN introduces a State Space Model (SSM)-based TGNN framework built around two core mechanisms. First, it uses parameter-free Personalized PageRank (PPR)-based node embeddings updated incrementally via a Forward Push algorithm, avoiding costly full GCN retraining on each graph change. A lazy-sampling strategy discretizes the continuous update stream by skipping intermediate embeddings until accumulated drift exceeds a threshold λ, with a theoretical bound (Lemma 1) guaranteeing the approximation error remains within n^(t)ε. Second, the sampled embeddings are fed into a linear SSM (Mamba-style) that updates node states as M^(t+1) = Ā·M^(t) + B̄·H^(t+1), compressing historical temporal information into a fixed-size state matrix. The paper also proves an RNN–Attention duality: under constrained parameters CODEN reduces to a gated-RNN update, while in the general case its unrolled dynamics are mathematically equivalent to masked kernel attention, providing theoretical justification for why CODEN achieves both efficiency and expressiveness. An ablation variant CODEN-A replaces SSM with explicit attention for direct comparison. Key Results: Evaluated on five dynamic graph datasets (DBLP, Tmall, Reddit, Patent, Papers100M with up to 111M nodes and 1.6B edges), CODEN achieves up to 44.80× speedup in total training time vs. state-of-the-art methods while maintaining comparable or superior Micro-F1 accuracy. On Papers100M, CODEN completes continuous prediction in 3.3 hours vs. 98.3 hours for TGL. Most RNN- and attention-based baselines run out of memory on the three large-scale datasets (Reddit, Patent, Papers100M) with a 24GB GPU, while CODEN handles all five. On accuracy, CODEN achieves average Micro-F1 of 76.35 (DBLP), 65.14 (Tmall), 92.61 (Reddit), 83.74 (Patent) — matching or surpassing DyGFormer, ASTGCN, MPNN, and other strong baselines. Training time per epoch on Patent scales from 1.71K to only 1.86K seconds as graph grows, versus 2.67K–44.80K× overhead for baselines. Dirichlet Energy analysis confirms CODEN produces less over-smoothed representations than CODEN-A. Strengths: - Principled theoretical foundation: formal complexity bounds, accuracy guarantees on incremental embeddings, and the RNN–Attention duality proof provide rigorous justification for design choices rather than relying purely on empirical results. - Scalability to billion-scale graphs where all comparable attention- and RNN-based TGNNs fail with OOM errors, enabled by parameter-free PPR embeddings and SSM's linear time complexity. - The lazy-sampling strategy elegantly controls the accuracy–efficiency tradeoff via a single interpretable threshold λ, with a closed-form bound on information loss. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The lazy-sampling threshold λ requires dataset-specific tuning; too large a λ degrades accuracy substantially (e.g., on DBLP accuracy drops sharply above λ=0.05), adding a hyperparameter sensitivity concern for practitioners. - The paper assumes static node attributes (x^(t+p) = x^(t)) as the primary setting, with attribute changes handled only as an appendix extension, which may limit applicability to graphs with rapidly evolving node features. - Evaluation is restricted to temporal node classification; generalization to link prediction or other downstream tasks on continuous-time dynamic graphs is not demonstrated.

#14 — Highly Innovative (78)

TT-Sparse: Learning Sparse Rule Models with Differentiable Truth Tables

Hans Farrell Soegeng, Sarthak Ketanbhai Modi, Thomas Peyrin · arxiv.org · neural symbolic - new results
Why this rank: TT-Sparse scores 78 for its creative Soft TopK operator that enforces exact cardinality constraints with closed-form gradients, combined with an exact rule extraction pipeline using Quine-McCluskey minimization — a genuinely novel differentiable bridge between neural training and provably minimal Boolean logic. The architecture achieves remarkable Pareto dominance over 9 interpretable baselines across 28 datasets while maintaining dramatically lower rule complexity (e.g., complexity 15 vs. 100–1000+ at comparable AUC). The seamless extension to binary, multiclass, and regression tasks with a unified framework, and the concrete demonstration that interpretable models can rival deep tabular baselines like TabM, represent a significant practical and conceptual advance in the interpretable ML space.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: High-stakes domains such as healthcare, finance, and criminal justice demand machine learning models that are both accurate and globally interpretable—meaning a single, human-readable set of rules governs all predictions. Existing rule-learning approaches face a fundamental tension: classical methods (decision trees, rule induction) use greedy heuristics that yield suboptimal rules, while neuro-symbolic relaxations either impose restrictive logical forms or require exponentially growing parameterizations. Human cognitive load studies indicate rule sets exceeding complexity 50 become functionally unintelligible, yet most interpretable models either sacrifice performance to stay simple or balloon in complexity to compete with black-box baselines. This paper addresses the specific regime of global and exact interpretability, where model inference reduces precisely to symbolic Boolean logic without approximation. Methodology: TT-Sparse introduces Learnable Truth Table (LTT) nodes as a differentiable neural building block. Each LTT node selects a sparse subset of k input features via a novel Soft TopK operator, then learns a Boolean function over those features parameterized as a thresholded linear combination (n+1 parameters per node, compared to the double-exponential complexity of DiffLogicNet or exponential complexity of DWN). The Soft TopK operator provides a continuous relaxation via entropic regularization: it finds a probability vector in [0,1]^n that maximizes the dot product with the logit scores while summing to exactly k, solved efficiently via bisection on the Lagrangian multiplier (converging to float32 precision in ~25 iterations, ~1ms per batch of 16). A straight-through estimator bridges forward (hard TopK for sparsity) and backward (soft TopK for gradient flow) passes. After training, each node's truth table is enumerated (2^k entries), Don't-Care Terms are identified from unseen input combinations, and Quine-McCluskey minimization converts the truth table to a compact CNF/DNF formula. An iterative magnitude-based pruning phase further eliminates unnecessary connections. The full model concatenates LTT outputs with raw features and feeds them to a linear classifier/regressor, supporting binary, multiclass, and regression tasks. Key Results: Evaluated across 28 tabular datasets (14 binary, 7 multiclass, 7 regression), TT-Sparse achieves average rank 1.53 on binary classification AUC among 9 interpretable models, substantially ahead of the next-best GLRM (3.60) and RuleFit (3.07). On multiclass tasks, TT-Sparse ranks 1st with average rank 1.0, dominating all competitors. On regression (R²), TT-Sparse ranks 2nd (1.71) behind only RuleFit (1.43). Crucially, TT-Sparse achieves these results with dramatically lower rule complexity: on the Heart dataset it achieves 91% AUC at complexity 15, while competitors achieving comparable AUC require complexity 100–1000+. On 11 of 14 binary datasets, TT-Sparse Pareto-dominates all other interpretable models. It also remains competitive with or occasionally surpasses TabM, the SOTA non-pretrained deep tabular model (e.g., Heart dataset). An ablation replacing Soft TopK with a softmax-based argmax baseline confirms consistent superiority of the proposed operator across all datasets. Strengths: - The Soft TopK operator is a genuine technical contribution: it enforces exact cardinality constraints in expectation with closed-form gradients via implicit differentiation, is efficient (bisection in ~25 iterations), and avoids the slot-redundancy and parameter-inefficiency problems of softmax-based argmax alternatives. - Rule extraction pipeline is exact (not approximate): enumerating 2^k truth table entries and applying Quine-McCluskey minimization with Don't-Care Terms yields provably minimal CNF/DNF formulas that faithfully reproduce training behavior. - The architecture is task-agnostic and seamlessly extends to binary, multiclass, and regression settings, with multiclass complexity counted globally (a shared rule contributes once rather than once per class). Weaknesses / Limitations: - Continuous features must be discretized before training, which fixes the literal representation and introduces a hyperparameter (number of encoding bits); though ablations show robustness to as few as 5 bits, the discretization choice affects expressiveness. - Training stability depends on the Soft TopK temperature parameter τ, which must be appropriately initialized; poor initialization can slow convergence, and the method inherits sensitivity to this hyperparameter shared by all temperature-based relaxations. - On a few binary datasets (blood, compas, covertype, jungle, road_safety) and regression tasks, TT-Sparse does not consistently dominate, particularly on high-complexity or high-dimensional datasets where the expressiveness of individual LTT nodes (bounded by 2^k truth table entries) may be insufficient without many nodes.
