#15 — Highly Innovative (78)

NLE: Non-autoregressive LLM -based ASR by Transcript Editing

Avihu Dekel, Samuel Thomas, Takashi Fukada, George Saon · arxiv.org · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: NLE scores 78 for its creative reformulation of LLM-based ASR as parallel transcript editing, exploiting the identity mapping bias of Transformers through an elegant interleaved blank-slot design. Achieving Pareto-optimal WER-RTFx on the Open ASR Leaderboard with a 27× single-utterance speedup over autoregressive baselines at near-identical accuracy is a striking result. The approach is principled and parameter-efficient (14M trainable params), offering a genuinely new decoding paradigm for LLM-ASR that sidesteps the sequential bottleneck. The main limitation — degradation under iterative refinement and on multilingual data — prevents a higher score, but the core contribution is both novel and practically impactful.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Autoregressive (AR) LLM-based ASR systems achieve strong transcription accuracy but suffer from inherently sequential decoding that limits parallelism and incurs high latency — a critical bottleneck in real-time conversational settings where batch processing is infeasible. Additionally, these systems discard the initial CTC hypothesis produced by the speech encoder, despite it frequently providing a reasonable draft amenable to refinement rather than full regeneration. Non-autoregressive (NAR) alternatives like CTC are fast but lack strong language modeling capabilities and struggle with insertion errors and long-range linguistic consistency. Methodology: NLE (Non-autoregressive LLM-based Editing ASR) reframes speech recognition as conditional transcript editing. A frozen 440M-parameter CTC Conformer encoder processes audio to produce both frame-level acoustic embeddings and an initial character-level hypothesis via greedy decoding. The hypothesis is re-tokenized using the LLM's subword tokenizer, then interleaved with explicit blank insertion slots: x̃ = (ε, x₁, ε, x₂, ..., ε, xN, ε), creating N+1 positions where insertions can occur locally without shifting the entire sequence. This interleaved layout exploits the identity mapping bias of Transformers (residual connections + tied embeddings), allowing the model to default to copying correct tokens and focus learning capacity on errors. A Q-Former projector (5× downsampling) maps acoustic embeddings into the LLM's embedding space; these are concatenated with the embedded interleaved hypothesis and fed to a Granite 4.0 1B base LLM whose causal attention mask is removed to enable bidirectional attention. LoRA adapters (rank 128, applied to attention and MLP layers, only 14M trainable parameters) adapt the model. Training uses a CTC-style latent alignment objective over the interleaved sequence plus an auxiliary copying regularization loss (λ=0.02) that encourages identity-mapping at each position. Inference is a single parallel forward pass with CTC greedy decoding. An enhanced variant NLE++ uses a larger projector, LoRA rank 160, 5 training epochs, and larger batch size (280M trainable parameters total). Key Results: On the Open ASR Leaderboard (39 models, as of Feb 2026), NLE achieves 5.79% average WER at RTFx 1722 and NLE++ achieves 5.67% WER at RTFx 1630 — both on the Pareto frontier (no other model achieves both lower WER and higher RTFx). In a controlled comparison using identical encoder, projector, LLM, and data: NLE (6.54% avg WER) substantially outperforms the CTC-only baseline (7.40%) and nearly matches the AR baseline (6.48%), while achieving 4× speedup in batched inference (RTFx 1722 vs. 430) and 27× speedup in single-utterance settings (RTFx 322 vs. 12). NLE outperforms CTC on 17/19 test sets. NLE slightly underperforms the AR baseline on CommonVoice multilingual subsets (5.79% vs. 5.18%), attributed to weaker non-English CTC hypotheses and an English-centric BPE tokenizer. Multi-step editing provides negligible gains (6.54% → 6.53% at step 2) at significant speed cost, and degrades at step 3 (6.59%) due to distribution mismatch. Error analysis shows AR models hallucinate more (higher insertion rate), while NLE adopts a conservative strategy with higher deletion and lower insertion rates. Strengths: - Novel and principled formulation of ASR as transcript editing, enabling fully parallel LLM-based decoding with a clean theoretical justification (identity mapping bias + interleaved insertion slots) - Achieves Pareto-optimal WER-RTFx tradeoff on the Open ASR Leaderboard, with 27× single-utterance speedup over the AR baseline at nearly identical accuracy - Highly parameter-efficient adaptation: only 14M trainable parameters (LoRA + projector) on top of a 1B LLM, with LoRA reversible to restore original causal LLM for downstream tasks like QA Weaknesses / Limitations: - Underperforms the AR baseline on multilingual CommonVoice subsets, likely due to English-centric CTC encoder training data and BPE vocabulary mismatch for non-English languages - Multi-step iterative refinement degrades at 3+ steps due to training/inference distribution mismatch (model trained on CTC hypotheses, but sees its own outputs at test time), limiting iterative improvement potential - Less flexible than AR models for tasks requiring outputs that substantially diverge from the input hypothesis (e.g., spoken QA); also requires a CPU-GPU round trip for retokenization when CTC and LLM use different tokenizers

#16 — Innovative (74)

Deep Dense Exploration for LLM Reinforcement Learning via Pivot-Driven Resampling

Yiran Guo, Zhongjian Qiao, Yingqi Xie, Jie Liu, Dan Ye, Ruiqing Zhang, Shuang Qiu, Lijie Xu · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: Deep Dense Exploration offers a well-motivated solution to the shallow exploration problem in LLM RL by combining utility-guided pivot selection with dense local resampling. The dual-stream optimization with gradient masking is a principled fix for known instabilities in tree-based methods, and the self-regulating compute property is elegant. However, the gains over strong baselines are modest (1-3% on most benchmarks), and the core idea of branching from intermediate states in search trees is well-established in planning literature.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Effective exploration in reinforcement learning for LLMs is fundamentally constrained by a fixed sampling budget. Standard GRPO-style methods sample exclusively from the root, causing a 'rich-get-richer' phenomenon where high-probability trajectories monopolize compute while deep, low-probability (but potentially correct) states remain statistically inaccessible. Tree-based branching methods attempt to address this by initiating rollouts from intermediate states, but disperse the limited budget across many branching points—resulting in only 2 samples per pivot, which is insufficient to compute stable local advantage estimates and causes training instability via prefix duplication bias. The core gap is the lack of a principled strategy to concentrate exploration on states that are both deep (complementary to root sampling) and recoverable (likely to yield correct completions). Methodology: The paper proposes Deep Dense Exploration (DDE), instantiated as DEEP-GRPO, built around three innovations. First, utility-guided pivot selection: each failed trajectory is segmented into T candidate branching points, and a pivot is sampled from a distribution Q(t) ∝ P_φ(success | s_{<t}) · (t/T)^γ, where P_φ is an online logistic regression model trained on a replay buffer to estimate recoverability from position alone, and (t/T)^γ is a depth bias term (γ=2 by default). This balances finding recoverable states against prioritizing deep, under-explored ones. Second, local dense resampling: K=8 auxiliary completions are generated from each selected pivot, providing enough samples to compute stable local advantage estimates without sparse-branch instability. Third, dual-stream optimization: main chain loss (global GRPO objective over root-sampled trajectories) and auxiliary chain loss (local GRPO over pivot branches, with gradient masking on the shared prefix) are optimized jointly as J(θ) = main_loss + λ·aux_loss with λ=1.0. Gradient masking prevents the prefix-duplication problem that destabilizes prior tree-based methods, and decoupled advantage normalization (local vs. global) prevents baseline bias from mixing heterogeneous trajectory distributions. Key Results: On GSM8K with Qwen2.5-0.5B-Instruct, DEEP-GRPO achieves 67.7% accuracy, outperforming GRPO (n=64) at 66.2% and AttnRL at 67.0%, despite using an equivalent total sampling budget (1 pivot × 8 branches = 8 rollouts vs. GRPO n=8). Scaling GRPO from n=8 to n=64 yields diminishing returns (64.1% → 66.2%), while DEEP-GRPO converts the same compute into meaningful gains. On five challenging benchmarks (AIME24, AMC, MATH500, Minerva, OlympiadBench) at 1.5B scale, DEEP-GRPO-1.5B achieves an average of 43.3% vs. 42.1% for Oat-Zero-1.5B (Dr.GRPO). At 7B scale, DEEP-GRPO-7B reaches 54.0% average vs. 51.4% for Oat-Zero-7B, with the largest gains on AIME24 (46.7% vs. 43.3%). Training dynamics show sustained higher policy entropy and longer response lengths compared to GRPO, indicating prevention of premature convergence and emergence of self-correction behavior. Strengths: - The utility function elegantly unifies multiple exploration strategies (root-only, uniform, linear, squared depth bias) under a single parameterization, with clear ablations validating each component's contribution. - Dual-stream optimization with gradient masking directly addresses the two root causes of tree-based method instability (prefix duplication and biased baselines), backed by both theoretical argument and empirical evidence of training collapse in baselines. - Budget allocation is adaptive: as the policy improves and fewer trajectories fail, the auxiliary sampling overhead naturally decreases, making the method computationally self-regulating. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The two-stage sampling process (root rollout → pivot selection → re-initiation of generation) introduces sequential dependencies that reduce training throughput compared to fully parallelizable root sampling in GRPO. - A substantial fraction of selected pivot states remain 'unrecoverable'—dense resampling fails to find any correct completion—indicating that rejection sampling alone cannot rescue certain deep error states, and no solution beyond future work (SFT on hard states) is offered.
Recommended 2602.14169 PDF
#17 — Innovative (74)

Targeted Bit-Flip Attacks on LLM-Based Agents

Jialai Wang, Ya Wen, Zhongmou Liu, Yuxiao Wu, Bingyi He, Zongpeng Li, Ee-Chien Chang · arxiv.org · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: Flip-Agent earns 74 for being the first to formalize targeted bit-flip attacks against multi-stage LLM agent pipelines, identifying two genuinely novel attack surfaces (final output steering and invocation manipulation) that do not exist in single-step models. The attention-enhancement term addressing trigger dilution in long agent contexts is a creative technical contribution. However, the white-box threat model with precise bit-flip capabilities is a strong assumption that limits near-term practical relevance.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Targeted bit-flip attacks (BFAs) exploit hardware fault-injection techniques (e.g., RowHammer) to flip bits in DRAM, altering model parameters to produce attacker-desired outputs on trigger-containing inputs while preserving normal behavior otherwise. All prior targeted BFA work focuses on single-step inference models such as image classifiers, leaving LLM-based agents entirely unexplored. LLM-based agents differ fundamentally: they operate through multi-stage pipelines, invoke external tools, and process intermediate environment feedback before producing a final output. This multi-stage structure both breaks the end-to-end differentiability assumed by prior gradient-based bit selection methods and introduces new attack surfaces—targeting intermediate stages and tool invocations—that simply do not exist in single-step models. Methodology: The paper introduces Flip-Agent, the first targeted BFA framework for LLM-based agents. It formalizes two attack surfaces: (I) Final Output Steering, where bit flips cause the agent to produce an attacker-chosen output whenever a trigger appears in the user prompt (prompt-level) or in an intermediate-stage input (internal-trigger); and (II) Invocation Manipulation, where bit flips redirect tool/API calls to an attacker-desired service while preserving the final output. Both surfaces are unified under a single stage-level objective (Eq. 2) combining a cross-entropy term to promote the target token sequence under triggered inputs and an L2 regularization term to preserve clean-input behavior. Two extensions are added: an attention-enhancement term (Eq. 3) that amplifies attention weights from trigger positions to target output positions, counteracting trigger dilution in long contexts; and a teacher-forcing term (Eq. 4) that enforces structural format consistency in the manipulated output. To efficiently identify which bits to flip within a hardware-constrained budget n_max=50, Flip-Agent uses a Prioritized-Search strategy: parameters are ranked by gradient magnitude, split into high- and low-influence groups via a threshold derived from the gradient distribution (median + β·(max−median)), and bits are selected greedily by evaluating the objective reduction from each candidate flip, focusing on the high-influence group first. Key Results: Flip-Agent is evaluated on six LLMs (Llama-3.2-3B, AgentLM-7B, AgentLM-13B, Llama-3.1-8B, Qwen3-4B, DeepSeek-R1-Distill-Llama-8B) against three SOTA baselines (TBT, TrojViT, Flip-S) adapted to the agent setting. For the prompt-level attack on WebShop, Flip-Agent achieves ASR of 92.6%–99.2% with CDA of 90%–100%, versus the best baseline per model of 61.1%–88.9% ASR. For the internal-trigger attack, Flip-Agent achieves 22.2%–94.4% ASR compared to baselines often below 30%. For the invocation attack on ToolBench, Flip-Agent achieves 67.3%–100% ASR with 90%–98% CDA, versus baselines ranging from 0% to 100% (with most under 65%). Flip-Agent reaches near-saturation with ~40 bit flips, while baselines still underperform even at 100 flips. Ablation confirms both the attention-enhancement term and Prioritized-Search are critical: removing either causes ASR to drop by 10–89 percentage points across models. A best-case defense that perfectly blocks the top 100 critical bits reduces ASR by only ~5–8%, with ASR remaining above 90% across all models. Strengths: - First principled formalization of two novel attack surfaces specific to multi-stage LLM agent pipelines, going beyond what prior single-step BFA work considers. - The unified objective with attention-enhancement and teacher-forcing extensions directly addresses the non-differentiability and trigger-dilution problems inherent to agent settings, yielding large margins over adapted baselines. - Comprehensive evaluation across six diverse LLMs, two benchmarks (WebShop and ToolBench), and three attack variants with ablations and a defense analysis, providing strong empirical grounding. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The threat model assumes white-box access (full knowledge of model architecture, parameters, and workflow) and the ability to precisely locate and flip selected bits in memory—a strong assumption that may not reflect realistic attacker capabilities for deployed cloud-hosted agents. - The defense analysis is superficial: the paper only evaluates a highly idealized defender with perfect knowledge of the attack's critical bits, and does not engage with or propose practical mitigations tailored to agent architectures. - The study is limited to single-agent systems, and multi-agent settings—where the attack surface may be further expanded—are explicitly deferred to future work.
Citations 2603.10042 PDF
#18 — Innovative (74)

DenoiseFlow: Uncertainty-Aware Denoising for Reliable LLM Agentic Workflows

Yandong Yan, Junwei Peng, Shijie Li, Chenxi Li, Yifei Shang, Can Deng, Ruiting Dai, Yongqiang Zhao, Jiaqi Zhu, Yu Huang · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: DenoiseFlow introduces a genuinely novel Noisy MDP formalization of accumulated semantic ambiguity in agentic workflows, providing principled theoretical grounding for adaptive resource allocation. The influence-based root-cause localization mechanism—approximating backpropagation over symbolic dependency graphs—is a creative departure from existing retry-or-restart correction paradigms. The label-free online calibration via temperature scaling and the adaptive branching that reduces compute by 40-56% over fixed strategies demonstrate strong practical innovation.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: LLM-based agentic workflows decompose complex tasks into multi-step reasoning chains, but reliability degrades as sequence length grows. The core failure mode, termed *accumulated semantic ambiguity*, occurs when minor interpretation errors in natural language instructions compound silently across steps—logical soft errors that degrade quality without triggering explicit crashes. Existing mitigations are inadequate: static exploration budgets (Tree-of-Thoughts) waste compute uniformly, reactive error recovery (Reflexion) regenerates entire plans rather than targeting root causes, and single-path execution ignores uncertainty entirely. No prior system simultaneously senses uncertainty at runtime, adapts branching to per-problem difficulty, traces failures to their upstream source, and recalibrates without ground-truth labels. Methodology: The paper formalizes multi-step agentic execution as a *Noisy MDP* where semantic noise perturbs state transitions, causing trajectories to drift from task specifications. Accumulated Semantic Divergence Δ_t propagates through a probabilistic dependency graph capturing step interdependencies via effective coupling coefficients (structural stability × semantic similarity). DenoiseFlow suppresses Δ_t through three coordinated stages. Stage 1 (Sensing) estimates per-step semantic uncertainty via Monte Carlo sampling (N=5 LLM calls at temperature=0.7), semantic clustering using MiniLM-L6-v2 embeddings with cosine similarity threshold τ_sim=0.85, and normalized entropy over cluster distributions. This local uncertainty is propagated through the dependency graph via a dual-channel recurrence: a bottleneck channel (λ=0.5) capturing critical-path risk from a single high-uncertainty ancestor, and an aggregation channel (β=0.3) capturing cumulative degradation from many mildly noisy predecessors. A temperature scaling mechanism (online self-calibration) corrects over/underconfidence by adjusting T based on verifier pass rates every Δ=20 observations without ground-truth labels. Stage 2 (Regulating) routes each step to one of three execution modes based on confidence c_t = 1 − r_t: Direct (c_t > θ_high, single greedy path), Branch (θ_low ≤ c_t ≤ θ_high, K_t parallel paths with consensus-based selection scoring validity + cohesion + log cluster size), or Refine (c_t < θ_low, escalate to Stage 3). Thresholds are set adaptively from the running confidence distribution (Q3 and Q1 − 0.5·IQR). Branch count scales with uncertainty: K_t = min(⌈κ(1−c_t)⌉, K_max=7). Stage 3 (Correcting) performs influence-based root-cause localization on the dependency graph—approximating backpropagation over symbolic structures to identify the upstream node k* with maximum transmission influence on the failure set. Asymmetric calibration boosts uncertainty at k* to 1.0 and enforces a minimum at downstream failure steps, steering subsequent execution into Branch mode at the true error source. The system rolls back to state s_{k*} and re-executes, capped at R=2 refinement retries. Key Results: Evaluated on six benchmarks (GSM8K, MATH, MBPP, HumanEval, HotpotQA, DROP) using GPT-4o-mini as the backbone, DenoiseFlow achieves 83.3% average accuracy—+1.3% over the strongest baseline (JudgeFlow at 82.0%) and +5.2% over AFlow (78.1%). Per-benchmark: GSM8K 93.9%, MATH 61.4%, MBPP 84.9%, HumanEval 93.9%, HotpotQA 77.5%, DROP 87.9%. The gains are most pronounced on MATH (+2.9% over JudgeFlow) and DROP (+1.8 F1). Cross-model generalization experiments confirm consistent improvements: on GPT-4o, DenoiseFlow reaches 99.5% GSM8K and 68.5% MATH (vs. CoT baselines of 98.0% and 62.0%); on DeepSeek-V2.5, gains are +6.8% and +12.5% respectively. Critically, adaptive branching reduces computational cost by 40–56% compared to fixed-K branching strategies (average cost $0.194 on GSM8K vs. $0.32 for fixed K=5; $1.012 on MATH vs. $1.69 for fixed K=7). Ablation studies show Adaptive Branching is the most critical component (−3.87% avg without it), followed by Online Calibration (−2.20% avg), Semantic Anchoring (−1.79% avg), and Closed-Loop Refinement (minimal accuracy impact but +13% cost without it). Uncertainty estimates are rank-consistent with actual task success (Spearman ρ = −0.782 on GSM8K/MATH), validating the routing logic. Root-cause tracing outperforms full restart by +2.64 F1 and local retry by +3.11 F1 on HotpotQA while reducing cost 13% relative to full restart. Strengths: - The Noisy MDP formulation provides principled theoretical grounding, with a formal propagation bound showing early-step errors have exponentially higher impact—directly motivating the adaptive resource allocation strategy. - Online self-calibration via temperature scaling achieves label-free domain adaptation, eliminating the need for held-out calibration sets that constrain most prior confidence methods. - Influence-based root-cause localization is a genuinely novel mechanism for agentic correction: it preserves valid intermediate computation rather than discarding entire trajectories, achieving 33–44% recovery rates when triggered. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Experiments rely primarily on GPT-4o-mini with API access; fully open-source models (Llama, Mistral) are untested, and different models may require recalibrating uncertainty thresholds from scratch. - The online calibration mechanism requires approximately 20 warm-up observations before temperature stabilizes, making uncertainty estimates unreliable early in deployment or when switching between task types, since temperature does not transfer across domains. - Stage 1 Monte Carlo sampling introduces overhead for simple high-confidence problems (MBPP routes 40.7% Direct, but still pays the N=5 sampling cost); a lightweight meta-predictor to skip uncertainty estimation for obviously easy problems could improve efficiency further.
Recommended 2603.00532 PDF
#19 — Innovative (73)

Speculative Decoding Scaling Laws (SDSL): Throughput Optimization Made Simple

Amirhossein Bozorgkhoo, Igor Molybog · arxiv.org · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: SDSL provides the first closed-form analytical relationship between pre-training scaling laws and speculative decoding throughput, yielding a remarkably simple sizing rule (N* ≈ M/370) validated across five model families. The theoretical contribution of connecting pre-training hyperparameters directly to inference-time draft model selection eliminates costly empirical search. However, the affine acceptance rate model achieves only R²=0.60 in pooled regression, and the framework's assumptions about comparable training distributions limit applicability to aligned or domain-specialized models.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Speculative decoding accelerates LLM inference by using a small draft model to propose candidate tokens that a larger target model then verifies in parallel, yielding significant throughput gains without sacrificing output quality. However, the technique's effectiveness is highly sensitive to the choice of draft model: a poorly chosen draft introduces latency bottlenecks that can negate or reverse any speedup. Prior work selects draft models through expensive empirical search and benchmarking across multiple architectures, requiring substantial compute and research overhead. This paper addresses the gap by developing an analytical framework—Speculative Decoding Scaling Laws (SDSL)—that connects pre-training scaling law hyperparameters directly to inference throughput, enabling principled, pre-training-time selection of the optimal draft model size without additional empirical search. Methodology: The framework proceeds in three stages. First, the authors establish that the expected token acceptance rate α (the probability a draft token is accepted by the target model) can be modeled as an affine function of the perplexities of the draft and target models: α = Ax + By + C, where x is draft perplexity and y is target perplexity. This relationship is empirically validated across OPT, Qwen1.5, Qwen2.5, LLaMA3, LLaMA3.1, and Seed-OSS model families on HellaSwag. Second, this affine α model is substituted into a closed-form throughput expression (in tokens per FLOP) derived from prior work, yielding a throughput formula parameterized solely by model size M, draft size N, and training dataset sizes D and D'. Third, using the Chinchilla/Hoffmann pre-training scaling law to express perplexity as a function of N and D, the authors derive throughput T entirely in terms of (M, N, D, D'). The throughput-optimal draft size N* is found numerically via grid search over logarithmically spaced (N, D, M, D') values, then fit analytically to the leading-order linear relation N*(M) = μM + M₀. Regression uses OLS with HC3 heteroskedasticity-robust standard errors over 288 observations. Key Results: The affine fit for α achieves R² = 0.60 across all model families and draft/target pairings (MSE = 0.00128), with draft model perplexity being the dominant explanatory variable—target model perplexity contributes only weakly and inconsistently. The throughput-optimal draft-to-target size ratio N*/M converges asymptotically to μ ≈ 2.71 × 10⁻³ (roughly 1/370), meaning the optimal draft model is approximately 200× smaller than the target in large-model regimes—consistent with empirical practice in the literature. The full regression of N*/M = μ + M₀/M + γ log D + γ' log D' achieves R² = 0.978, with dataset size effects (γ, γ') entering only as statistically marginal second-order corrections; target training data size D' is statistically indistinguishable from zero. Latency-based empirical validation on OPT-13B with a single A100 GPU confirms the analytical predictions: measured TTFT, TTOT, and TPOT are minimized at draft sizes closest to the analytically predicted N*, with latency increasing monotonically as |N − N*|/M grows, across heterogeneous draft families (OPT, Qwen1.5, Qwen2.5). Strengths: - Provides the first closed-form analytical relationship between pre-training hyperparameters (model size, dataset size) and speculative decoding throughput, removing the need for costly empirical draft model search before training. - The linear scaling law N* ≈ μM + M₀ is remarkably simple, robust across five diverse model families, and empirically validated with wall-clock latency measurements (not just token/FLOP proxies). - Dataset size effects are rigorously shown to be negligible second-order corrections, substantially simplifying the practical sizing rule to depend only on target model size M. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The framework assumes draft and target models are trained on comparable data distributions and with similar training recipes; architectural asymmetry, domain specialization, or post-training alignment (RLHF, instruction tuning) may degrade the quantitative accuracy of the scaling coefficients. - Analysis is restricted to autoregressive decoder-only text models; encoder–decoder architectures, mixture-of-experts, and multimodal models are explicitly out of scope and may require additional modeling assumptions. - The affine α model achieves only R² = 0.60 in the pooled cross-family regression, indicating meaningful residual variance not captured by perplexity alone, which could limit precision for specific model pairings far from the training distribution of the fit.

#20 — Innovative (73)

EgoReasoner: Learning Egocentric 4D Reasoning via Task-Adaptive Structured Thinking

Fangrui Zhu, Yunfeng Xi, Jianmo Ni, Mu Cai, Boqing Gong, Long Zhao, Chen Qu, Ian Miao, Yi Li, Cheng Zhong, Huaizu Jiang, Shwetak Patel · arxiv.org · karypis - new results
Why this rank: EgoReasoner introduces the well-motivated insight that structurally distinct 4D reasoning tasks require fundamentally different chain-of-thought decompositions, directly showing that task-agnostic RL degrades spatial-temporal reasoning. The combination of task-adaptive thinking templates with grounded multi-signal reward functions is creative, and achieving superior results with a 3B model over 7B baselines validates the approach. However, the work is currently narrow in domain scope (kitchen egocentric video only) and the SLAM-dependent data pipeline limits generalizability.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Egocentric video understanding requires continuous 4D spatial-temporal reasoning — maintaining a coherent world model from a constantly moving first-person camera. Existing methods fail on a suite of under-explored tasks from the HD-EPIC benchmark: fixture interaction counting, viewpoint-relative fixture location (clock-face orientations), object movement itinerary tracking, object location, object movement counting, and stationary object localization. These tasks demand three fundamentally different cognitive operations — spatial anchoring relative to a moving camera, temporal tracking of object trajectories over long horizons, and duration reasoning over static intervals — yet prior Chain-of-Thought and reinforcement learning approaches apply uniform reasoning pipelines across all tasks. The paper demonstrates that task-agnostic RL actively destabilizes spatio-temporal reasoning: accuracy on fixture interaction counting and fixture location degrades as training progresses under uniform optimization pressure. Methodology: EgoReasoner is a two-stage framework built on Qwen2.5-VL-3B. Stage I (Structured Cold-Start via SFT) uses Task-Adaptive Thinking Templates — six distinct CoT templates, one per task type — that decompose reasoning into grounded sub-steps matched to each task's cognitive structure (e.g., gaze initialization → target search → angular mapping for fixture location; entity grounding → trajectory breakdown → persistence check for object movement counting). A teacher model (Gemini) generates structured CoT traces from ground-truth 4D metadata derived from the Ego-Exo4D dataset via SLAM-calibrated 3D object detections and refined narrations. The student model is fine-tuned on 4.3K QA pairs to imitate these traces. Stage II (Grounded Reinforcement Fine-Tuning via GRPO) optimizes the Stage I model with four task-aware reward signals: an accuracy reward (binary answer correctness), a grounding reward (entity grounding via regex-parsed entity names + temporal grounding within a ±2.0s soft-matching window), a logic reward (task-specific consistency checks such as trajectory segment counts, clock-face angular accuracy computed as max(0, 1−d/6) on circular distance, and duration arithmetic), and a format reward (structural `<think>`/`<answer>` tag compliance). The composite reward r = αR_acc + λR_grd + γR_log + δR_struct is optimized via GRPO with KL regularization against the Stage I reference model. Training used 8 NVIDIA H100 GPUs for ~24 hours total. Key Results: On HD-EPIC benchmark, the unified Stage 2 model achieves 37.5% average accuracy across six tasks, surpassing the best open-source baseline Qwen2.5-VL-7B (25.7%) by over 10 points despite having fewer parameters (3B vs 7B). Key task-level results: Object Movement Counting reaches 59.5% (+26.5% over the best baseline of 33.0%), Object Location reaches 50.4% (+9.1% over best baseline of 41.2%), Object Movement Itinerary reaches 30.5% (vs. 20.2% for the best baseline). Ablations confirm both stages are necessary: SFT+CoT alone achieves 31.9% average, RFT without task-aware rewards achieves 31.1%, while the full pipeline reaches 36.8% on per-task training. Fixture Interaction Counting improved from 24.3% (SFT without CoT) to 34.3% with structured CoT (+10%). The ablation on reward components shows that grounding rewards are critical for temporal tracking tasks while logic rewards stabilize spatial tasks; standard RFT without these rewards degrades performance on fixture interaction counting and fixture location over training steps. Human baseline is 91.1%, indicating the benchmark remains very challenging. Strengths: - The insight that different 4D reasoning tasks require structurally distinct CoT decompositions — not just generic chain-of-thought — is well-motivated and empirically validated; the ablation directly shows task-agnostic RL hurts performance. - The automated metadata-driven pipeline for generating grounded, hallucination-resistant training data from SLAM-calibrated 3D detections and Gemini-refined narrations avoids reliance on expensive human annotation at scale. - Task-aware reward functions provide per-step intermediate supervision (entity grounding, temporal alignment, logical consistency) that coarse outcome-only rewards cannot offer, with demonstrated stabilization of training dynamics across all six task categories. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Stationary Object Localization remains an outlier where all variants struggle (~20–24.5% vs. 84.3% human baseline), because these videos are 8–10 minutes long — far exceeding the 3–5 minute clips the model handles well — exposing fundamental context-window limitations that neither SFT nor RFT resolve. - The approach is currently limited to kitchen-domain egocentric video from Ego-Exo4D; generalization to other egocentric domains (outdoor, industrial, AR/VR) is untested, and the SLAM-based metadata pipeline may not generalize without domain-specific calibration.

#21 — Innovative (73)

TimeWarp: Evaluating Web Agents by Revisiting the Past

Md Farhan Ishmam, Kenneth Marino · arxiv.org · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: TIMEWARP introduces a genuinely novel problem formulation — temporal generalization of web agents across UI design eras — that no prior benchmark has systematically studied. The TIMETRAJ plan-distillation algorithm cleverly separates one-time human annotation cost from scalable multi-version rollout, and the TIMEWARP-BC training approach (supervising on thinking/planning/memory tokens, not just actions) yields substantial gains. The finding that visual agents are far more brittle to version shifts than text-based agents is actionable and non-obvious.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Web agents are increasingly evaluated on static benchmarks—either simulated environments with fixed UI or live web benchmarks that cannot be studied in a controlled setting. Neither captures the dynamic, ever-changing nature of the real internet. A critical open question is whether agents trained on one version of a website generalize when the UI, layout, or interaction paradigm changes. No prior benchmark systematically studies this temporal generalization problem, leaving researchers unable to diagnose or mitigate web agents' brittleness to distributional shift across web design eras. Methodology: TIMEWARP is a containerized benchmark built around three web environments—Wiki (Wikipedia-style encyclopedic), News (BBC-style information retrieval), and Shop (Amazon/Temu-style e-commerce)—each with six frontend versions (v1–v6) spanning distinct eras of web UI design, from early 2000s layouts to modern interfaces. The 1,386 tasks (231 goals × 6 versions) are manually authored without template placeholders and validated by external verifiers. Evaluation uses GPT-5 as an LLM-as-a-judge with binary success/failure rewards. To support training, the authors introduce TIMETRAJ, a human-in-the-loop plan distillation algorithm: an LLM planner generates draft execution plans that humans refine into version-invariant checkpoint-based plans; a teacher agent then rolls out trajectories across all six versions automatically. This separates the high-cost human annotation (done once on one version) from the low-cost automated rollout (done across all versions). TIMEWARP-BC extends standard behavior cloning by training on the full teacher-agent response—including action, thinking, planning, and memory tokens—rather than action tokens alone, improving the student agent's ability to reason, plan, and remember across complex multi-step tasks. Key Results: Zero-shot VLM agents exhibit severe performance instability across versions: Qwen-3 VL 8B with Set-of-Marks ranges from 2.6% to 21.4% success rate across versions, Qwen-3 VL 8B Thinking spans 1.3–12.3%, and Gemma-3 12B spans 7.4–14.8%. Text-based agents (HTML/AXT observations) are substantially more robust, with most models showing ~6% variation across versions. The highest zero-shot performance is 29.2% (Qwen-3 4B Thinking, AXT). Training with TIMEWARP-BC yields substantial gains: +17.3% overall for Qwen-3 4B (20.4% → 37.7%) and +26.3% for Llama-3.1 8B (0% → 27.0%), with improvements consistent across all versions. Multi-version training (v1–v5) generalizes better to held-out versions than single-version training. Ablations on Qwen-3 4B confirm that training on all four token types (action + thinking + memory + planning) achieves 38.5% overall vs. 23.3% with action tokens only. TW performance scales approximately linearly with training sample count, while standard BC saturates early. Cross-dataset pretraining on WebArena Lite degrades TIMEWARP performance, suggesting distribution mismatch. Strengths: - Principled operationalization of web temporal generalization: six historically grounded UI versions per site enable controlled, reproducible study of distributional shift that live-web benchmarks cannot provide - TIMETRAJ's plan-distillation design cleanly separates one-time human cost from scalable automated rollout, making trajectory collection across future web versions practical - Comprehensive ablations on token types, context length, LoRA vs. full fine-tuning, version generalization, and continual learning collectively establish clear guidance for training robust web agents Weaknesses / Limitations: - Coverage is limited to three websites and six versions each; the chosen eras and design inflection points may not generalize to the full diversity of real-world web evolution - TIMEWARP-BC training focuses on textual observations (AXT); visual agent training remains underexplored, and the gap between text and vision performance suggests visual robustness is still an unsolved problem - Continual learning experiments reveal catastrophic forgetting when fine-tuning sequentially on new versions, but no solution is proposed—this key practical challenge is left as future work

#22 — Innovative (73)

Distilling LLM Semantic Priors into Encoder-Only Multi-Talker ASR with Talker-Count Routing

Hao Shi, Yusuke Fujita, Roman Koshkin, Mengjie Zhao, Yuan Gao, Lianbo Liu, Yui Sudo · arxiv.org · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: This paper presents a well-engineered solution to a real problem — using LLM semantic priors only at training time via distillation, then discarding the LLM for fast encoder-only multi-talker inference. The dramatic improvement on 3-talker overlap (24.5% vs. 39.1% WER) while being 26× faster is a compelling result, and the complete failure without distillation (WER ~70%) strongly validates the approach. However, the core techniques (knowledge distillation, branch architectures, CTC) are individually well-established, and evaluation is limited to the controlled LibriMix benchmark.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Multi-talker automatic speech recognition (MT-ASR) must transcribe all speakers from overlapping speech, a challenging task where existing approaches fall into two camps: (1) encoder-decoder systems using large language models (LLMs) as decoders that provide strong semantic priors but are slow and degrade significantly under heavy 3-talker overlap, and (2) encoder-only CTC-based systems that are fast but unstable under severe overlap without semantic regularization. Both approaches also typically require knowing the number of talkers a priori. The core bottleneck identified is that LLM decoders are forced to disentangle heavily mixed encoder representations, suggesting the encoder itself is under-equipped for overlapping speech. Methodology: The paper proposes an encoder-only MT-ASR framework that uses an LLM (LLaMA-3.2-1B) exclusively during training as a distillation teacher, eliminating it at inference. The architecture uses WavLM-Large (24 Transformer layers) as the backbone, split into 12 shared layers followed by two independent branches (for 2-talker and 3-talker conditions), each initialized from WavLM layers 13–24. Each branch is followed by an LSTM-based post-encoder separator that produces talker-ordered streams, on which serialized CTC is applied per-talker stream. Training proceeds in two phases: Phase 1 adapts the LLM to multi-talker conditioning via LoRA and special token embeddings while distilling its SOT-based guidance into the encoder via backpropagation; Phase 2 trains the separator and serialized CTC heads while continuing the SOT distillation signal with a hybrid objective L = α·L_CTC + (1−α)·L_SOT. A Talker-Count Head (TCH) is introduced to predict the number of speakers (2 vs. 3) using attentive statistics pooling over the shared encoder output, dynamically routing decoding to the appropriate branch. Key Results: On LibriMix (noisy condition), the proposed encoder-only model (ID-8, with 6 TCH layers) achieves WER of 9.7% on Libri2Mix eval and 24.5% on Libri3Mix eval, compared to the LLM baseline (SOT-Llama-1B) at 11.3% and 39.1% respectively. On clean Libri2Mix, the model matches SOT-Llama-1B (4.1% vs. 4.6%) and dramatically outperforms it on clean Libri3Mix (14.3% vs. 21.6%). The encoder-only model also outperforms larger LLM decoders (SOT-Llama-3B: 31.7% noisy 3-mix) in the 3-talker condition. Inference speed is dramatically faster: RTF of 0.0043 vs. 0.1150 (Libri2Mix) and 0.0106 vs. 0.0981 (Libri3Mix) on a single H100 GPU — roughly 26× and 9× faster respectively. Training without LLM distillation (ID-5) completely fails (WER ~70%), confirming that semantic regularization is critical for stable CTC training under overlap. TCH accuracy reaches ~99% for 2-talker and ~88–94% for 3-talker conditions (clean) with 12 Transformer layers. Strengths: - Novel train-time-only LLM usage: the distillation framework captures semantic priors without any inference-time cost, achieving a highly favorable accuracy/speed tradeoff. - Significant improvement on 3-talker overlapped speech, which is the hardest regime and where prior LLM-decoder approaches regress substantially. - The Talker-Count Head elegantly removes the fixed-talker-count assumption using attentive statistics pooling, enabling a single model to handle variable speaker counts. Weaknesses / Limitations: - TCH accuracy for 3-talker mixtures (88–94% clean, lower for noisy) lags well behind 2-talker accuracy (~99%), and TCH errors directly degrade ASR performance; the gap between oracle talker count (ID-6: 22.2% WER) and TCH-guided (ID-8: 24.5%) remains non-trivial. - Evaluation is limited to LibriMix (read speech from LibriSpeech), a relatively controlled dataset; generalization to spontaneous, real-world multi-talker scenarios with disfluencies, reverberation, or more than 3 speakers is not demonstrated.

#23 — Innovative (73)

Towards Controllable and Interpretable Latent Modeling for Vision and Beyond

ses.library.usyd.edu.au · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: This thesis introduces a unifying principle of selective latent modeling across four diverse settings, with each chapter contributing a distinct novel method. The SAVA framework stands out for its formal theoretical guarantee of recovering minimal sufficient shared latents via masked reconstruction, grounding empirical results in identifiability theory. OCP-CL and CoF are genuinely practical plug-and-play contributions that improve existing baselines without architectural changes. However, the unification is more narrative than technical — the four chapters share a conceptual theme but no shared formalism or system — and the thesis format compiles previously published work rather than presenting a single cohesive advance.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Deep learning models for vision and multimodal tasks rely on high-dimensional latent representations that are difficult to interpret and control. Latent variables typically entangle task-relevant semantic factors with task-irrelevant noise, offering no mechanism to selectively preserve, suppress, or align specific factors. This lack of structured organization limits reliability in tasks requiring fine-grained semantic reasoning, cross-modal coherence, or transparent decision-making. Existing disentanglement approaches treat latent spaces as homogeneous vectors and lack explicit mechanisms for selective invariance aligned with task semantics. This thesis investigates how to impose meaningful structure on latent representations to achieve both interpretability and controllability across four diverse settings: ordinal visual learning, visual in-context reasoning, multimodal generation, and agentic workflows. Methodology: The thesis unifies four published works under the principle of selective latent modeling — explicitly distinguishing task-relevant from task-irrelevant factors rather than enforcing uniform invariance. Chapter 2 introduces Ordinal Content Preserving Contrastive Learning (OCP-CL). Standard contrastive learning augmentations (Gaussian blur, color jitter) destroy ordinal cues (e.g., wrinkles for age estimation, microaneurysms for diabetic retinopathy grading). OCP-CL trains a GAN (StyleGAN2 backbone) under a minimal change principle: the latent space is partitioned into ordinal factor z_o and non-ordinal factor z_n via a learned sparse mask (L1-regularized). By fixing z_o and resampling z_n, ordinal-preserving contrastive augmentations are synthesized and incorporated into existing ordinal regression frameworks (OR-CNN, CNNPOR, SORD, POE, MWR) as a plug-and-play regularizer. Chapter 3 introduces Chain-of-Focus (CoF) prompting for Large Autoregressive Vision Models (LAVMs). Analogous to chain-of-thought for LLMs, CoF injects intermediate saliency-based reasoning steps into visual prompts without modifying model parameters. Prompt pairs are selected using two criteria: Jaccard similarity between VQ-GAN codebook entries (query relevance) and codebook index diversity of prompt targets (annotation richness). Intermediate steps are constructed by incrementally revealing image regions in descending saliency order using a pretrained U²-Net saliency detector. Chapter 4 introduces Selective Audio-Visual Alignment (SAVA) for text-to-audio-video (T2AV) generation. Prior T2AV methods assume full audio-visual correspondence, but audio and video share only partial semantics (e.g., roads are visible but silent). SAVA learns binary mask functions conditioned on both modalities that isolate the minimal shared latent block — provably recovering the Markov blanket of each modality in the other. A three-stage pipeline follows: (1) masked latent adaptation via cross-modal reconstruction with L1 sparsity, (2) LoRA fine-tuning of diffusion models conditioned on aligned latents, (3) cascaded diffusion inference. A formal theorem proves that the masked reconstruction objective with sufficient sparsity recovers exactly the minimal sufficient shared latents. Chapter 5 introduces MedDCR, which treats agentic coding workflows as latent procedural structures to be learned rather than manually designed. A meta-agent system with three roles (Designer, Coder, Reflector) iteratively proposes, executes, and evaluates workflows for ICD-10 medical coding, maintaining a memory archive of high-performing designs for reuse and progressive refinement. Key Results: OCP-CL consistently improves all five baseline ordinal regression models across three datasets. On the Adience age estimation dataset, accuracy improvements range from +1.0% to +5.3% and MAE reductions from 4.4% to 8.5%. On diabetic retinopathy grading, accuracy improves by 0.8–1.8% across baselines. In transfer learning evaluation (linear probing), OCP-CL achieves 65.2% accuracy and 0.50 MAE on diabetic retinopathy, 53.1%/0.61 on Adience, and 57.5%/0.52 on SkyFinder, outperforming SupMoCo, SupCon, S-LooC, and SupCReg across all three datasets. CoF prompting improves image segmentation IoU by 4.8% over the second-best method (SupPR) on LLaMA-300M, 3.0% on LLaMA-1B, and 6.3% on LLaMA-7B. Failure rates are reduced by up to 13.9% on LLaMA-1B. On pose estimation, P-ACC improves by up to 11.15% on LLaMA-1B and 18% on LLaMA-7B. Ablation confirms cognitive reasoning (saliency-based intermediate steps) is the dominant component. SAVA achieves FVD of 662.9 and FAD of 5.49 on VGGSound (6.5% and 16.0% relative reductions over the best baseline CoDi), with the highest AVHScore (0.206) and CAVPSIM (0.165) among all T2AV methods. On AudioCaps, FVD reaches 817.6 with AVHScore of 0.142. V2A generation also outperforms SpecVQGAN and SeeHear across KL divergence, Inception Score, and FAD. MedDCR outperforms prior agentic and expert-designed baselines on MDACE and ACI-BENCH medical coding benchmarks, with ablations confirming the necessity of workflow exemplars, reflector feedback, and guideline constraints. Strengths: - The unifying principle of selective latent modeling provides genuine theoretical and empirical coherence across four otherwise disparate problem settings. - Chapter 4 includes a formal theoretical guarantee (provable recovery of minimal sufficient shared latents) grounding the empirical SAVA results in identifiability theory. - OCP-CL and CoF are plug-and-play: they improve existing baselines without requiring architectural changes, making them highly practical. - The thesis targets settings with limited supervision and minimal architectural modification, addressing a realistic constraint in deployment. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The thesis is a compilation of four published works with limited unification beyond the conceptual framing; the connection between chapters is mostly narrative rather than technically instantiated in a single system. - OCP-CL relies on a GAN (StyleGAN2) for augmentation generation, which introduces training instability and sensitivity to the sparsity hyperparameter λ; performance on fine-grained medical images with severe class imbalance (diabetic retinopathy) shows only modest gains. - CoF prompting depends on the quality of off-the-shelf saliency detectors (U²-Net) and is restricted to tasks where meaningful segmentation masks are available in the prompt pool; performance saturates or degrades for the smallest model (LLaMA-300M) with two intermediate steps, suggesting sensitivity to context length. - SAVA's theoretical guarantees require four non-trivial assumptions (DAG faithfulness, block-wise reparameterization, decoder universality, mask universality) whose validity in practice is not empirically validated, and the cascaded generation pipeline propagates errors from the video generation stage into audio.

#24 — Innovative (73)

Matching clinicians with clinical trials using AI

nature.com · Jimeng Sun - new articles
Why this rank: DocTr reframes clinical trial site selection as a clinician ranking problem rather than the standard patient-eligibility classification task, which is a genuinely novel problem formulation. The cross-modal fusion of unstructured trial documents, structured ICD-10 claim distributions, and payment-based enrollment proxies via heterogeneous graph attention is technically creative, and the decoupled genetic algorithm for multi-objective fairness optimization is an elegant architectural choice. The 58–62% improvement over baselines with strong zero-shot generalization (only 12% degradation under temporal split vs. 43% for baselines) demonstrates the approach is not merely well-engineered but captures fundamentally better representations. The practical cost estimation capability (CCC > 0.75) adds a dimension absent from all prior work.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Clinical trial site selection is chronically inefficient: 37% of sites under-enroll, 11% enroll no participants at all, and 80% of trials miss their timelines, costing $600,000 to $8 million per day in delays. Manual recruitment methods are slow, geographically biased, and favor well-known investigators, leading to poor participant diversity. Existing AI approaches focus on individual patient–trial eligibility (a classification task), not on clinician-level site recommendation (a ranking task). There is no large-scale, data-driven system for recommending investigator sites that simultaneously optimizes for accuracy, demographic fairness, and operational efficiency. Methodology: DocTr is a cross-modal deep learning framework that formulates site selection as a clinician ranking problem. It integrates three complementary data sources: (1) unstructured trial documents (summaries and eligibility criteria from ClinicalTrials.gov) encoded via ClinicalBERT into semantic embeddings; (2) structured patient encounter data from the IQVIA medical claims database, representing each clinician as a five-year average distribution over 1,718 three-digit ICD-10 codes, compressed into latent vectors; and (3) historical clinician–trial payment relationships from the CMS OpenPayments database, used as a proxy for past trial enrollment. These are fused via a heterogeneous graph attention network (Hetero-GAT / LightGCN) that propagates information between clinician and trial nodes, producing structurally enriched embeddings. Semantic and structural matching scores are combined via a learned parameter α, and the model is trained with Bayesian Personalized Ranking (BPR) loss. A downstream genetic algorithm post-processes the ranked list to jointly optimize accuracy (composite similarity score), demographic fairness (entropy over race, ethnicity, and geography), and minimal competing trial overlap—without using demographic attributes in the primary matching score itself. Key Results: Evaluated on 24,984 US clinicians and 5,210 clinical trials under a challenging temporal split (training on trials from 1989–2019, testing on trials starting 2020–2026 including 27.3% entirely unseen clinicians), DocTr achieves a composite similarity (CS) score of 0.60 across all list sizes (CS@5, CS@10, CS@20, CS@GT), compared to 0.37–0.38 for the best baseline (NCL), representing a 58–62% improvement. Both ablated variants (DocTr-semantic and DocTr-structural) outperform baselines but lag full DocTr, confirming each component is necessary. The genetic optimization (DocTr-genetic) increases race fairness entropy by 7–9% and ethnicity fairness entropy by 17–25% relative to ground truth enrolment, while reducing the average number of competing trials per recommended clinician from 2.1 (DocTr-regular) to 0.03. For recruitment cost estimation, DocTr achieves CCC = 0.83 for total cost per trial, CCC = 0.75 for total cost per clinician, and CCC = 0.84 for average cost per enrolment per clinician. Strengths: - Strong zero-shot generalization: performance degrades only 12% from random to temporal split, far less than the 43% average drop across baselines, demonstrating robustness to unseen trials and clinicians. - Multi-objective optimization via the genetic algorithm elegantly decouples fairness from the primary ranking signal, allowing trial managers to tune fairness weights without retraining the model. - Practical cost and enrolment estimation (CCC > 0.75 across all financial metrics) provides actionable budget planning support, a capability absent from all baselines. Weaknesses / Limitations: - ICD-10 billing codes as the sole clinician representation introduce reimbursement bias and are particularly sparse for subspecialists (e.g., oncologists, cardiologists), leading to lower performance in these high-value trial domains. - Geographic fairness is approximated using zip-code census data rather than actual patient demographics, which can diverge substantially for specialists drawing patients from wide regions; the model is also restricted to US clinicians due to data constraints. - OpenPayments data conflates multiple payment types (consulting, research, royalties), and the assumption that payment amounts are proportional to enrolment is a necessary but potentially inaccurate proxy for actual recruitment history.

#25 — Innovative (72)

SageSched: Efficient LLM Scheduling Confronting Demand Uncertainty and Hybridity

Zhenghao Gan, Yichen Bao, Yifei Liu, Chen Chen, Quan Chen, Minyi Guo · arxiv.org · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: SageSched brings genuine novelty by applying the Gittins index from bandit theory to LLM scheduling, exploiting full output-length distributions rather than point estimates — a theoretically principled departure from prior heuristic or prediction-based schedulers. The training-free semantic-aware history predictor is a clever design that avoids the retraining burden of fine-tuned length predictors, and the unified O²/2 + IO cost model elegantly captures the compute-memory duality that prior work ignores. However, the individual components (retrieval-based prediction, Gittins index, cost modeling) are established techniques recombined for a new setting rather than fundamentally new methods.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: LLM inference scheduling is complicated by two fundamental properties absent in conventional workloads: *uncertainty* (output token length is non-deterministic and unknowable until generation completes) and *hybridity* (inferences are simultaneously compute- and memory-intensive due to KVCache). Existing schedulers either use demand-agnostic policies like FCFS (suffering head-of-line blocking) or prediction-based approaches that rely on heavy fine-tuned models to predict a single output-length value—losing distributional information critical for optimal scheduling—and ignore memory costs entirely. The gap is a scheduler that handles distributional uncertainty and jointly models compute and memory resource consumption. Methodology: SageSched integrates three novel components built atop vLLM. First, a *semantic-aware history-based predictor* replaces fine-tuned length models: for each incoming request, it retrieves historically similar requests (using FAISS cosine-similarity search with a 0.8 threshold over a 10,000-entry FIFO history window) and uses their empirical output-length distribution directly as the prediction—no training required. Second, a *resource-bound-based cost model* captures both compute and memory costs with a unified formula C = O²/2 + IO, where I and O are input and output token lengths; this formula applies uniformly across memory-bound and compute-bound regimes since both costs share the same quadratic-linear structure (differing only in units). Third, an *uncertainty-aware scheduling policy* applies the Gittins index from multi-armed bandit theory: each request's Gittins index G(D) = inf_{Δ>0} E[min{X,Δ}]/P(X≤Δ) is computed from its cost distribution and used as the scheduling priority, with indices refreshed at bucket boundaries (bucket size 200) to balance timeliness and stability. Preemption is supported via swap-compute overlapping and KVCache prefetching. Key Results: On mixed-dataset workloads (SharedGPT, Alpaca-Summarization, Document-Write) with Llama3.1-8B and Qwen3-32B models, SageSched outperforms the next-best scheduler (TRAIL) by over 28.7% in average TTLT at 8 requests/second. It consistently leads across all individual datasets and task rates. Ablations confirm each component's contribution: the semantic-aware predictor beats both semantic-unaware history-based and LLM-based (DistillBert) predictors; the resource-bound cost model outperforms output-length-only and overall-length-based alternatives; and Gittins-index ordering with runtime refresh beats mean-value ordering. The predictor adds only ~0.37 ms per request (0.22 ms embedding + 0.15 ms retrieval) versus ~3.6 ms for LLM-based predictors, with zero training overhead. Scheduling overhead grows only linearly with cluster size, remaining ~100 ms per request at 64 GPU nodes—negligible relative to multi-second inference latencies. Strengths: - Training-free output-length distribution predictor that is both lighter-weight (<0.5 ms latency) and more accurate than fine-tuned model alternatives, with zero LLM-specific retraining when switching models. - Theoretically grounded scheduling policy (Gittins index) with proven optimality for minimizing average latency under unknown duration distributions, properly exploiting distributional rather than point estimates. - Unified cost model that correctly handles the compute-memory hybrid nature of LLM inference, which prior schedulers ignore by using output length alone as a proxy for service cost. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The history-based predictor requires a warm-up period; during early operation when similar historical requests are sparse, it falls back to public dataset distributions, which may be less accurate for specialized deployment workloads. - The approach is evaluated only on three datasets and two model sizes; its effectiveness on highly heterogeneous or long-context workloads (e.g., reasoning models with very long outputs) is not thoroughly characterized, and the O²/2 + IO cost formula may need revisiting as reasoning LLMs with very long decode sequences become prevalent.

#26 — Innovative (72)

Retrieving Minimal and Sufficient Reasoning Subgraphs with Graph Foundation Models for Path-aware GraphRAG

Haonan Yuan, Qingyun Sun, Junhua Shi, Mingjun Liu, Jiaqi Yuan, Ziwei Zhang, Xingcheng Fu, Jianxin Li · arxiv.org · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: This paper introduces a genuinely novel label-free Information Bottleneck formulation for subgraph selection that avoids expensive ground-truth annotations, and the cross-domain GFM pre-training over 60 knowledge graphs with prototype-driven contrastive alignment is an ambitious design that yields strong zero-shot generalization across 7 out-of-distribution domains. The path-aware prompt construction is a meaningful contribution to making GraphRAG reasoning interpretable. However, the overall paradigm remains within the established GraphRAG framework, and the reliance on pre-built knowledge graphs with OpenIE extraction limits practical novelty.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Graph-based retrieval-augmented generation (GraphRAG) systems typically reduce knowledge graphs to ranked entity/document lists, discarding relational structure critical for multi-hop reasoning. The few subgraph-based retrieval methods that exist rely on heuristic rules tightly coupled to domain-specific graph distributions, making them fragile under cold-start conditions where target-domain data is scarce. Without principled supervision, these methods produce reasoning contexts that are either informationally incomplete (missing key reasoning hops) or structurally redundant (containing irrelevant entities). Three concrete challenges motivate this work: (1) cross-domain subgraph retrieval without domain-specific training data, (2) identifying subgraphs that are simultaneously minimal and sufficient for answering a query, and (3) translating retrieved graph structure into actionable in-context prompts for LLMs. Methodology: The paper proposes GFM-Retriever, a three-stage framework. In the pre-training stage, a query-conditioned Graph Foundation Model (GFM) is trained as a cross-domain retriever over multi-domain knowledge graphs (60 KG indexes, 14M+ triples). The GFM uses query-dependent message passing that initializes query-entity embeddings from the question tokens, then propagates relational context through triple-level encoding and aggregation. Cross-domain generalization is achieved via prototype-driven contrastive alignment (aligning entity representations to domain prototypes) and an Information Gain Contrast (IGC) regularizer that encourages semantically discriminative embeddings. In the fine-tuning stage, the pre-trained GFM backbone is frozen and a lightweight subgraph selector is trained using a label-free Information Bottleneck (IB) objective. The IB objective minimizes subgraph size (via a Laplacian-based connectivity penalty) while maximizing mutual information between the subgraph and the query — all without requiring ground-truth subgraph labels. Differentiable node selection is achieved via Gumbel-Sigmoid relaxation. During inference, the retrieved subgraph is converted into structured in-context prompts by extracting top-ranked relational paths (via DFS up to hop budget l, scored by entity selection probability and relation depth penalties), enabling explicit path-aware multi-hop reasoning. Key Results: GFM-Retriever achieves state-of-the-art average rank of 1.1 on entity/document retrieval across HotpotQA, MuSiQue, and 2WikiMultiHopQA (Table 1), compared to runner-up PropRAG at 2.8 and HippoRAG 2 at 3.1. For end-to-end QA (Table 2), GFM-Retriever alone ranks 3.0 average, and GFM-Retriever + IRCoT ranks 1.3, outperforming HippoRAG + IRCoT (4.7). On HotpotQA, GFM-Retriever + IRCoT achieves 60.5 EM / 72.9 F1 / 75.5 P / 74.5 R vs. HippoRAG + IRCoT at 45.7 / 59.3 / 59.0 / 62.9. Cross-domain generalization over seven out-of-distribution QA benchmarks (biomedical PubMedQA, customer support DelucionQA/TechQA/ExpertQA/EManual, general knowledge MS Marco/HAGRID) shows GFM-Retriever consistently outperforms PropRAG and HippoRAG 2, which exhibit clear domain-shift degradation. Efficiency analysis shows GFM-Retriever achieves near-highest R@5D with sub-second retrieval time, favorably compared to iterative methods like HippoRAG + IRCoT that trade latency for recall. Ablation studies confirm that all five components (Proto+IGC pre-training, Tune fine-tuning, SubG selector, and Path-aware Prompt) each contribute meaningfully, with removing Proto+IGC causing the largest drop in retrieval (degrading to GFM-RAG level) and removing SubG causing the largest drop in QA. Strengths: - The label-free IB formulation for subgraph selection is theoretically principled, with proven bounds connecting the tractable surrogate objectives to the true IB objective, and does not require expensive ground-truth subgraph annotations. - The cross-domain pre-training design (prototype alignment + IGC regularizer over 60 multi-domain KGs) provides strong zero-shot generalization, clearly demonstrated across 7 out-of-distribution domains including biomedical and customer support. - Explicit relational path extraction into structured in-context prompts makes multi-hop reasoning interpretable and directly improves QA performance, as confirmed by ablation (removing Prompt reduces QA while leaving retrieval largely intact). Weaknesses / Limitations: - The framework depends on pre-built knowledge graphs with explicit entity/relation triples; constructing these KGs requires an OpenIE extraction pipeline (using GPT-4o-mini) and entity resolution, adding substantial preprocessing cost that may be prohibitive for new domains. - DFS-based relational path extraction has worst-case exponential complexity in the hop budget l and maximum node degree Δ (O(Δ^l)), making it potentially expensive on dense, high-degree subgraphs; the paper mitigates this with a fixed hop budget but does not fully address the scalability concern for very dense KGs.

#27 — Innovative (72)

XGenBoost: Synthesizing Small and Large Tabular Datasets with XGBoost

Jim Achterberg, Marcel Haas, Bram van Dijk, Marco Spruit · arxiv.org · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: XGenBoost demonstrates genuine methodological creativity by repurposing XGBoost — a discriminative learner — as the backbone for two complementary generative architectures, solving non-trivial constraints (single-output, no mini-batch, permutation invariance). The hierarchical binary classifier approach for numerical features with quantile dequantization is a particularly clever design. The ~240x speedup over TabSyn on million-row datasets while matching or exceeding quality represents a meaningful practical advance, and the CPU-only requirement democratizes synthetic data generation.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Synthetic tabular data generation is important for data augmentation, privacy-preserving data sharing, and federated learning. Current state-of-the-art methods rely on deep neural networks requiring GPUs and substantial compute, which are inequitably distributed globally. Tree ensembles like XGBoost have well-known inductive biases that make them superior discriminators for mixed-type tabular data — they handle categorical splits natively, require minimal hyperparameter tuning, and are efficient to train. However, adapting tree-based learners to generative architectures is non-trivial: they are single-output (requiring feature-specific models), do not support mini-batch training, and require extending the training set to learn permutation invariances — making them poorly suited to large datasets. No prior tree-based generative model scales well to large tabular data. Methodology: The paper presents XGenBoost, two complementary generative architectures both built on XGBoost. For small datasets, XGenB-DF is a Denoising Diffusion Implicit Model (DDIM) using XGBoost as the score estimator. It combines Gaussian diffusion for numerical features and multinomial diffusion for categorical features, training one XGBoost model per feature per diffusion timestep (T=50, K=100 noise levels). A velocity-prediction reparameterization with DDIM sampling yields high-fidelity samples. A dropout procedure (p=0.1, masking numerical inputs with their mean) prevents memorization. For large datasets, XGenB-AR is a fixed-order hierarchical autoregressive model where conditionals are learned by XGBoost classifiers/regressors. Numerical features use hierarchical binary XGBoost classifiers (meta-tree height H=5) that impose ordinal inductive bias and model non-continuous distributions via empirical quantile function dequantization. Categorical features use multi-class XGBoost classifiers. High-cardinality categories are handled via KMeans clustering of mean-vector embeddings into at most K_max clusters. The fixed feature order eliminates the need for training-set extension, making the approach scalable. XGenB-AR uses 30 boosting rounds and max depth 3; XGenB-DF mirrors the FD/FF hyperparameters (100 rounds, depth 7). Key Results: On the Small Benchmark (27 datasets from Jolicoeur-Martineau et al. 2024), XGenB-AR achieves the best average rank across Shape (2.28), Trend (3.59), and Detection (3.59) metrics, outperforming all neural baselines (CTGAN, TVAE, TabDDPM) and tree-based baselines (ARF, FD, FF, UT). XGenB-DF variants achieve competitive or best performance on Trend and MLE metrics. On the Big Benchmark (11 larger datasets from Mueller et al. 2025), XGenB-AR achieves rank 1.00 on Shape, 1.36 on Trend, and 1.82 on Detection — outperforming TabSyn, CTGAN, TVAE, TabDDPM, ARF, and SMOTE on nearly all fidelity and utility metrics. Critically, XGenB-AR trains in ~0.04–2.9 minutes on the Big Benchmark datasets (e.g., 2.877 min on acsincome with 1.6M rows), compared to 684 minutes for TabSyn on the same dataset — a ~240x speedup. Inference time is competitive with diffusion models. XGenB-AR achieves rank 1.00 on training time across all Big Benchmark datasets. Strengths: - Principled architecture design respecting tree-learner constraints (single-output, no mini-batch, native categorical handling), yielding both correctness and efficiency gains - Dramatic scalability improvement for XGenB-AR: trains in minutes on CPU for datasets with millions of rows, without quality degradation vs. GPU-dependent neural methods - Comprehensive evaluation across fidelity (Shape, Trend, Detection, α-Precision, β-Recall), utility (MLE with ROCAUC/R²/F1/RMSE), and privacy (DCR) metrics across 38 total datasets Weaknesses / Limitations: - XGenB-DF requires extending the training set K≈100 times, making it prohibitively expensive for large datasets; it is excluded from the Big Benchmark for this reason - Neither model currently supports imputation natively, though extensions via pseudo-likelihood or inpainting are discussed as future work - XGenB-AR's sequential inference over features may become slow for datasets with hundreds or thousands of features, though in practice inference time on Big Benchmark datasets was comparable to diffusion models

#28 — Innovative (72)

Boosting deep Reinforcement Learning using pretraining with Logical Options

Zihan Ye, Phil Chau, Raban Emunds, Jannis Blüml, Cedric Derstroff, Quentin Delfosse, Oleg Arenz, Kristian Kersting · arxiv.org · neural symbolic - new results
Why this rank: H²RL presents a creative integration of differentiable symbolic logic as a pretraining scaffold that is discarded at inference time, elegantly solving the runtime overhead problem of neuro-symbolic RL. The results are compelling with order-of-magnitude improvements on long-horizon Atari tasks, and extension to continuous action spaces is meaningful. However, the reliance on hand-crafted logical rules and pretrained option workers limits generality, and the core idea of distilling structured priors into neural weights during pretraining builds on well-established knowledge distillation and curriculum learning paradigms.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Deep reinforcement learning agents frequently suffer from policy misalignment — they exploit early, dense reward signals ("shortcut learning" or "reward hacking") rather than pursuing the intended long-horizon objectives. For example, in Atari games like Seaquest and Kangaroo, PPO agents fixate on shooting enemies rather than refilling oxygen or climbing to reach the joey. Prior symbolic and neuro-symbolic approaches address this by integrating logical reasoning at inference time, but this creates computational overhead, latency, and difficulty scaling to continuous action spaces. Reward shaping can guide exploration but requires tedious domain-specific tuning. Methodology: The paper introduces Hybrid Hierarchical Reinforcement Learning (H²RL), a two-stage framework inspired by the cognitive concept of scaffolding. The core innovation is using differentiable symbolic logic and options exclusively during a pretraining phase to inject structured behavioral priors into neural network parameters, after which the symbolic engine is discarded at inference time. H²RL has four components: (1) a differentiable symbolic logic manager that maps object-centric symbolic state z_t to a distribution over a finite set of options via forward-chaining first-order logic made differentiable through tensor encodings, softmax rule selection, and softor (log-sum-exp) approximations of logical disjunction; (2) pretrained option workers — low-level neural policies trained on subtasks like "climb_up" or "use_hammer"; (3) a neural RL policy (PPO-based CNN) operating on raw pixels; and (4) a Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) gating module that blends the logic-induced hierarchical policy with the neural policy using learned mixture weights β. The full hybrid policy π_H is a convex combination: β^L · π_L + β^N · π_N. Stage 1 (pretraining) jointly trains the neural policy, gating module, and logic critic with PPO while the logic manager selects options. Stage 2 (posttraining, yielding H²RL++) further fine-tunes only the neural policy component via standard environment interaction, allowing it to surpass the symbolic scaffold. Key Results: H²RL++ achieves scores orders of magnitude higher than all baselines on Kangaroo (131,842 ± 1,221) and DonkeyKong (216,793 ± 125,655), versus PPO's 14,592 and 4,536 respectively. On Seaquest, H²RL++ reaches 4,759 vs. PPO's 3,247. Critically, although PPO and DQN achieve high raw scores on Kangaroo, they remain stuck in floor 1 with 0% success rate on reaching higher floors — H²RL-pretrained variants achieve 100% success on floors 2–4. H²RL serves as a universal pretraining substrate: H²DQN+ reaches 116,665 on Kangaroo and 6,887 on DonkeyKong vs. base DQN's 14,822 and 3,205. In the Continuous Atari Learning Environment (CALE), H²RL achieves 84,665 on Kangaroo vs. PPO's 1,785 and hPPO's 19,854. An ablation study confirms that neither symbolic-only (hReason: 2,238/0 Kangaroo success), hierarchical-neural-only (hPPO: 10,601/0), nor extended-input PPO (exPPO: 14,247/0) can replicate H²RL's performance — the logic-informed pretraining itself is the critical factor. Strengths: - Elegantly eliminates the inference-time cost of symbolic reasoning by distilling logical priors into neural weights during pretraining, achieving both symbolic coherence and neural speed at deployment. - Demonstrated as a universal pretraining substrate compatible with on-policy (PPO, C51) and off-policy (DQN) algorithms, with consistent improvements across all. - Extends effectively to continuous action spaces (CALE), showing the approach is not limited to discrete domains — a key limitation of many prior neuro-symbolic RL methods. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Requires domain-specific hand-crafted logical rules and pretrained option workers, which may be costly to design for novel or complex environments without domain expertise. - Performance gains on simpler environments (Seaquest) are modest or absent, suggesting the approach is most beneficial specifically in settings with deceptive dense rewards and strong long-horizon dependencies — limiting generality. - The logic manager and options are noted to be potentially suboptimal (trained with HackAtari modifications), and badly designed logical rules could cause rigid, hard-to-override flawed behavior, as noted in the broader impact statement.
