#29 — Innovative (71)

Sparse-BitNet: 1.58-bit LLMs are Naturally Friendly to Semi-Structured Sparsity

Di Zhang, Xun Wu, Shaohan Huang, Yudong Wang, Hanyong Shao, Yingbo Hao, Zewen Chi, Li Dong, Ting Song, Yan Xia, Zhifang Sui, Furu Wei · arxiv.org · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: Sparse-BitNet provides a genuinely interesting finding — that 1.58-bit ternary models exhibit a 'delayed collapse' under increasing sparsity compared to full-precision models — backed by a clean mechanistic explanation rooted in weight polarization and magnitude stratification. The Dual STE design enabling gradient flow through both quantization and masking operations is a well-motivated technical contribution with rigorous ablation support. However, the absolute quality gap to dense BF16 remains substantial, and the requirement to train from scratch limits practical applicability.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Large language models face growing computational costs at training and inference time. Two leading efficiency techniques—semi-structured N:M sparsity (e.g., the 2:4 pattern supported by NVIDIA Sparse Tensor Cores) and extreme low-bit quantization (e.g., 1.58-bit BitNet with ternary weights {-1, 0, +1})—have been studied almost exclusively in isolation. Prior work applying N:M sparsity to full-precision (BF16) models finds that accuracy collapses rapidly under strict sparsity constraints, limiting practical deployment. This paper asks whether 1.58-bit BitNet is inherently more compatible with N:M sparsity than full-precision models, motivated by the observation that ~42% of quantized BitNet weights are already zero, forming a natural sparse structure. Methodology: The paper proposes Sparse-BitNet, a unified training framework that jointly enforces 1.58-bit ternary weight quantization and N:M semi-structured sparsity from scratch. The core building block is the Sparse-BitLinear layer, which follows a "quant-and-mask" paradigm: (1) a binary N:M sparsity mask is computed each training step from the continuous full-precision master weights using magnitude-based Top-N selection per M-element block; (2) ternary quantization is applied to the masked weights for the forward pass, producing effective sparse-quantized weights W_eff = W_q ⊙ M_{N:M}. Gradients flow through both the quantization and masking operations via a Dual Straight-Through Estimator (STE): gradients pass to all master weights including masked ones ("dense gradient flow"), preventing premature freezing of pruned weights. Critically, masks are derived from continuous master weights rather than discrete ternary values, avoiding tie-breaking instabilities. The main experiments use 6:8 sparsity (25% sparsity) on Qwen2.5 models at 0.5B, 1.5B, and 3B scales, trained on 50B tokens of RefinedWeb. Ablations compare four training variants and sweep N:8 patterns from dense (8:8) to aggressive (2:8). Key Results: Sparse-BitNet consistently incurs smaller performance degradation than sparse BF16 under identical 6:8 sparsity constraints. On perplexity, enforcing 6:8 sparsity increases PPL by only +0.32/+0.24/+0.17 for BitNet at 0.5B/1.5B/3B, versus +1.20/+0.60/+0.45 for BF16. On five downstream benchmarks (HellaSwag, ARC-E, PIQA, BoolQ, COPA), the average accuracy drop is 1.15/3.79/0.80 points for BitNet versus 3.02/7.71/3.20 for BF16. In a sparsity sweep from 8:8 to 2:8, BF16 crosses a 10% normalized PPL degradation threshold at 4:8 sparsity (+18.8%), while BitNet stays below it at 4:8 (+5.7%) and only crosses at 3:8, demonstrating a "delayed collapse" property. End-to-end throughput on a custom 6:8 sparse kernel reaches up to 1.30× speedup on NVIDIA A100 (prefill) and 1.18× on B200 (decode) for the 3B model. Ablations show that blocking gradients to masked weights or computing masks from quantized ternary weights (rather than continuous master weights) both severely degrade convergence, and that dense-to-sparse training schedules (switching late to sparsity) consistently underperform sparse-from-scratch training. Strengths: - Provides a rigorous, controlled comparative analysis of sparsity-friendliness between quantized and full-precision LLMs, with a clear mechanistic explanation (weight polarization and magnitude stratification decoupling pruning thresholds from active weights). - The Dual STE design choice—allowing gradients to flow through masked weights—is well-motivated and empirically validated against ablation baselines, directly connecting theory (projected optimization, mask flip rate dynamics) to practice. - Demonstrates practical hardware speedups (up to 1.30×) via a custom 6:8 kernel, making the efficiency claims concrete rather than theoretical. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Experiments are limited to the Qwen2.5 architecture family at scales up to 3B parameters; it is unclear whether the sparsity-friendliness advantage of BitNet generalizes to larger models (e.g., 7B, 70B) or other architectures. - The absolute accuracy of Sparse-BitNet remains below that of Dense BF16 (e.g., 57.96 vs. 63.38 average accuracy at 3B), so the method expands the efficiency Pareto frontier but does not close the quality gap to full-precision dense models. - Training from scratch under joint quantization and sparsity constraints requires a full pretraining budget; the paper shows that post-hoc dense-to-sparse conversion degrades quality, limiting applicability to settings where retraining from scratch is feasible.

#30 — Innovative (71)

Graph-of-Mark: Promote Spatial Reasoning in Multimodal Language Models with Graph-Based Visual Prompting

Giacomo Frisoni, Lorenzo Molfetta, Mattia Buzzoni, Gianluca Moro · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: Graph-of-Mark introduces a genuinely novel idea—embedding scene graphs directly as pixel-level visual marks on input images—that bridges a clear gap between isolated object annotations and relational spatial reasoning. The training-free, plug-and-play nature and consistent gains across heterogeneous MLM architectures are compelling. However, the approach is largely an integration of existing components (OWL-V2, SAM-HQ, MiDaS, FastText) into a pipeline, and the scalability ceiling at 10+ entities limits real-world applicability.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Multimodal language models (MLMs) consistently struggle with spatial reasoning — distinguishing concepts like left/right, above/below, in front of/behind — because their architectures prioritize global image-level understanding over explicit spatial composition. Existing pixel-level visual prompting methods like Set-of-Mark (SoM) annotate detected objects with numeric IDs but treat them as isolated entities, ignoring the relational structure that governs scenes. This leaves a rich modeling layer untapped: the pairwise spatial relationships between objects that are essential for visual question answering, referring expression comprehension, and real-world applications like robotic manipulation, autonomous navigation, and medical imaging. Methodology: Graph-of-Mark (GoM) is a training-free, plug-and-play pixel-level visual prompting technique that overlays scene graphs directly onto the input image before feeding it to an MLM. The pipeline has five stages. First, an ensemble of three complementary object detectors (OWL-V2, YOLOv8-X, Mask R-CNN) is run and merged via Weighted Boxes Fusion, then refined into pixel-accurate masks using SAM-HQ. Second, pairwise spatial relations are computed for all object pairs across three categories: directional (above, below, left_of, right_of), depth-based (in_front_of, behind) via MiDaS monocular depth estimation, and proximity-based (near), with optional closeness modifiers (touching, very_close, close). Third, a two-step query-driven filtering pipeline prunes the graph to only objects and relations relevant to the task prompt, using lexical matching and FastText cosine similarity. Fourth, the filtered graph is rendered onto the image: object regions receive class-colored semi-transparent masks with ID labels, while relations are drawn as directed head-to-tail arrows with optional textual labels. A collision-free placement algorithm handles overlapping marks. Finally, the augmented image I^SG (and optionally a verbalized textual scene graph T^SG) is passed to the frozen MLM. Two prompting modes are evaluated: Visual SG only, and Visual + Textual SG combined. Key Results: GoM is evaluated on 3 open-source MLMs (Qwen2.5-VL-7B, Gemma-3-4B, LlamaV-o1-11B) across 4 datasets (GQA, VQAv1, VQAv2, RefCOCOg). GoM consistently outperforms both raw image baselines and SoM across all model/dataset combinations. The best GoM variant achieves 83.6% on GQA with LlamaV-o1 (+11 pp over raw image, +10.7 pp over SoM), 82.5% on VQAv1 and 80.5% on VQAv2 with Qwen2.5-VL, and 57.5% on RefCOCOg with Qwen2.5-VL. Ablations show that graph density matters: performance peaks with 3–10 entities and 4–16 relations, with surplus annotations introducing noise beyond this range. Visual SG representations consistently outperform purely textual SG encodings (up to +10 pp), and combining both modalities yields additional modest gains. Computationally, GoM adds only ~1.13 seconds per image compared to 0.77s for segmentation-only and 0.92s for SoM, with the overhead attributable to relation estimation. Textual object IDs (e.g., chair_2) are preferable for VQA tasks, while numeric IDs are better for REC tasks. Strengths: - First method to embed scene graphs directly as pixel-level visual marks, enabling MLMs to leverage relational structure without any fine-tuning or architectural changes. - Broadly applicable: works across heterogeneous MLM architectures and scales (4B–11B parameters), demonstrating consistent gains on both VQA and referring expression tasks. - Computationally lightweight: adds only ~0.21s overhead over SoM and requires no ground-truth annotations, as object detectors and relation estimators are fully automatic and open-vocabulary. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Performance degrades for dense scenes (>10 entities, >16 relations) as surplus annotations introduce visual noise, suggesting scalability challenges for cluttered real-world images. - No single GoM variant is universally optimal: textual IDs favor VQA while numeric IDs favor REC, requiring task-aware configuration; and the method shows model-dependent sensitivity (Qwen exhibits adverse sensitivity to SoM-like configurations).

#31 — Innovative (71)

Differential Privacy in Two-Layer Networks: How DP-SGD Harms Fairness and Robustness

Ruichen Xu, Kexin Chen · arxiv.org · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: Scores 71 for providing the first unified theoretical framework explaining three distinct DP-SGD side effects through a single metric (feature-to-noise ratio) without requiring convexity or smoothness assumptions. The novel proof techniques for non-smooth ReLU networks under DP noise represent genuine theoretical craftsmanship. The restriction to two-layer CNNs on stylized data and modest empirical mitigations (~1.6% gain) temper the practical impact.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Differentially private stochastic gradient descent (DP-SGD) is the standard algorithm for training machine learning models on sensitive data with privacy guarantees. However, empirical studies consistently show that DP-SGD degrades model performance, introduces fairness issues (disparate impact across classes and demographic groups), and reduces adversarial robustness. The theoretical underpinnings of these phenomena in modern, non-convex neural networks remain largely unexplored. Prior theoretical analyses typically rely on assumptions such as convexity or Lipschitz-smoothness that do not extend to ReLU networks, leaving a significant gap in the understanding of why and how DP-SGD causes these side effects. Methodology: The paper introduces a unified feature-centric framework to analyze the feature learning dynamics of DP-SGD in two-layer ReLU CNNs. The model assumes a structured binary classification setting where each labeled example consists of two patches: one containing class-relevant majority or minority features, and one containing Gaussian noise. The key technical innovation is the introduction of the feature-to-noise ratio (FNR), defined as F_{i,j} = ||u_{i,j}||_2 / σ_n, which measures how strongly a class/group's features dominate the DP noise. Three central challenges are overcome: (1) non-smoothness of ReLU is handled by tracking model output increments rather than weight monotonicity; (2) randomness from DP-SGD noise is addressed by bounding model output increments probabilistically; and (3) non-linearity of cross-entropy and softmax is handled by approximating with piecewise linear functions. This yields tight upper and lower bounds on test loss (Theorems 3.4 and 3.5), adversarial test loss (Theorem 3.7), and private fine-tuning test loss (Proposition 4.4), all governed by the FNR. Three mitigation strategies are also proposed: stage-wise network freezing/pruning, data augmentation to amplify FNR, and careful pretraining dataset selection. Key Results: The theoretical framework yields three main explanations for DP-SGD side effects, all controlled by the FNR: (1) Disparate impact arises from imbalanced FNRs — classes or groups with weaker features (smaller ||u_{i,j}||_2) or smaller data proportions suffer disproportionately higher test loss, with the most misclassified samples being those with long-tailed or weak features; (2) Reduced adversarial robustness occurs because DP noise causes the model to learn non-robust, class-irrelevant noise features, increasing adversarial vulnerability at a rate of O(√T) with training iterations; (3) Public pretraining does not guarantee improvement when the feature distribution shift between pretraining and fine-tuning datasets is large (parametrized by rotation angle θ), with test loss provably degrading as θ increases. Experiments on synthetic data (n=450, d=100) confirm that test loss increases with DP noise σ_n and decreases with feature size. On MNIST with ε=3 privacy budget, adversarial accuracy drops from 95% to 20% as image padding ratio increases from 0% to 75% (effectively reducing FNR), and CIFAR-10 adversarial accuracy collapses from 3% to 0%. For CIFAR-10, model accuracy under public-pretraining fine-tuning degrades from 70% to 49% as rotation angle increases from 0° to 67.5°. Stage-wise network freezing improves LeNet accuracy on MNIST from 95.11% to 96.71%. Strengths: - Provides the first unified theoretical framework explaining three distinct DP-SGD side effects (disparate impact, adversarial vulnerability, pretraining limitations) under a single metric (FNR), without requiring convexity or smoothness assumptions. - Develops novel proof techniques specifically for non-smooth ReLU networks under DP noise, including piecewise linear approximation of cross-entropy and output-increment-based tracking of feature learning dynamics. - Theoretical predictions are well-supported by controlled synthetic experiments and real-world MNIST/CIFAR-10 results, with tight qualitative agreement. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Analysis is restricted to two-layer ReLU CNNs on a stylized, structured data distribution; the extent to which results generalize to deeper networks, transformers, or unstructured real-world data is not formally established. - The structured data model (orthogonal feature vectors, fixed majority/minority patch structure) is a significant abstraction that may not capture the complexity of real datasets, limiting the quantitative applicability of the bounds. - The proposed mitigation (stage-wise freezing) yields only a modest empirical gain (~1.6% on MNIST) and the theoretical guarantees for the mitigation strategies are deferred or absent.

#32 — Innovative (71)

Timer-S1: A Billion-Scale Time Series Foundation Model with Serial Scaling

Yong Liu, Xingjian Su, Shiyu Wang, Haoran Zhang, Haixuan Liu, Yuxuan Wang, Zhou Ye, Yang Xiang, Jianmin Wang, Mingsheng Long · arxiv.org · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: Timer-S1 introduces Serial-Token Prediction (STP), a principled middle ground between next-token prediction and full autoregressive rollout that captures serial dependencies in a single forward pass. The multi-stage training pipeline and scaling to 8.3B parameters with consistent gains are well-executed. However, the core building blocks (sparse MoE, patch-based tokenization, RoPE) are established techniques recombined rather than fundamentally new, and the restriction to univariate forecasting limits practical novelty.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Prior time series foundation models have hit a scalability bottleneck: parallel forecasting models predict all future steps simultaneously, missing the inherently serial nature of forecasting (where long-horizon accuracy depends on step-by-step reasoning), while autoregressive models correctly mirror this serial nature but suffer from expensive rolling inference and severe error accumulation over long horizons. Existing attempts to adapt Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) architectures from LLMs to time series have yielded limited gains. The field lacks a billion-scale time series foundation model that can efficiently incorporate serial computations without the overhead of rolling autoregression. Methodology: Timer-S1 is an 8.3B-parameter sparse MoE model (0.75B activated per token) with a context length of 11,520, built around three axes of "Serial Scaling": architecture, data, and training pipeline. Architecturally, Timer-S1 combines a decoder-only Transformer backbone with two specialized block types: TimeMoE blocks (sparse MoE with E=32 experts, K=2 activated, using QK-Norm and RoPE for causal self-attention) for contextual representation, and TimeSTP blocks for Serial-Token Prediction (STP). Each TimeSTP block concatenates token embeddings from the preceding block with the original input patch embeddings via a linear projection, then passes them through an internal TimeMoE block, producing shifted-by-one patch predictions. A sequence of H=16 TimeSTP blocks generates multi-horizon predictions in a single forward pass without rolling. A shared quantile forecasting head outputs 9 quantiles (0.1–0.9) per patch. For data, the authors curate TimeBench, a corpus of over one trillion time points spanning finance, IoT, climate, ECG, energy, and synthetic signals, with rigorous preprocessing (causal mean imputation, k-σ/IQR outlier removal) and augmentation via resampling (Fourier-based downsampling/interpolation) and value-flipping to mitigate predictive bias. The training pipeline is multi-stage: pre-training with uniform STP + NTP objectives on TimeBench; continued pre-training (CPT) with a horizon-decay weighted STP objective (weight 1/√j for the j-th TimeSTP block) to improve short-term accuracy; and long-context extension (LCE) via RoPE scaling from context length 2,880 to 11,520. Key Results: On the GIFT-Eval benchmark (24 datasets, 144K time series, 177M data points), Timer-S1 achieves state-of-the-art MASE of 0.693 and CRPS of 0.485 as a pre-trained zero-shot model, outperforming all other foundation models including Chronos-2 (MASE 0.598, CRPS 0.485), TimesFM-2.5 (MASE 0.705, CRPS 0.490), Moirai2 (MASE 0.728, CRPS 0.516), and Sundial-Base (MASE 0.750, CRPS 0.559). Compared to its direct predecessor Timer-3 (Sundial), Timer-S1 achieves 7.6% lower MASE and 13.2% lower CRPS trained on the same TimeBench corpus. The gains are most pronounced on medium- and long-term forecasting horizons, validating the serial computation hypothesis. Ablation against training objective: Timer-S1 (24-MoE, 16-STP) achieves MASE 0.693/CRPS 0.485 vs. Timer-NTP (40-MoE) at MASE 0.733/CRPS 0.547, and Timer-MTP (40-MoE) at MASE 0.719/CRPS 0.506—showing STP is strictly better than both NTP and MTP under equal compute budgets. The post-training pipeline contributes meaningfully: PT alone gives MASE 0.728/CRPS 0.512, +CPT improves to 0.706/0.496, and +LCE further improves to 0.693/0.485. Data augmentation yields MASE 0.693 vs. 0.703 without it. Strengths: - The Serial-Token Prediction (STP) formulation is a principled and well-motivated departure from both NTP and MTP: it captures the serial dependency structure of forecasting without requiring rolling inference at test time, since TimeSTP blocks are retained and reused during inference to generate multi-step predictions in a single forward pass. - The multi-stage training pipeline (pre-training → continued pre-training with horizon-decay weighting → long-context extension) is well-designed and systematically validated, with each stage demonstrating measurable additive gains. - Thorough scaling analysis demonstrating consistent improvement up to 8.3B parameters (24 TimeMoE + 16 TimeSTP blocks), validating the scaling law for this architecture class and surpassing the parameter scale of all prior time series foundation models. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Timer-S1 does not natively incorporate exogenous covariates, a significant practical limitation acknowledged by the authors; the model relies solely on univariate historical context, forgoing cross-variate correlations that many real forecasting tasks depend on. - The fixed patch size P=16 introduces a periodic artifact: ablations show an RMSE spike at sinusoidal periods near 16 (matching the patch size), indicating the model is sensitive to signal periodicity alignment with patch boundaries—a known but unresolved structural fragility.

#33 — Innovative (71)

Fast and Optimal Differentially Private Frequent-Substring Mining

Peaker Guo, Rayne Holland, Hao Wu · arxiv.org · karypis - new results
Why this rank: Scores 71 for achieving a quadratic-to-near-linear complexity reduction (O(n²ℓ⁴) → O(nℓ log|Σ|)) while preserving asymptotically optimal DP error guarantees—a meaningful algorithmic advance enabled by the genuinely clever structural Lemma 4.4 that sidesteps pairwise candidate concatenation. However, the problem formulation and framework were established by prior work (Bernardini et al.), and the contribution is primarily an efficiency improvement within that existing paradigm rather than a new direction; the lack of any empirical evaluation also limits evidence of practical impact.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Given a dataset of n user-contributed strings each of length at most ℓ, the goal is to identify all frequent substrings while preserving each user's privacy under ε-differential privacy. This problem arises in NLP pipelines (autocomplete, next-word prediction), genomic data analysis, and transit data mining, where frequent substrings capture sensitive personal patterns. Prior work by Bernardini et al. (PODS'25) provided the first theoretically rigorous algorithm with near-optimal additive-error guarantees and a matching lower bound, but at a prohibitive O(n²ℓ⁴) time and space cost—infeasible for realistic datasets such as Reddit (n ≈ 10⁶, ℓ ≥ 3000). The central research question is whether near-linear time and space can be achieved while retaining asymptotically optimal error. Methodology: The paper presents a new ε-DP algorithm achieving O(nℓ log|Σ| + |Σ|) time and O(nℓ + |Σ|) space. The core bottleneck in prior work is its candidate generation: given frequent substrings C_k of length k, it exhaustively tests all |C_k|² concatenated pairs to find length-2k candidates, inducing quadratic blowup. The new algorithm avoids this via two innovations. First, a refined candidate-generation strategy exploits a structural lemma (Lemma 4.4): any τ_T-frequent substring of length k+t must have its length-k prefix in C_k and its length-t suffix must appear as a suffix of some string in C_k. This allows candidates to be enumerated by traversing a shared suffix trie T_k (built once per phase over character-aligned suffixes of C_k) concatenated with each s ∈ C_k, reducing per-phase cost to O(n·k) rather than O(|C_k|²·k). Second, frequency-guided pruning prunes entire subtrees when noisy counts fall below a threshold, eliminating infrequent branches early. Noisy frequency estimation uses the Binary Tree Mechanism applied to a heavy-light decomposition of T_k, enabling prefix-sum computation with additive error Õ(ℓ/ε) using only O(log d) noise per query. The algorithm preprocesses strings into a binary alphabet (length ℓ_bit = ℓ(⌈log|Σ|⌉+1)) and operates over ⌈log ℓ⌉ doubling phases, building sets C_r, C_{r+1}, ..., C_{ℓ_bit} inductively. Key Results: The algorithm is proved ε-differentially private and achieves the (τ_T, τ_⊥)-Inclusion-Exclusion Criterion with probability at least 1−β, where τ_T = 9·τ* and τ_⊥ ≥ ℓ(log ℓ_bit), for τ* = ε₀⁻¹·(log ℓ_bit)·ln(nℓ_bit/β) and ε₀ = (ε/log ℓ)/(4ℓ log(nℓ_bit)). Time complexity is O(nℓ_bit + |Σ|) = O(nℓ log|Σ| + |Σ|) vs. O(n²ℓ⁴ + |Σ|) for prior work—a quadratic-to-near-linear reduction. Space drops from O(n²ℓ⁴) to O(nℓ + |Σ|), making memory proportional to the dataset size. The frequency threshold τ_T is slightly higher than prior work by additional log factors attributable to the binary encoding, but for small alphabets (e.g., genomic Σ = {A,C,G,T}) these factors are negligible. The number of candidate strings at each phase is bounded by n/log ℓ_bit (Corollary 5.2), ensuring the pruned search remains efficient. Strengths: - Reduces both time and space from quadratic O(n²ℓ⁴) to near-linear O(nℓ log|Σ|), enabling applicability to realistic large-scale datasets where prior methods are completely infeasible. - Provides complete, rigorous theoretical guarantees for both privacy (ε-DP) and utility (near-optimal additive error), with formal proofs covering correctness, privacy composition via the Binary Tree mechanism, and asymptotic performance. - The structural Lemma 4.4 and reuse of suffix trie T_k across all s ∈ C_k is a genuinely novel insight that sidesteps pairwise concatenation—a key distinction from all prior work. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The frequency threshold τ_T incurs additional log|Σ| factors over prior work due to binary encoding, meaning the gap τ_T − τ_⊥ is slightly larger; this is a real (though often small) loss in utility for large alphabets. - The paper is entirely theoretical with no empirical evaluation on real datasets—there is no demonstration of practical runtime, utility, or comparison with prior implementations on benchmark corpora such as Reddit.

#34 — Innovative (71)

SkillTracer: Structural Failure Attribution and Refinement of Agentic Skills in Long-Horizon Web Tasks

openreview.net · karypis - new results
Why this rank: SkillTracer introduces a genuinely novel architectural choice by representing agent skills as editable attributed plan graphs rather than monolithic macros, enabling node-level failure localization through deterministic programmatic verification. The structural conservative objective for skill refinement is a creative formalization that prevents catastrophic forgetting while enabling targeted repair. However, the verification predicates are limited to two simple checks, and the core ideas build naturally on prior skill-induction and plan-repair literature rather than representing a paradigm shift.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Long-horizon web agents frequently fail without being able to pinpoint where or why execution broke down. Existing skill-induction approaches represent skills as monolithic program macros (e.g., Python functions), making their internal decision structure opaque during execution. When a skill fails due to UI drift or grounding errors, the agent only observes a terminal failure signal without access to intermediate states, making precise failure attribution impossible. Reflection-based methods operate on flattened execution traces that discard internal decision boundaries, causing "attribution ambiguity" — the inability to align negative outcomes with the specific internal decisions that produced them. This prevents localized, targeted repair and forces episodic retries rather than systematic refinement. Methodology: SkillTracer reimagines skills as editable attributed plan graphs rather than atomic macros. Each skill is represented as a directed attributed graph G = (V, E), where nodes encode typed atomic interaction decisions (each node carrying interaction intent τ, UI component type γ, executable operation α, and semantic description δ), and edges encode verifiable preconditions. During execution, SkillTracer performs programmatic verification at each node via two deterministic predicates: Component Availability (checking that the required UI component type exists in the current AXTree state) and Anchor Text Presence (confirming a synthesized textual anchor exists on the page). This binary verification yields precise failure localization to the specific plan node where execution deviated. Upon failure, a Diagnostic Reasoning Module synthesizes three evidence signals — transition evidence (DOM state changes), verification evidence (edge predicate outcomes), and memory evidence (immediate interaction history) — to derive a structural repair operator Ω targeting a specific node attribute at one of four granularity levels: intent/stage, component type, executable action, or semantic description. A dual-layer memory system maintains short-term memory (failure-repair tuples within an episode, preventing cyclic repairs) and long-term memory (successful repairs consolidated across episodes). Skills are stored as parameterized structural templates with symbolic placeholders, retrieved via RAG (top-k=3) for new subgoals, and updated through a structural conservative objective that minimizes failure risk while penalizing structural edit distance from the original plan. Key Results: Evaluated on the WebArena benchmark (812 tasks across Shopping, Admin, Reddit, GitLab, Map, and Cross-domain settings) using GPT-4o as the backbone, SkillTracer achieves an average success rate of 57.8% — improvements of 43.1%, 94.0%, and 62.8% over ASI, SkillWeaver, and AWM respectively (all skill-induction baselines using comparable LLM backbones). In the cross-domain setting, SkillTracer achieves 32.5% vs. the strongest baseline at 20.8% (a 56.3% relative gain). Ablations confirm the contribution of each component: removing skill reuse drops Shopping success from 65.7% to 45.16% and increases token cost by 31.7%; removing failure localization and repair drops Shopping success from 65.7% to 44.4%. Skill induction token cost is reduced 66.5% compared to ASI (1,932 vs. 5,773 tokens), owing to subgoal-triggered induction rather than full-horizon extraction. Strengths: - The plan-graph representation provides genuine white-box interpretability of skill execution, enabling failure localization to specific nodes rather than entire trajectories — a principled architectural advance over monolithic macro approaches. - The programmatic verification mechanism produces deterministic, binary failure signals (rather than heuristic LLM critiques), making repair attribution reliable and reproducible across heterogeneous web environments. - The structural conservative objective for skill refinement formalizes a non-monotonic learning paradigm that preserves verified substructures, enabling partial skill reuse and preventing catastrophic forgetting of valid interaction logic. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The verification predicates are restricted to two simple checks (component availability and anchor text presence), which may miss more subtle failure modes such as semantic mismatches in content or ordering errors that don't manifest as missing UI elements. - SkillTracer incurs higher per-task token costs than simpler baselines (e.g., 82,370 vs. 59,613 tokens on Shopping vs. ASI), and the maximum episode length is capped at 10 steps, which may limit applicability to tasks requiring longer interaction horizons than those in WebArena.

#35 — Innovative (71)

Pancake: Hierarchical Memory System for Multi-Agent LLM Serving

Zhengding Hu, Zaifeng Pan, Prabhleen Kaur, Vibha Murthy, Zhongkai Yu, Yue Guan, Zhen Wang, Steven Swanson, Yufei Ding · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: Pancake addresses a genuinely underserved systems problem—dynamic ANN search for agentic memory—with a coherent multi-tier design. The FSM-based workload modeling for cache prefetching and the hybrid graph unifying multi-agent coarse indexes are creative contributions that go beyond straightforward engineering optimization. The 4.29× average throughput improvement with sub-10% degradation at 20 agents demonstrates strong practical impact.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: As LLM-based agents increasingly rely on external memory to maintain context, action history, and knowledge across multi-turn interactions, the performance of approximate nearest neighbor (ANN) search over these memory stores becomes a critical bottleneck. Existing vector database frameworks (e.g., LlamaIndex, LangChain, Mem-GPT, A-Mem) are designed for static RAG-style workloads and fail to handle the highly dynamic, fine-grained update patterns characteristic of agentic memory. Two specific failure modes are identified: (1) for single agents, frequent in-place vector insertions scatter semantically related items across many clusters due to the high-dimensional shell effect, degrading both search efficiency and recall; (2) for multi-agent workloads, maintaining independent per-agent indexes causes coarse-search overhead to scale near-linearly with agent count, reaching 80% of total latency at 20 agents. Existing hybrid CPU-GPU approaches further fail to handle frequent insertions efficiently, since GPU-cached clusters cannot flexibly support concurrent dynamic updates. Methodology: Pancake is a multi-tier ANN memory management system built around three coordinated techniques. First, a three-level index cache (L0/L1/L2) exploits agent-specific access patterns modeled as Finite-State Machines (FSMs). L0 maintains a small pattern table of the most recently accessed vectors; L1 caches top-k' neighbors around frequently accessed clusters; L2 holds stable coarse-grained clusters. FSM-based pattern matching enables workload-aware search reordering and prefetching, with early termination triggered when all top-k candidates at the current cache level fall within a threshold distance (α_et · d_agent). Second, for multi-agent workloads, Pancake constructs a hybrid graph that unifies all agents' coarse indexes into a single traversable structure, enabling cross-scope coarse search in one graph traversal rather than one per agent. Per-cluster agent profiles (sorted lists of recently accessed vector IDs) further guide fine-search reordering to reduce irrelevant computation. Third, a GPU-CPU coordinated index management scheme uses hotspot-aware GPU caching for frequently accessed clusters, a CPU-side insertion buffer (size B_insert=128 vectors) to absorb frequent small-batch insertions, asynchronous CPU-to-GPU data migration to avoid transfer latency, and GPU-accelerated K-means for on-GPU cluster splitting. The system exposes a Python interface compatible with Mem-GPT, LangChain, and LlamaIndex. Key Results: Evaluated on a CPU-GPU server with AMD EPYC 9534 and 8× NVIDIA H100 GPUs across six agent datasets (AgentGym, Gsm8k, Prm800k, UltraChat, UltraFDBK, APIGen) and four workload access patterns (One-Search-One-Insert, Search-Then-Step-Insert, Step-Search-Then-Insert, Search-Only), Pancake achieves: end-to-end throughput improvements of 1.12× to 26.18× over existing memory frameworks (A-Mem, Mem-GPT, LlamaIndex, LangMem), with an average speedup exceeding 4.29×. Memory operation time is reduced to an average of 3.2% of total execution time. Against standalone vector database libraries (SpFresh, Quake, DiskANN), Pancake achieves 1.9× to 4.2× query throughput improvements, with an additional 2.2× gain from GPU acceleration. In multi-agent settings with up to 20 concurrent agents, end-to-end throughput degradation remains below 10.2%, compared to 29.9%–55.9% degradation in existing frameworks. The hybrid graph construction reduces coarse search computation by up to 11.6%, and agent profiles provide an additional 21.8% reduction in average computation cost. GPU caching achieves up to 1.92× speedup over CPU baseline with only 5–15 GB of GPU memory, and the multi-level cache reduces latency by up to 2.23× compared to IVF-Static over long-serving workloads. Strengths: - Addresses a well-motivated and practical systems problem (agentic memory at scale) with a coherent multi-component solution that is validated across diverse realistic workloads and agent types. - The FSM-based workload modeling is a lightweight yet effective mechanism for capturing agent-specific access patterns online without requiring pre-labeled semantic clusters or expensive dimensionality reduction. - The hybrid graph design for multi-agent coarse search achieves near-linear scalability improvements (>20× reduction in coarse search cost) with controlled inter-graph connection density, demonstrating strong engineering rigor. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The system's benefits depend heavily on workloads with sufficient temporal locality; datasets with broader, less structured access patterns (e.g., APIGen, AgentGym) show proportionally lower gains because broader dataset coverage increases graph traversal cost. - The FSM construction and merging heuristics are empirically tuned (e.g., α_et = 0.6–0.8, α_ic = 4–8), and the paper does not provide theoretical guarantees on FSM convergence quality or the impact of pattern mismatch on recall under distribution shift. - GPU memory contention with LLM model weights and KV cache is noted as a real constraint, and the evaluation hardware (dedicated H100s) may not reflect memory-constrained co-located deployment scenarios accurately.

#36 — Innovative (69)

MoE-SpAc: Efficient MoE Inference Based on Speculative Activation Utility in Heterogeneous Edge Scenarios

Shuhuai Li, Jianghao Lin, Dongdong Ge, Yinyu Ye · arxiv.org · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: MoE-SpAc introduces a genuine paradigm shift by repurposing speculative decoding's verification window as a lookahead sensor for memory management rather than merely a compute accelerator. The theoretical grounding—showing SD enriches expert activation signals with √(γ+1)× better SNR—is compelling, and the unified utility metric governing both prefetching and eviction elegantly eliminates cache-thrashing from decoupled systems. The O(1) convex threshold computation enables real-time per-layer adaptation. Limited to single-GPU batch-size-1 evaluation, which constrains demonstrated practical impact.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) large language models offer parameter efficiency but impose severe memory constraints on edge devices, where the full expert set cannot reside in GPU VRAM. Existing heterogeneous offloading strategies suffer from I/O bottlenecks caused by the dynamic, low-information nature of autoregressive (AR) expert activation signals—each step produces only a binary {0,1} indicator per expert, making reliable prefetching nearly impossible. Prior prefetching systems (MoE-Infinity, SP-MoE, HybriMoE) either rely on noisy AR signals or decouple their prefetching and caching policies, leading to suboptimal load balancing and synchronization overhead. Methodology: MoE-SpAc repurposes Speculative Decoding (SD) from a mere compute accelerator into an informative *lookahead sensor* for memory management. The framework has three integrated components. First, a Speculative Utility Estimator tracks per-expert demand using an *activation frequency map* derived from the SD verification window (γ draft tokens), which transforms binary AR signals into richer frequency-valued signals in [0, γ+1]. Utility scores are maintained in a discrete space [0, K] via an *Inertial Utility Transition* mechanism (scores increment/decrement only when frequency fluctuation exceeds adaptive thresholds) and an *Adaptive Boundary Calibration* that adjusts thresholds via exponential moving average. Second, a Heterogeneous Workload Balancer solves a per-layer online integer optimization problem at each verification step to find a global threshold τ* partitioning experts into "hot" (GPU-resident) and "cold" (CPU-executed). The objective minimizes |T_cpu − T_gpu| subject to I/O bandwidth and VRAM constraints; because the objective is convex over integer τ, the optimum is found in O(1) time. Third, an Asynchronous Execution Engine implements prefetching and eviction under the same unified utility metric using a multi-level priority queue (prefetcher) and a Red-Black tree sorted pool (evictor), overlapping I/O with the SD drafting phase to hide transfer latency. The system is built atop llama.cpp via a custom ids_tensor modification enabling seamless CPU/GPU expert dispatch. Key Results: Evaluated on seven benchmarks (MMLU-Pro, MT-bench, HumanEval, GSM8K, Alpaca, CNN/DailyMail, QA) with Qwen3-30B-A3B as target and Qwen3-4B-FP8 as draft model on a single RTX 4090 (24GB) connected via PCIe 4.0: MoE-SpAc achieves an average 4.04× speedup in TPS over all baselines (Accelerate, vLLM, llama.cpp, Mixtral Offloading, MoE-Infinity, SP-MoE, Fate, HybriMoE). Against the best SD-based baseline (llama.cpp-w/SD), it achieves a 41.9% improvement in average TPS (25.80 vs ~18.17 TPS). On a second model (DeepSeek-V2-Lite with int4 draft), MoE-SpAc outperforms the best baselines by 52.9% in TPS and 45.6% in latency. Ablation confirms that removing the Speculative Utility Estimator causes the largest single degradation, followed by removal of the Heterogeneous Workload Balancer and the prefetcher/evictor pair. The utility estimator achieves hot-or-cold prediction accuracy of ~0.85 on MMLU-Pro. MoE-SpAc also operates stably at expert cache ratios as low as 17% GPU VRAM, outperforming baselines that use 25% cache, demonstrating the value of allocating ~8% VRAM to the draft model. Strengths: - Theoretically grounded paradigm shift: SD's verification window provably enriches expert activation signals (higher entropy, √(γ+1)× better SNR than AR), and bounded-drift Lipschitz analysis justifies inertial utility estimation. - Unified scheduling objective: a single utility metric governs both prefetching and eviction, eliminating the cache-thrashing and synchronization overhead that plagues decoupled systems. - O(1) optimal threshold computation via convexity of the load-balancing objective enables real-time per-layer, per-step adaptation to dynamic I/O and memory constraints. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The draft model (Qwen3-4B) consumes ~8% GPU VRAM, reducing the expert cache budget; the paper acknowledges an OOM boundary at 21% cache ratio in experiments, though MoE-SpAc still outperforms baselines at 25%. - Evaluation is limited to a single GPU (RTX 4090) in a single-request, batch-size-1 setting; generalization to multi-GPU or multi-request serving scenarios is not demonstrated and left as future work. - The framework's benefit is contingent on SD acceptance rate α being high enough for expert reuse to amortize draft model overhead; domain mismatches between draft and target models could reduce effectiveness.

#37 — Innovative (69)

Super Research: Answering Highly Complex Questions with Large Language Models through Super Deep and Super Wide Research

Yubo Dong, Nianhao You, Yuxuan Hou, Zixun Sun, Yue Zhang, Liang Zhang, Siyuan Zhao, Hehe Fan · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: Super Research's Research Graph as structured ground truth is a meaningful methodological innovation for evaluating long-form research synthesis, demonstrating 3-6× higher responsiveness to quality perturbations than LLM-as-a-Judge. The identification and quantification of distinct failure modes (Complexity Trap, Spatial Overload) provides actionable diagnostic insights. However, the benchmark's reliance on expensive expert construction limits scalability.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Existing LLM research paradigms — Deep Research (high depth, narrow scope) and Wide Search (broad coverage, shallow synthesis) — are fundamentally inadequate for "super-complex" questions found in professional intelligence, scientific discovery, and strategic planning. These questions require simultaneously reconciling 1,000+ web pages across heterogeneous sources while maintaining rigorous multi-hop reasoning chains. Current benchmarks fail to capture this challenge: LLM-as-a-Judge metrics are alignment-prone, shallow fact-recall metrics (e.g., FActScore) reward atomic retrieval over synthesis, and human Elo systems are prohibitively expensive. No existing framework measures logical coherence, bias calibration, or source diversity in long-form research reports. Methodology: The paper introduces SuperResearch, a benchmark and evaluation framework with three core pillars. First, a Structured Decomposition stage where a Planner agent breaks complex queries into MECE (Mutually Exclusive, Collectively Exhaustive) DAG-structured research plans. Second, Super Wide Retrieval using multi-query expansion and aspect-based retrieval for thematic coverage. Third, Super Deep Investigation via iterative follow-up queries that resolve uncertainties through Researcher and Summarizer agents operating in a dynamic memory loop. The benchmark itself consists of 300 expert-vetted questions across 10 domains, requiring an average of 97 depth reasoning steps and 697 retrieval pages (up to 140 steps and 1,200 pages maximum). Ground truth is established through a four-phase pipeline: hierarchical task decomposition and retrieval, collaborative Research Graph construction (transforming unstructured sub-reports into structured Fact→Insight→Global Conclusion hierarchies with human-in-the-loop validation), report synthesis via a Writer agent, and automated QA construction including "Dialectical Confidence Audit" questions for bias calibration. Evaluation spans five dimensions: Coverage & Comprehension (Depth-Weighted Recall R_weighted assigning topological weights to graph nodes), Logical Consistency (C_logic = product of valid inference ratio × graph coverage, penalizing hallucinated conclusions), Report Utility (U_qa via closed-context reading comprehension), Objectivity Score (O_bias measuring stance calibration against ground-truth thesis/antithesis support levels), and Citation Health (Source Dominance D_src and Narrative Monopolization M_mono). The graph-anchored evaluation is compared against LLM-as-a-Judge through controlled perturbation experiments measuring Responsiveness Rate. Key Results: All 12 evaluated systems score below 29 overall, confirming super-complex questions remain a largely unsolved frontier. Gemini Deep Research leads with 28.62 overall (Coverage 33.15, Consistency 22.92, Utility 22.98, Objectivity 35.43). Sonar Deep Research scores 27.04 second overall. Surprisingly, Native Search-Integrated agents (Kimi-k2: 26.16, Grok-4-1-fast: 24.55) outperform specialized Deep Research systems like o3 (22.04) and o4-mini (21.91). Search-Augmented Baselines using LangGraph+Tavily cluster in the 16–23 range. A critical finding is the Reasoning Bottleneck: limited retrieval coverage (R_weighted < 16) imposes a ceiling on logical consistency (C_logic < 14), yet even high-coverage systems like Gemini (R_weighted ≈ 33.15) achieve only C_logic ≈ 22.92, indicating retrieval breadth alone is insufficient for deep synthesis. The graph-based evaluator demonstrates significantly higher responsiveness (57.4–79.6%) to controlled quality perturbations versus LLM Judge (14.8–22.2%), and 6× lower variance on Consistency (0.83% vs 4.69% normalized STD) and 65× lower variance on Objectivity (1.07% vs 7.82%). Strengths: - The Research Graph as ground truth is a principled innovation — it enables verifiable, structure-anchored evaluation that surpasses LLM-as-a-Judge in both responsiveness and cross-model consistency, with rigorous mathematical formulations for all five metrics. - The benchmark construction methodology (Cognitive-Rank Constrained Expert Simulation + human Ph.D. vetting) produces genuinely hard tasks resistant to surface-level keyword search, with three enforced complexity principles (Frontier-Level Specificity, Search-Intensive Complexity, Professional Precision). - The identification and quantification of distinct failure modes — Complexity Trap (high retrieval volume failing to produce logical depth), Single-Source Regression, Spatial Overload illusion of diversity, and Defensive Summarization Paradox — provides actionable diagnostic insights for future system design. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The benchmark is frozen at 300 tasks and requires substantial human expert effort (Ph.D. candidates, senior practitioners) for construction and graph validation, making it difficult to extend or maintain as models rapidly improve. - The graph construction process involves LLM-driven claim verification and subgraph extraction, introducing potential systematic biases in what gets labeled as a valid "Fact" versus "Insight" node, and the semantic equivalence judgments in Coverage recall remain dependent on an LLM judge despite the framework's goal of reducing such dependence. - Computational costs are prohibitive: Gemini Deep Research averages 544 seconds per task with 34 web searches and 71 unique URLs, making comprehensive evaluation across all 300 tasks extremely expensive, and these latency/cost tradeoffs are not systematically analyzed in the main results.

#38 — Innovative (68)

PromptTuner: SLO-Aware Elastic System for LLM Prompt Tuning

Wei Gao, Peng Sun, Dmitrii Ustiugov, Tianwei Zhang, Yonggang Wen · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: PromptTuner earns credit for identifying and characterizing a genuinely novel workload class (LPT-as-a-Service) with distinct properties — particularly the ITA sensitivity to initial prompts, which motivates the Prompt Bank. The two-layer K-medoid lookup structure that reduces metric computation from O(C) to O(√C) is an elegant design. The overall system is a well-engineered combination of workload-specific insights, though the scheduling component (warm/cold GPU pools with deadline-sorted allocation) follows conventional elastic resource management patterns.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: LLM Prompt Tuning-as-a-Service (LPT) has become a critical offering for IT enterprises, yet existing cluster management systems designed for DL training and inference are ill-suited to manage LPT workloads. Two key inefficiencies plague existing systems: (1) DL training systems provision fixed-size GPU clusters, leading to ~56% cluster utilization and near-doubling of resource costs; (2) DL inference systems incur 37–41% GPU allocation overhead from container/framework initialization per job, causing up to 50% end-to-end latency inflation and SLO violations reaching 70%. Additionally, LPT has a unique characteristic absent from training/inference workloads: convergence speed is highly sensitive to the choice of initial prompt, with the median ITA (Iterations-To-Accuracy) varying by 1.7–4.5× across random initial prompts. Methodology: PromptTuner introduces two co-designed innovations implemented atop Knative. First, the Prompt Bank is a query engine that exploits *prompt reusability*—the observation that prompts optimized for one task can accelerate convergence on similar tasks. It uses a two-layer data structure built with K-medoid clustering on LLM activation features of thousands of public prompts. During lookup, the first layer identifies the nearest cluster by computing a representative prompt's score (average loss on evaluation samples without tuning), and the second layer selects the lowest-scoring prompt within that cluster, reducing metric computations from C to K + C/K (ideally 2√C). Selection completes within 10 seconds. Second, the Workload Scheduler exploits *runtime reusability*—many LPT jobs share the same LLM, so pre-loaded PyTorch/CUDA runtimes and model weights can be reused across jobs. It maintains per-LLM warm GPU pools alongside a shared cold GPU pool. A warm-pool allocation algorithm (Algorithm 1) progressively assigns GPUs to pending jobs sorted by SLO deadline. A cold-pool algorithm (Algorithm 2) dynamically adds/removes GPUs to warm pools using a DelaySchedulable function that delays non-urgent jobs to avoid unnecessary GPU provisioning. A latency budget caps Prompt Bank overhead at 20% of the job's SLO latency. Key Results: Evaluated on a 32-GPU cluster (NVIDIA A100-80GB) with GPT2-Base, GPT2-Large, and Vicuna-7B across 12 datasets, PromptTuner achieves: (1) 4.0× SLO violation reduction vs. INFless and 7.9× vs. ElasticFlow; (2) 1.6× cost reduction vs. INFless and 4.5× vs. ElasticFlow. Under heavy workloads with LLaMA-30B, PromptTuner reduces SLO violations by 1.36–2.90× and costs by 1.20–1.79× over baselines. For Qwen7B-R1, it achieves 1.56–3.24× SLO violation reduction and 1.39–2.28× cost savings. The Prompt Bank selects initial prompts achieving 90%+ of ideal ITA performance and outperforms induction initialization by 1.81×, 1.38×, and 1.28× ITA speedup for GPT2-Base, GPT2-Large, and Vicuna-7B respectively. The two-layer structure reduces lookup overhead by up to 40× while preserving selection quality. At scale (96-GPU cluster), PromptTuner's scheduling overhead is only 13–67 ms. Strengths: - Addresses a genuinely novel workload class (LPT) with a principled characterization study that motivates both system innovations; the prompt sensitivity analysis (ITA CDF) is particularly well-evidenced. - The two-layer K-medoid Prompt Bank is an elegant data structure that reduces metric computation from O(C) to O(K + C/K) while keeping selection latency under 10 seconds, with empirical validation that it matches near-ideal prompt quality. - End-to-end evaluation on physical GPU clusters with real LPT traces, spanning small (GPT2) to large (LLaMA-30B, Qwen7B-R1) models, with ablations isolating each component's contribution. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The Prompt Bank requires offline extraction of LLM activation features for all prompt candidates whenever a new LLM is added, which can take hours and may limit adaptability to rapidly changing model versions. - The system assumes a fixed cold GPU pool size, which may not reflect real cloud environments where GPU availability fluctuates; the cold pool sizing strategy is treated as given rather than optimized. - Evaluation uses only gradient-based prompt tuning; the paper acknowledges PromptTuner can support other algorithms but does not validate with reinforcement learning or zero-order optimization variants, which have different convergence profiles.

#39 — Innovative (68)

Entropy-Aware On-Policy Distillation of Language Models

Woogyeol Jin, Taywon Min, Yongjin Yang, Swanand Ravindra Kadhe, Yi Zhou, Dennis Wei, Nathalie Baracaldo, Kimin Lee · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: EOPD provides a clean diagnostic insight — reverse KL fails specifically at high-entropy teacher tokens — and a simple, well-motivated fix of selectively applying forward KL at those positions. The approach is computationally efficient and broadly validated across model sizes and families. However, the core contribution is a relatively straightforward hybrid loss with entropy-based gating, building directly on well-understood properties of forward vs. reverse KL divergence.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: On-policy distillation (OPD) trains student language models on their own generated trajectories using reverse KL divergence, which encourages mode-seeking behavior. While effective at matching the teacher's dominant predictions, reverse KL causes diversity collapse and unstable learning signals at token positions where the teacher distribution has high entropy. This is particularly problematic for mathematical reasoning, where high-entropy tokens represent genuine decision points with multiple valid solution paths. Empirically, students trained with standard OPD retain only 6.8% of high-entropy tokens (entropy ≥ 1.0), compared to 18.5% in the teacher, indicating aggressive mode collapse that fails to transfer the teacher's uncertainty and distributional diversity. Methodology: The paper introduces Entropy-Aware On-Policy Distillation (EOPD), which augments the standard clipped reverse KL objective with a forward KL term that activates selectively based on the teacher's token-level entropy. The per-token loss is: L_EOPD = L_OPD + 1[H_t^te > τ] · L_FKL, where τ is an entropy threshold (default 0.8) and L_FKL is the forward KL computed over the teacher's top-k tokens (k=16). Forward KL is mode-covering, encouraging the student to spread probability mass across all plausible continuations rather than collapsing to the teacher's dominant mode. By restricting forward KL to high-entropy positions (approximately 15–20% of tokens), EOPD avoids the computational overhead and capacity strain of naive full forward KL. The method integrates seamlessly into existing PPO-style on-policy training pipelines without architectural changes. A toy experiment with categorical distributions validates the mechanism: under high-entropy teachers, reverse KL reward produces unstable top-1 change counts (84.0 ± 16.7 updates per step), while the proposed approach stabilizes this. Key Results: EOPD is evaluated on three Qwen3 student models (0.6B, 1.7B, 4B Base) against KD, GRPO, and OPD baselines across six math benchmarks (MATH500, AMC23, Minerva, OlympiadBench, AIME24, AIME25). Compared to OPD: Qwen3-0.6B-Base improves Avg@8 by +1.16 and Pass@8 by +1.37; Qwen3-1.7B-Base improves Avg@8 by +0.99 and Pass@8 by +2.39; Qwen3-4B-Base improves Avg@8 by +1.80 and Pass@8 by +5.05. On harder benchmarks (AIME24/25), the Pass@k performance gap widens as k increases, reaching Pass@128 differences of roughly 5–10 points, suggesting more diverse trajectory exploration. Out-of-domain evaluation on GPQA-Diamond, MMLU-Pro, and AlpacaEval 2.0 shows EOPD is competitive or superior to OPD, with GPQA Pass@8 improving from 77.83 to 81.31. EOPD also outperforms entropy-promoting baselines (Entropy Bonus, Advantage Shaping) by achieving lower forward KL at high-entropy positions despite similar overall entropy levels. Results generalize to Llama-3.2-3B/3.1-8B model pairs. Strengths: - Principled diagnosis: the paper provides rigorous theoretical and empirical analysis showing that reverse KL's mode-seeking property specifically fails at high-entropy token positions, motivating the fix cleanly. - Computationally efficient: by restricting forward KL to ~15–20% of tokens using the teacher's top-k (k=16) distribution, EOPD avoids the memory and compute cost of full forward KL while capturing most of the probability mass. - Broad empirical validation: consistent gains across three model sizes, six math benchmarks, two model families (Qwen3 and Llama), and out-of-domain tasks, with ablations on τ sensitivity and forward KL placement strategies. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The entropy threshold τ is a hyperparameter that must be tuned; while ablations show robustness across τ ∈ [0.6, 1.6], lower values activate forward KL more broadly and can hurt performance, requiring some search. - Evaluation is limited to mathematical reasoning domains; it remains unclear how well entropy-aware distillation transfers to other task types (e.g., code generation, open-ended dialogue) where high-entropy positions may carry different semantics. - Forward KL is approximated over only the teacher's top-k tokens, which excludes low-probability tails; while k=16 empirically captures sufficient probability mass, this approximation may miss important distributional structure in some settings.

#40 — Innovative (67)

Sink-Aware Pruning for Diffusion Language Models

Aidar Myrzakhan, Tianyi Li, Bowei Guo, Shengkun Tang, Zhiqiang Shen · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: The spatial/temporal variance decomposition revealing that attention sinks in diffusion language models are transient rather than stable is a genuinely novel diagnostic insight that challenges assumptions inherited from AR model pruning. The resulting sink-aware pruning method is principled and lightweight. However, the accuracy gains are modest (typically <2 percentage points), the evaluation is limited to post-training pruning without recovery fine-tuning, and the niche focus on DLMs narrows the immediate impact.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Diffusion Language Models (DLMs) incur high inference costs due to iterative denoising over multiple timesteps, making pruning critical for practical deployment. Existing pruning heuristics are largely inherited from autoregressive (AR) LLMs, which treat attention sink tokens — a small set of positions that disproportionately attract attention mass — as structurally essential anchors that must be preserved during pruning. This paper challenges that assumption: it shows that attention sink positions in DLMs exhibit substantially higher temporal variance across denoising steps compared to AR models, meaning DLM sinks are often transient rather than stable anchors. Naively applying AR-style sink-preservation heuristics to DLMs therefore wastes sparsity budget protecting ephemeral positions. Methodology: The paper introduces two variance statistics to characterize sink behavior: spatial variance (how unevenly attention concentrates across positions on average) and temporal variance (how much the sink centroid drifts across generation/denoising steps). Empirically, AR models like LLaMA-3 show high spatial but near-zero temporal variance — sinks stay fixed. DLMs like LLaDA and Dream show the opposite: lower spatial but orders-of-magnitude higher temporal variance (0.0149–0.0173 for DLMs vs. 3.4e-6–1.2e-5 for AR models), confirming sinks shift substantially across diffusion timesteps. Building on this, the authors propose Sink-Aware Pruning, a post-training, retraining-free method that plugs into existing pruning criteria (Wanda or SparseGPT). The algorithm: (1) aggregates per-token attention mass across all layers and heads at each diffusion timestep over a calibration set; (2) identifies sink tokens via a threshold criterion (attention mass exceeding the mean by ε) and converts this to a soft sink score via sigmoid; (3) averages scores across uniformly sampled timesteps to obtain a step-invariant estimate; (4) constructs a sink-masked activation X̃ by down-weighting sink positions by ω = 1 − s̄(j), suppressing their contribution; (5) substitutes X̃ into Wanda's or SparseGPT's importance scoring, so that transient sink positions do not inflate importance estimates and can be freely pruned. The calibration set uses 128 sequences of 2048 tokens from WikiText-2. Key Results: Sink-Aware Pruning is evaluated on LLaDA-8B, Dream-7B, LLaDA-1.5-8B, and MMaDA-8B across 8 benchmarks (MMLU, ARC-C, PIQA, WinoGrande, GSM8K, HellaSwag, GPQA, RACE) at 25%, 50%, and 75% unstructured sparsity. It consistently matches or outperforms Wanda and SparseGPT baselines. Gains are most pronounced at high sparsity: on LLaDA at 75% sparsity, Sink-Aware (Wanda) improves average accuracy from 29.99 to 30.94 (+0.95 pp) and Sink-Aware (SparseGPT) from 32.57 to 32.57 (flat but with individual benchmark gains). On LLaDA-1.5 at 75% sparsity with Wanda, average accuracy improves from 31.03 to 32.89 (+1.86 pp). For structured pruning on LLaDA, Sink-Aware outperforms LLaDA-structure at both 0.3 and 0.5 pruning ratios (e.g., PIQA: 0.6955 vs. 0.6834 at 0.3; 0.6037 vs. 0.5898 at 0.5). The method works as a general-purpose correction that complements both Wanda and SparseGPT backbones. Strengths: - Provides a principled, quantitative diagnosis of why AR pruning heuristics fail for DLMs, grounded in the novel spatial/temporal variance decomposition with clear empirical evidence across four model families. - The method is lightweight and retraining-free: it adds only a calibration-time attention aggregation step on top of existing pruning pipelines, with no architectural changes or fine-tuning. - Demonstrated generality: works as a plug-in for both Wanda (first-order) and SparseGPT (second-order) criteria, and across unstructured and structured pruning settings. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Sink statistics are estimated from a fixed calibration distribution (WikiText-2), so distribution shift may reduce reliability in out-of-domain settings. - Evaluation is primarily post-training pruning without recovery fine-tuning; combining sink-aware scoring with lightweight post-pruning adaptation could yield further gains but is not explored. - Broader validation on larger models (>8B parameters), longer contexts, and more multimodal DLM settings is still needed; the multimodal coverage is limited to one model (MMaDA-8B).

#41 — Innovative (67)

Turning Time Series into Algebraic Equations: Symbolic Machine Learning for Interpretable Modeling of Chaotic Time Series

Madhurima Panja, Grace Younes, Tanujit Chakraborty · arxiv.org · neural symbolic - new results
Why this rank: This paper earns 67 for its systematic, large-scale application of symbolic regression to chaotic time-series forecasting—a setting where interpretability is genuinely valuable yet underexplored. The complementary design of gradient-based (SyNF) and evolutionary (SyTF) symbolic forecasters is methodologically creative, and the finding that compact algebraic equations consistently outperform deep learning on 132 chaotic attractors is a meaningful and somewhat surprising empirical contribution. However, the core techniques (EQL networks, PySR) are existing tools adapted rather than invented, and the restriction to univariate one-step-ahead forecasting limits the practical novelty for real chaotic systems.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Chaotic time series are notoriously difficult to forecast due to sensitive dependence on initial conditions, strong nonlinearities, and regime-dependent variability. While modern deep learning achieves strong short-horizon accuracy, its black-box nature limits scientific insight and practical trust in domains where understanding underlying dynamics matters (epidemiology, climate science, ecology). Existing symbolic regression work has focused on recovering differential equations or static functional relationships rather than evaluating predictive performance in rolling forecasting settings. This paper asks whether symbolic regression can discover compact, interpretable forecasting equations that remain competitive with modern ML models in chaotic time-series prediction. Methodology: The paper proposes two complementary symbolic forecasters. The first, Symbolic Neural Forecaster (SyNF), adapts the Equation Learner (EQL) neural architecture to time-series forecasting by replacing conventional activations (ReLU, tanh) with fixed algebraic base functions (identity, sine, cosine, elementwise multiplication), enabling end-to-end differentiable discovery of interpretable algebraic equations from lagged inputs via gradient descent. Variants include SyNF-Reg (L1 regularization for sparsity) and SyNF-Div / SyNF-Div-Reg (learnable division units with curriculum-based stability constraints to capture rational functional forms). The second, Symbolic Tree Forecaster (SyTF), builds on PySR's genetic programming framework, evolving expression trees through a multi-population evolve-simplify-optimize loop with tournament selection, simulated annealing, adaptive parsimony penalties via 'frecency,' and Pareto-front model selection to balance accuracy and complexity. An extended variant SyTF-Div-Exp enlarges the operator set with division and exponential functions. Both frameworks operate on lagged windows of observations (p = 5, 10, or 25 lags) in a rolling-window nowcasting (one-step-ahead) setting. Uncertainty quantification is added via conformal prediction intervals. Evaluation uses 132 low-dimensional chaotic attractors from the 'dysts' benchmark and two real-world series: weekly San Juan dengue incidence (1990–2013) and El Niño 3.4 sea-surface temperature index (1990–2021). Baselines span Random Forest, XGBoost, LightGBM, LSTM, NBeats, NHiTS, TiDE, NLinear, and Transformer. Key Results: On the 132 synthetic chaotic attractors, SyTF achieves the lowest median errors across all lag configurations, confirmed as statistically best by the Multiple Comparison with the Best (MCB) test (mean rank 1.98 on RMSE, 2.02 on SMAPE with 5 lags). SyTF-Div-Exp ranks second. Specific median figures with 5 lags: SyTF RMSE = 0.004, MAE = 0.003, MARRE = 0.034, SMAPE = 0.342—compared to LSTM (RMSE = 0.020, MAE = 0.014) and Transformer (RMSE = 0.086, MAE = 0.074). All symbolic methods substantially outperform all deep learning baselines on synthetic attractors. On real-world data, the performance profile shifts: SyNF-Reg achieves the lowest errors on San Juan dengue across all metrics (SMAPE = 20.95, RMSE = 20.69, MAE = 15.76, MARRE = 6.91), and SyNF-Div-Reg achieves the best results on El Niño SST (SMAPE = 0.79, RMSE = 0.26, MAE = 0.20, MARRE = 6.30), outperforming all neural and ensemble baselines. The MCB test confirms SyNF-Div-Reg as the statistically best real-world forecaster (mean rank 2.50 on RMSE). Discovered equations are human-readable; for example, SyTF learns ŷ_t = 0.985y_{t-1} + 0.417 for El Niño SST, while SyNF learns a richer polynomial-trigonometric expression. Conformal prediction intervals from SyNF-Div-Reg adapt dynamically to volatility regimes in both real-world datasets. Strengths: - Provides the first large-scale systematic benchmark (132 chaotic attractors) for symbolic machine learning in time-series forecasting, establishing a rigorous empirical baseline for the field. - Achieves state-of-the-art one-step-ahead accuracy on both synthetic chaotic attractors and real-world datasets while simultaneously producing explicit, human-readable algebraic equations that expose underlying temporal dynamics. - Complementary design of SyNF (gradient-based, richer expressions, stronger on noisy real-world data) and SyTF (evolutionary, compact expressions, strongest on synthetic attractors) provides practitioners with a principled model-selection framework guided by data characteristics. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Evaluation is restricted to one-step-ahead (nowcasting) rolling-window forecasting; multi-step-ahead performance and the stability of discovered equations under iterative application are not assessed, which is critical for chaotic systems where errors amplify exponentially. - Both methods are limited to univariate inputs; real-world chaotic systems (climate, epidemics) are inherently multivariate, and excluding exogenous drivers likely constrains both accuracy and the interpretability of recovered functional relationships.

#42 — Innovative (67)

Blackbird Language Matrices: A Framework to Investigate the Linguistic Competence of Language Models

Paola Merlo, Chunyang Jiang, Giuseppe Samo, Vivi Nastase · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: The adaptation of Raven Progressive Matrices to linguistic competence testing is a creative cross-domain transfer that enables fine-grained counterfactual error analysis impossible with binary minimal-pair benchmarks. The VAE probing revealing specific latent units encoding semantic roles and voice is a meaningful interpretability contribution. However, the absence of systematic evaluation on frontier generative LLMs limits the paper's diagnostic relevance to current models.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Large language models excel at fluent generation and translation yet demonstrably fail at systematic generalisation and abstraction—the hallmark of human linguistic competence. Existing diagnostic benchmarks are insufficient: BLiMP tests only binary minimal pairs anchored to single phenomena, ARC uses abstract visual grids with no linguistic grounding, and Holmes aggregates pre-existing datasets without a principled structure for probing internal representations. There is a gap for a framework that (i) tests complex, multi-dimensional linguistic generalisations across sentences, (ii) supports fine-grained error diagnosis, and (iii) can probe *why* models succeed or fail rather than merely recording accuracy. Methodology: The paper introduces Blackbird Language Matrices (BLMs), linguistic puzzles modelled on the visual Raven Progressive Matrices (RPMs) used in IQ tests. Each BLM instance is a multiple-choice problem with a 7-sentence context sequence and an 8–9-item answer set; the correct answer continues the linguistic pattern encoded in the context. Patterns are formally defined via a grammar G_LP specifying objects (NP/PP chunks), features (semantic roles, grammatical number), external and internal rules, and progression operators (ALTERNATION or PROGRESSION). Contrastive wrong answers violate exactly one rule dimension, enabling counterfactual error analysis. Seven linguistic phenomena are covered: subject-verb agreement (Agr), causative/inchoative Change-of-State verbs (CoS), Roll verbs (manner-of-motion), object-drop (OD), spray/load alternation, sequence-of-tense, and a mixture. Datasets exist in up to four languages (English, French, Italian, Romanian) at three levels of lexical variation (Type I: fixed lexicon; Type II: one word varies per sentence; Type III: fully randomised). Seed sentences come from natural corpora (SPIKE, VerbNet) and are augmented via masked language modelling (DistilBERT, RoBERTa-Large) or ChatGPT-4 with Python prompts, followed by manual expert validation on 5-point Likert scales for grammatical and semantic plausibility. Baseline models use FFNN or CNN over averaged Electra or OpenAI-GPT token embeddings with a max-margin loss. To probe whether chunk structure is encoded in representations, a VAE compresses sentence embeddings (32×24 Electra arrays) into a 5-dimensional latent space trained with contrastive max-margin + KL loss. To investigate systematicity across the sequence, a two-level VAE stacks the sentence-level VAE with a task-level encoder-decoder that reads the compressed 7-sentence sequence and predicts the correct answer. Key Results: The FFNN baseline is solvable across all configurations: F1 reaches 0.94–0.99 for CoS English Type I, and stays above 0.40 even in the hardest spray/load Type I→III cross-evaluation. Agreement baselines achieve F1 up to 0.84 for English/French/Italian with Electra, but drop to 0.22–0.45 for Romanian. Error analysis reveals systematic patterns: for spray/load, errors concentrate on Alt-NP, Alt-PP, and LexPrep (grammatically correct but wrong sequence structure), confirming syntactic pattern learning but not full sequential logic; for CoS/OD, the dominant error is I-Int (transitive structure identified but subject's semantic role wrong—e.g., choosing 'The student broke the vase' instead of 'The vase broke'); for agreement, WN1/WN2 (wrong attractor grammatical number) dominate, showing local agreement is learned but long-distance sequence rules are not. The VAE probing achieves F1 of 0.896–0.975 (Table 5) in detecting chunk patterns from sentence embeddings, confirming LLMs encode phrase-level linguistic objects. Latent traversal analysis identifies specific units: unit 1 encodes agent/theme role distinction and active/passive voice; unit 5 encodes PP type (agentive *by*-PP vs. non-agentive PPs). The two-level VAE improves over the FFNN baseline particularly for Type III data and produces a large reduction in grammatical agreement errors (AEV, AEN1, AEN2), confirming that induced sentence structure contributes directly to systematic generalisation. Strengths: - Principled formal framework grounded in linguistic theory, enabling precise definition of error types (language errors vs. reasoning/sequence errors) and multi-level counterfactual analysis not possible with binary minimal-pair benchmarks. - Naturalistic, semi-manually curated data avoids the artificial independence assumptions of CFG-generated datasets, making findings more ecologically valid. - Multilingual parallel design (English, French, Italian, Romanian; with templates for Turkish and Hebrew) supports cross-linguistic comparative studies and reveals language-specific bottlenecks (e.g., Romanian). - VAE probing methodology provides mechanistic insight into what linguistic abstractions (chunks, semantic roles, voice) are encoded in pretrained embeddings, advancing interpretability beyond accuracy metrics. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Template construction is expert-dependent, time-consuming, and does not scale easily to new phenomena or languages without specialist linguistic knowledge. - Experiments focus on small encoder-based baselines (Electra, OpenAI-GPT embeddings + FFNN/VAE); large generative LLMs (GPT-4, Llama, etc.) are not systematically benchmarked, limiting conclusions about the state of the art. - The 5-dimensional VAE latent space may be too compressed for complex phenomena; Roll-verb and spray/load results on Italian/Romanian remain substantially lower, and the two-level VAE's improvement is uneven across tasks. - Sequence-of-tense and mixture datasets are much smaller (used only for few-shot ChatGPT experiments), leaving these phenomena underexplored relative to others.
