#71 — Moderately Innovative (57)

An Efficient Memoization Engine for Concurrent Graph Query Processing (complete version)

gaosen.org · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: KGraph delivers strong empirical results (up to 12.5x speedup) through the well-motivated insight that partition-level memoization avoids the overhead that makes naive whole-graph memoization counterproductive. The self-tuning optimizer combining a cost model with a decision tree for pivot selection is a practical contribution. However, memoization and graph partitioning are individually well-studied; the novelty lies in their careful combination and engineering rather than a conceptual departure.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Concurrent Graph Query (CGQ) processing — executing many independent graph queries simultaneously on the same graph — is a fundamental bottleneck in applications like DeepWalk, Personalized PageRank, Single-Source Shortest Path (SSSP) batches, and real-world services like Grab's taxi hailing (which processes over 1.5K SSSP queries per minute at peak). While prior work (e.g., ForkGraph) exploits cache/resource sharing among CGQs, it ignores a complementary opportunity: the results of completed queries are heavily reused by other queries. Empirical profiling shows that for SSSP on power-law graphs, ~90% of edges belong to the result of at least two queries, and >10% of edges appear in 90+ query results. Naively applying memoization to large graphs, however, degrades performance — on a 100M-edge graph, a hand-tuned memoization program is 20% slower than no memoization, because the overhead of scanning full memoized results offsets the reduction in compute. Methodology: The paper presents KGraph, a memoization engine for CGQs built on two core optimizations. First, *partition-based memoization with adaptation*: instead of memoizing query results over the entire graph, KGraph divides the graph into partitions and restricts memoization to within-partition results only. This fine-grained approach dramatically reduces the number of wasted memoization operations (scanning results that don't help the current query) while preserving high locality. A cost model automatically tunes the partition size by balancing intra-partition processing cost against inter-partition coordination overhead, subject to an LLC memory constraint: |E|/|P| + k/|P| × Mem_max ≤ Size_LLC. Second, *strategic pivot query selection*: rather than memoizing all queries, KGraph pre-selects a small set of "pivot" queries — vertices with high in-degree or boundary vertices at partition edges — whose results have the highest probability of being reused by other queries. Six selection strategies (S1–S6) spanning random, degree-based, and boundary-based approaches are evaluated, and a decision tree model trained on graph structural features (average degree, max degree, |V|, |E|, |P|) automatically selects the best strategy and pivot count for a given graph. The system exposes two APIs (INITMEMOIZATIONSTORAGE, MEMOIZATIONFUNCTOR) so users program only a sequential algorithm; KGraph automatically parallelizes it with memoization. KGraph is built on top of ForkGraph's cache-efficient fork-processing codebase, injecting memoization into intra-partition processing. Key Results: KGraph is evaluated against ForkGraph (state-of-the-art CGQ system) and Glign on 8 real-world graphs (up to 133M vertices, 5.5B edges) and 30 synthetic R-MAT graphs (10K–100M edges, densities 2–64) across five applications: SSSP, SSWP, SSR, DeepWalk, and PPR. On traversal-based queries (SSSP, SSWP, SSR), KGraph outperforms ForkGraph by up to 12.5× with an average speedup of 5.9×. On random walk queries (DeepWalk, PPR), KGraph achieves roughly 2× speedup on road networks but only ~20% improvement on dense social networks. Against Glign (optimized for power-law graphs), KGraph performs comparably on social networks but significantly outperforms it on road networks. On synthetic graphs scaling to 100M edges with density 64, KGraph achieves 49.4× speedup over Ligra and substantially higher operation reduction ratios than Ligra* (memoization with random pivots): 47.8% vs. 21.9% operation reduction at density 64 on 100M edges. In a real-world case study with Grab's Jakarta taxi dataset (3.9M vertices, 4.2M edges, 113 concurrent SSSP queries), KGraph reduces total runtime from 6519ms to 725ms (8ms for memoization overhead), well within acceptable latency for real-time service. Strengths: - The partition-based memoization insight is well-motivated by thorough profiling that precisely characterizes when and why naive memoization fails on large graphs, making the design decisions principled rather than heuristic. - The optimizer combining a cost model (for partition sizing) and a decision tree (for pivot selection) enables KGraph to self-tune across diverse graph structures without manual configuration, a significant practical advantage. - Comprehensive evaluation across five algorithm classes, eight real-world graphs spanning road networks, social networks, and web graphs, and a production case study with Grab provides strong evidence of broad applicability. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The performance gains on random walk applications (DeepWalk, PPR) on dense social networks are modest (~20%), because the large number of boundary vertices makes full boundary memoization memory-prohibitive, limiting pivot coverage and thus sharing opportunity. - The approach requires graph partitioning as a prerequisite, and for very large graphs (e.g., Tw and Uk with billions of edges), METIS-based partitioning runs out of memory or time, forcing fallback to chunk partitioning which produces high inter-partition edge ratios and reduces memoization effectiveness. - Memory overhead for random walk applications grows super-linearly with the number of pivots (exceeding graph size when α > 1.0), constraining how aggressively memoization can be applied for walk-based workloads.
Citations
#72 — Moderately Innovative (57)

Orion: Characterizing and Programming Apple's Neural Engine for LLM Training and Inference

Ramchand Kumaresan · arxiv.org · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: Scores 57 as an impressive reverse-engineering and systems effort that opens Apple's Neural Engine to public LLM work for the first time, with delta compilation being a genuinely clever workaround to the compile-time weight baking constraint. However, the contribution is primarily engineering rather than algorithmic innovation, the ANE decode being slower than CPU undermines the practical value proposition, and validation is limited to 110M-parameter training for only 1,000 steps without convergence or downstream evaluation.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Apple's Neural Engine (ANE) — present in all Apple silicon since 2017, delivering up to 38 TOPS on the M4 — remains entirely unused by public LLM frameworks. CoreML, the only official interface, operates as a black-box scheduler with no developer control over ANE dispatch, no support for on-device training, and no ability to inspect or program the ANE's native instruction set (MIL). Existing workarounds (ANEgpt, maderix) demonstrated raw ANE dispatch but lacked compilers, suffered NaN divergence during training, and required Python orchestration. The gap is a complete end-to-end system combining direct ANE programming, a compiler, and stable multi-step training. Methodology: Orion bypasses CoreML entirely by reverse-engineering Apple's private `_ANEClient` and `_ANECompiler` APIs from `AppleNeuralEngine.framework`. The system is structured as five layers: CLI applications, a model layer, a compiler, a core runtime, and the ANE hardware. The compiler accepts a 27-operation graph IR and lowers it through five optimization passes (dead code elimination, identity elimination, cast fusion, SRAM annotation, ANE constraint validation) to generate ANE-native MIL programs with BLOBFILE weight references — 13 verified frontends covering GPT-2 inference and Stories110M training kernels. The runtime manages IOSurface-backed zero-copy tensor I/O, a program cache keyed by (model, layer, sequence length, weight version), and the key innovation: delta compilation. Because the ANE bakes weights at compile time, naïve training requires full recompilation (~4,200 ms/step for 60 kernels). Delta compilation bypasses `ANECCompile()` entirely by unloading a compiled `_ANEModel`, patching its BLOBFILE weight files on disk, and reloading — exploiting the fact that programs sharing the same `hexStringIdentifier` (composite SHA-256 of MIL text) reuse the same microcode. Numerical stability required fixing three interacting bugs: (1) deferred compilation to load checkpoint weights before compiling programs; (2) fp16 activation clamping to [-65504, +65504] before softmax/LayerNorm to prevent overflow cascades; (3) gradient sanitization (NaN→0, ±∞→±65504) with post-BLOBFILE-load validation. LoRA is implemented via adapter-as-input: matrices A and B are passed as IOSurface inputs rather than baked weights, enabling zero-recompilation hot-swap of adapters. Key Results: Delta compilation reduces per-step recompilation overhead from 4,200 ms to 494 ms (8.5× speedup), yielding a 3.8× total training speedup — 1,000 training steps on Stories110M complete in 22 minutes vs. ~85 minutes with full recompilation, with zero NaN occurrences in both. A stability stress test across 5 independent resume chains (25 total steps) shows monotonically decreasing loss in all chains (13.975±0.003 at step 1 → 13.913±0.007 at step 5), 100% exec() restart success, and 0.612 TFLOPS throughput. For GPT-2 124M inference, ANE decode achieves 170 tok/s with 100% top-1 argmax agreement against CPU fp32 (max logit error 0.073). CPU decode (283 tok/s) outperforms ANE decode due to ~2.3 ms IOSurface per-dispatch overhead, but ANE prefill reaches 165 tok/s after program caching. Specific operations show large ANE advantages: softmax over vocab=32,000 is 33.8× faster on ANE vs. CPU (2.40 ms vs. 81.11 ms). Strengths: - Delta compilation is a technically novel workaround to a fundamental hardware constraint (compile-time weight baking), reducing an 83.9% overhead to 36.8% with no compiler invocation - Systematic catalog of 20 ANE programming constraints (14 newly discovered), providing the most comprehensive public documentation of ANE MIL IR restrictions, memory layout requirements, and numerical behaviors - Complete, open-source Objective-C runtime requiring no Python for inference or training, with native BPE/SentencePiece tokenizers and reproducible checkpoint resume Weaknesses / Limitations: - Relies on undocumented private Apple APIs (`_ANEClient`, `_ANECompiler`) that may break without notice across macOS updates, with no mitigation path - ANE decode (170 tok/s) is slower than CPU decode (283 tok/s) for autoregressive generation due to fixed ~2.3 ms IOSurface round-trip overhead per dispatch, undermining the hardware's value for this critical workload - Validation is limited to M4 Max hardware, Stories110M (110M parameters) on TinyStories, and only 1,000 training steps — not convergence to a useful language model nor evaluation on downstream tasks; quantization (INT8/INT4) and learning rate schedules are absent
Search Results 2603.06728 PDF
#73 — Moderately Innovative (56)

DyLLM: Efficient Diffusion LLM Inference via Saliency-based Token Selection and Partial Attention

Younjoo Lee, Junghoo Lee, Seungkyun Dan, Jaiyoung Park, Jung Ho Ahn · arxiv.org · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: DyLLM identifies and exploits layer-wise temporal sparsity in masked diffusion LLM denoising, achieving impressive 7-9× speedups while being training-free and model-agnostic. The formal error bounds connecting temporal cosine similarity to FFN approximation quality provide principled justification. The approach targets an emerging but still niche model class, and the threshold parameter requires per-model tuning.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Masked Diffusion Language Models (MDLMs) like LLaDA and Dream enable parallel token decoding through iterative denoising, offering a promising alternative to autoregressive generation. However, their iterative refinement process is computationally expensive because the entire sequence must be re-processed at every denoising step — a "repeated prefill" problem. Unlike autoregressive models that benefit from incremental KV caching, MDLMs use bidirectional attention that invalidates simple caching strategies. Existing acceleration approaches (Fast-dLLM, dKV-Cache, dLLM-Cache, Elastic-Cache) rely on fixed refresh schedules or rigid heuristics that do not account for layer-wise dynamics, and scale poorly as parallel decoding degree increases. Methodology: DyLLM is a training-free inference framework that exploits layer-wise temporal sparsity in MDLM denoising. The core insight is that across consecutive diffusion steps, the majority of token representations remain stable — only a small subset, termed "salient tokens," undergo meaningful semantic transitions. DyLLM identifies salient tokens at each layer by computing the cosine similarity of attention context vectors between adjacent steps (temporal cosine similarity s_{t,l}); tokens whose similarity falls below a threshold τ are designated salient and recomputed, while the rest reuse cached activations. This layer-adaptive selection allows aggressive pruning in early layers (which exhibit near-unity similarity) while expanding the salient set in deeper layers. For attention, DyLLM introduces a saliency-aware approximate attention mechanism: salient tokens receive full row-wise attention recomputation (exact update), while non-salient tokens receive only a sparse column update via attention weights from salient source tokens (reducing attention complexity from O(N²d) to O(N·|A_{t-1,l}|d)). Additionally, DyLLM separates response-only steps (processing only response tokens for ~75% of steps) from full-sequence steps (every 4 steps), mirroring the prefill/decode split of autoregressive models. A warmup phase of T_full=4 full steps initializes the caches before switching to sparse steps. Key Results: Evaluated on LLaDA 8B Instruct and Dream 7B Instruct across GSM8K, MBPP, MATH, and MMLU-Pro benchmarks on a single H100 GPU. DyLLM achieves up to 7.6× throughput improvement on LLaDA and 9.6× on Dream compared to original implementations, while preserving or slightly improving accuracy. For example, on GSM8K with n_u=1: DyLLM (τ=0.99) achieves 79.08 accuracy at 87.21 tokens/sec (7.60× speedup) vs. original 77.79 at 11.47 tok/s on LLaDA; Dream sees 79.30 at 111.79 tok/s (8.89×) vs. 75.59 at 12.57. On MBPP (LLaDA), DyLLM reaches 30.00 accuracy at 169.62 tok/s (5.12×) vs. original 29.20 at 33.11. DyLLM consistently outperforms Fast-dLLM and dLLM-Cache on accuracy-throughput tradeoffs, particularly at higher parallel decoding degrees (n_u=2,4), where Fast-dLLM degrades rapidly due to expensive full-sequence refresh steps. In some configurations, DyLLM even surpasses baseline accuracy — attributed to suppression of softmax noise from irrelevant tokens. Strengths: - Training-free and model-agnostic: requires no fine-tuning and applies directly to existing MDLMs (LLaDA, Dream) with negligible overhead for saliency computation. - Theoretically grounded: Propositions 3.1 and 3.2 formally bound the FFN approximation error as a function of temporal cosine similarity, providing principled justification for the threshold-based selection policy. - Superior scalability with parallel decoding: Unlike Fast-dLLM whose refresh step cost grows with n_u, DyLLM avoids full-sequence refresh entirely, maintaining efficiency at higher parallelism degrees where diffusion LLMs are most competitive. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Single threshold parameter τ requires tuning per model family (τ=0.99 for LLaDA, τ=0.9975 for Dream), and is not fully adaptive across layers or tasks — though the layer-adaptive selection partially addresses this. - The warmup phase (T_full full steps) and periodic full-sequence steps every 4 iterations still incur non-trivial overhead; the optimal T_full and refresh interval are fixed heuristics rather than dynamically determined. - Evaluation is limited to two open-source MDLM families on a single GPU; performance on larger models, longer sequences, or multi-GPU settings is not studied, and the interaction with semi-AR decoding (which substantially boosts accuracy) is not explored in combination with DyLLM.

#74 — Moderately Innovative (56)

LLM-WikiRace: Benchmarking Long-term Planning and Reasoning over Real-World Knowledge Graphs

Juliusz Ziomek, William Bankes, Lorenz Wolf, Shyam Sundhar Ramesh, Xiaohang Tang, Ilija Bogunovic · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: The WikiRace benchmark is a well-executed operationalization of planning over real-world knowledge graphs, and the 'planning gap' finding — that knowledge-matched models diverge dramatically in navigation success — is a useful diagnostic. The loop analysis revealing systematic inability to escape revisitation cycles is insightful. However, game-based LLM benchmarks are increasingly common, and the oracle action-space filtering partially undermines ecological validity.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Most existing LLM planning benchmarks rely on synthetic or highly structured environments with limited state and action spaces, leaving open the question of how well LLMs can leverage pretrained world knowledge to reason, plan, and adapt in large, real-world knowledge graphs. The paper addresses this gap by introducing LLM-WikiRace, a benchmark based on the WikiRace game, where an agent must navigate Wikipedia's hyperlink graph from a source page to a target page by selecting hyperlinks step by step. This setting requires coordinating long-horizon planning, semantic reasoning over open-domain concepts, and operational deployment of world knowledge under partial observability — capabilities not probed by prior benchmarks. Methodology: The benchmark is built on a June 2025 snapshot of the Wikipedia hyperlink graph, retaining the largest strongly connected component of 549,232 pages. Three difficulty levels are defined by shortest-path length between source and target: easy (path length 3–4, 200 pairs), medium (length 5–6, 150 pairs), and hard (length 7–8, 100 pairs). At each step the LLM agent observes the current page title, target page, traversal history, and up to 50 outgoing links (filtered using oracle shortest-path distances, but these distances are never revealed to the model). A maximum of 30 steps is allowed per episode. The authors evaluate over 20 models spanning closed-source (GPT-5, Gemini 3, Claude Opus 4.5, Grok 4.1-Fast, DeepSeek R1, Kimi K2) and open-source (LLaMA 3 1B–70B, Gemma 3 4B–27B, Mistral, Qwen, diffusion LMs) systems. To disentangle world knowledge from planning ability, the authors design a separate binary classification experiment probing whether models know which Wikipedia pages are directly hyperlinked. They also run preliminary DAPO-based fine-tuning experiments on Qwen-2.5-7B and compare top models against a human gameplay corpus. Key Results: Performance degrades sharply with difficulty. Top models achieve >90% success on easy (Gemini 3: 95%, GPT-5: 92.5%, Claude Opus 4.5: 91.5%), roughly 50–66% on medium (Gemini 3: 66%, GPT-5: 60%, Opus 4.5: 56%), and below 25% on hard (Gemini 3: 23%, GPT-5: 15%, Opus 4.5: 18%). Open-source models largely fail on hard tasks, with LLaMA 3 70B reaching only 7% and smaller variants scoring 0%. A key finding is the "planning gap": models with similar world-knowledge F1 scores diverge dramatically in game success rates, with reasoning/instruct-tuned models consistently outperforming base models. Trajectory analysis reveals that loops (revisiting a page within one episode) are strongly negatively correlated with success (regression coefficient −1.02, 95% CI [−1.13, −0.91]); Gemini 3 enters loops in 66% of hard games and Claude Opus 4.5 in 61%, with near-zero recovery rates on hard tasks. DAPO fine-tuning on Qwen-2.5-7B improves easy success from 22.5% to 67.5% after 300 steps but yields no improvement on hard. On the Human Gameplay Corpus, all three frontier models achieve 100% success vs. 98.5% for humans, but models take fewer suboptimal steps (Gemini 3: 0.9, GPT-5: 1.2, Opus 4.5: 1.1) compared to humans (2.3). Strengths: - Reveals a concrete and interpretable "planning gap" between world-knowledge-matched models, providing a diagnostic that goes beyond aggregate accuracy metrics. - Comprehensive evaluation across 20+ models at varying scales, including both open- and closed-source systems, enabling a broad assessment of current frontier capabilities. - Trajectory-level behavioral analysis (loop frequency, recovery rate, average max visitation) provides mechanistic insight into failure modes — specifically that models recognize loops but systematically fail to escape them. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The action-space filtering using oracle shortest-path distances (to bound prompt size to 50 links) is an environment-side oracle intervention that could artificially simplify or distort the true difficulty of the task, as it implicitly guides which links are plausible. - DAPO fine-tuning experiments are preliminary and show zero improvement on hard instances, with no exploration of multi-step RL approaches that might more directly target long-horizon replanning failures — the paper identifies the gap but offers little toward closing it. - The human baseline is drawn from a public game corpus with heterogeneous player skill and is acknowledged as a coarse reference, limiting the validity of human-vs-LLM comparisons.

#75 — Moderately Innovative (56)

SafeNet: A Neural - Symbolic Network for Safe Planning in Robotic Systems using Formal Method-Guided LLM Fine-Tuning

par.nsf.gov · neural symbolic - new results
Why this rank: SafeNet presents a meaningful integration of formal methods with LLM fine-tuning, using STL-based reward machines to automate supervision for the SFT→DPO pipeline. The automated reward machine replacing manual trajectory labeling is a practical contribution, and the results show large absolute gains across model families. However, the approach is fundamentally a combination of existing techniques (LoRA, DPO, STL evaluation) applied to simplified simulated environments, with significant performance degradation at scale.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Robotic systems face a fundamental tension between the flexibility needed for complex real-world tasks and the formal safety guarantees required when operating near humans. Traditional control methods (model predictive control, potential fields) struggle with the dynamism of modern robotic environments, while pure machine learning approaches lack verifiable safety guarantees and suffer from data bias and generalization failures. Pre-trained LLMs offer broad reasoning capabilities but lack domain-specific knowledge of robotic constraints, making them unreliable for safety-critical trajectory generation and task planning without further adaptation. Methodology: SafeNet is a neural-symbolic framework that fine-tunes pre-trained LLMs using formal method-derived supervision. The core pipeline has three main components. First, a formal method-based reward machine automatically evaluates trajectory compliance with Signal Temporal Logic (STL) specifications — using quantitative semantics that produce scalar compliance scores (positive = satisfied, negative = violated) rather than binary pass/fail. For combinatorial spaces (block manipulation), the reward machine checks logical constraint adherence; for continuous spaces (path planning), it extends the Argus framework with a time-constrained "Finally" operator to handle temporal reach-by-deadline requirements. Second, a two-phase fine-tuning strategy is applied: Supervised Fine-Tuning (SFT) with LoRA trains the model on golden trajectories verified by the reward machine, enforcing correct output format and constraint adherence; Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) then aligns the model by contrasting safe trajectories (y+) against unsafe or invalid ones (y−), with the SFT weights serving as both initialization and reference model (β=0.2). Third, prompt optimization using a structured template encodes task description, STL notation, initial state, and a worked example in HTML-tagged fields. Experiments cover five models from the Mistral and Llama families (3B–12B parameters, 4-bit quantized) across two tasks: Block-World (combinatorial block ordering satisfying STL temporal ordering constraints) and grid-based Path Planning (navigation with obstacle avoidance and timed goal-reaching). Key Results: For Block-World (Mistral-7B), the safe rate improved from 1.17% (pre-trained) to 88.67% after SFT and 91.60% after SFT+DPO, out of 512 test cases. Invalid trajectories collapsed from 475 to just 14. For Path Planning (small difficulty level), all five fine-tuned models improved dramatically: Mistral-Small achieved the highest safe rate of 90.63% (SFT+DPO) versus 7.23% pre-trained; Llama-3.1-8B reached 88.09% versus 1.37% baseline; even the small Llama-3.2-3B reached 47.46% from 0.78%. Performance degrades with map complexity: Mistral-7B achieves 85.74% on small maps, 65.2% on medium, and 55.9% on large. Sensitivity analysis shows LoRA rank correlates with performance (45.61% at rank 4 vs. 58.40% at rank 32 for SFT-only), with diminishing returns above rank 16 (only +2.74% gain from rank 16 to 32). Strengths: - Automated reward machine replaces manual trajectory labeling, providing consistent, scalable supervision signal and enabling large DPO datasets (85K+ block-world pairs; 753K+ path planning pairs) - Two-phase SFT→DPO fine-tuning with LoRA is computationally efficient (4-bit quantized models) and produces large absolute gains across model families and sizes - Extension of the Argus STL evaluator with a time-constrained Finally operator meaningfully expands the expressiveness of enforceable constraints in continuous navigation settings Weaknesses / Limitations: - Performance degrades significantly with environment complexity (55.9% on large maps vs. 85.7% on small), and the authors attribute part of this to 4-bit quantization, leaving the ceiling of the approach unclear - Evaluation is limited to simulated, discrete-grid tasks; no physical robot experiments or continuous-dynamics simulators are used, leaving a gap between demonstrated capability and real deployment - The one-to-one environment–STL formula mapping assumption is acknowledged as a simplification; scaling to richer, multi-specification environments or dynamic constraint updates is not addressed

#76 — Moderately Innovative (55)

Sparsity Induction for Accurate Post-Training Pruning of Large Language Models

Minhao Jiang, Zhikai Li, Xuewen Liu, Jing Zhang, Mengjuan Chen, Qingyi Gu · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: Sparsity Induction is a well-engineered preprocessing step that reshapes weight distributions before pruning via learnable scale/shift transforms, achieving zero inference overhead through mathematical absorption. The 22.65× speedup in Hessian updates is a practical contribution. However, the core idea of distribution reshaping before pruning is relatively straightforward, and gains over strong baselines like SparseGPT at moderate sparsity are marginal, suggesting diminishing returns in this direction.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Post-training sparsity (PTS) methods for LLMs — such as magnitude pruning, Wanda, and SparseGPT — focus almost exclusively on designing better importance scores to select which weights to remove. However, native dense LLM weight matrices are not inherently sparse, so directly applying any pruning criterion disrupts the distributional and scaling structure established during training. The resulting inter-channel imbalances propagate through attention and feed-forward paths, causing accuracy degradation that importance-score refinements alone cannot remedy. The paper argues that returns from ever-more-refined scoring have become marginal, and that the fundamental bottleneck is the pre-pruning weight distribution itself. Methodology: The authors propose Sparsity Induction (SI), a training-light pre-pruning step that reshapes the model's weight distribution and feature structure to be more sparsity-friendly before any mask is applied, without adding inference-time parameters or overhead. SI operates along two complementary axes. At the distributional level, mathematically equivalent per-channel scaling (s) and shifting (δ) transformations are introduced for linear layers (Y = W X + b reparameterized via S⁻¹ and WS) and inverse scale pairs for Q/K attention projections; these factors are learned via a lightweight MSE objective on 128 calibration samples over 1–5 epochs and then fully absorbed back into the weight matrices, leaving no residual modules. At the feature level, a Spectral Norm Loss regularizer (Rp = exp(−α Σ ‖Wℓ‖p)) is added to encourage low-rank structure, combined with a robust per-channel initialization derived from the median-vs-mean statistics of calibration outputs to stabilize convergence. An efficiency optimization replaces repeated forward passes for importance-score refresh with a diagonal Gauss–Newton/Hessian proxy that updates in O(d_in) via cached diagonals, yielding a 22.65× speedup in metric recomputation. SI is plug-and-play: it is applied before pruning and is compatible with any downstream PTS method (Magnitude, Wanda, SparseGPT) and with both unstructured and N:M semi-structured sparsity patterns. Key Results: Experiments cover OPT-125M/350M/1.3B/2.7B and LLaMA-1/2 7B/13B across 50% unstructured, 2:4, and 4:8 sparsity regimes. On WikiText-2 perplexity at 50% sparsity, SI+Magnitude reduces LLaMA-1-7B PPL from 17.28 to 12.08; SI+SparseGPT reduces OPT-125M from 36.97 to 35.79. At 2:4 sparsity SI+Wanda on LLaMA-1-7B improves from 11.53 to 10.51. Zero-shot accuracy (average over ARC-Easy/Challenge, HellaSwag, BoolQ, Winogrande, OpenBookQA) on LLaMA-1/2 7B/13B shows consistent gains: e.g., SI+Magnitude at 50% sparsity on LLaMA-1-7B improves from 43.17% to 47.52%; SI+SparseGPT at 2:4 on LLaMA-2-13B improves from 52.14% to 52.59%. The fast Hessian update reduces Wanda metric refresh time on LLaMA-7B from 345.91 s to 15.27 s (22.65×). End-to-end inference latency under 2:4 sparsity is unchanged vs. the base Wanda pruned model (251 ms), confirming zero runtime overhead from SI. Strengths: - Zero inference-time overhead: all learned scale/shift parameters are mathematically absorbed into weights before deployment, preserving full compatibility with N:M hardware acceleration. - Plug-and-play design: SI acts as a preprocessing step decoupled from the pruning criterion, making it immediately applicable on top of any existing PTS method without modification. - Highly efficient calibration: only 128 samples and 1–5 epochs are needed, and the fast Hessian proxy gives a 22.65× speedup in importance-score refresh, keeping the total overhead modest. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Evaluation is limited to decoder-only models (OPT and LLaMA families) up to 13B parameters; it is unclear how well SI scales to very large models (70B+) or encoder-decoder architectures where inter-layer distribution shifts may differ. - The gains on strong baselines like SparseGPT at moderate sparsity (50% unstructured) are small (e.g., LLaMA-1-7B PPL 7.17 → 7.13), suggesting SI's benefit is concentrated in the high-sparsity or weaker-baseline regime and may not always justify the additional calibration pass.
Recommended 2602.21652 PDF
#77 — Moderately Innovative (55)

$ P^ 2$ GNN: Two Prototype Sets to boost GNN Performance

Arihant Jain, Gundeep Arora, Anoop Saladi, Chaosheng Dong · arxiv.org · karypis - new results
Why this rank: P²GNN offers a clean plug-and-play engineering contribution with two complementary prototype mechanisms addressing real GNN limitations (homophily assumption, lack of global context). The comprehensive evaluation across 18 datasets including proprietary production graphs is thorough. However, prototype-based methods and global-context augmentation for GNNs are established ideas, and the framework introduces substantial hyperparameter complexity without fundamentally changing how we think about message passing.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Message Passing Graph Neural Networks (MP-GNNs) face two fundamental limitations in practice: (1) heavy reliance on local context with no access to global or graph-level features, and (2) an implicit assumption of strong homophily among connected nodes, causing degraded performance on heterophilous graphs where neighbors often belong to different classes. These issues are especially acute in industry applications like fraud detection, where malicious nodes are deliberately connected to benign nodes to camouflage their behavior. Standard GNNs (GCN, GAT, GraphSAGE) can underperform simple MLP baselines on noisy heterophilous datasets — e.g., GAT drops 2.6% below MLP on the Film dataset. Methodology: The paper introduces P²GNN, a plug-and-play framework that augments any message-passing GNN with two complementary sets of learnable prototype nodes. The first set, Prototypes as Neighbours (P_N), consists of K_N class-independent pseudo-nodes connected to all real nodes via directed edges. These prototypes participate in message passing using a separate GNN branch with its own weight parameters, injecting global context into every node's aggregation. Their contributions are mixed with standard neighbor messages via a learned attention mechanism (Eq. 2). The second set, Prototypes for Message-Alignment (P_A), consists of K_A learnable prototypes operating in hidden embedding space. Before passing a node's aggregated message, it is aligned to the nearest P_A prototype via a softmax attention mechanism (Query=P_A, Key/Value=H_N), effectively performing hard-clustering to reduce intra-cluster variance and denoise the neighborhood signal (Eq. 3–4). Both prototype sets are jointly optimized end-to-end with the downstream task loss plus three auxiliary losses: an alignment loss (maximizing cosine similarity to nearest prototype), a diversity loss (maximizing entropy of the assignment distribution to prevent collapse), and a sparsity loss (L1+L2 Frobenius regularization on prototype matrices). The combined adjacency matrix extension adds O(K×|V|) rather than O(|V|²) edges, making it scalable. Key Results: P²GNN is evaluated on 18 datasets — 16 public benchmarks and 2 proprietary Amazon e-commerce graphs. On 8 small-scale public datasets (Table 5), P²GNN achieves an average rank of 1.5 vs. the next best (GloGNN++ at 4.0, ACM-GCN at 3.6), with statistically significant improvements over ACM-GCN backbone on 6/8 datasets (p < 0.05). On 6 large-scale non-homophilous datasets (Table 7), P²GNN achieves average rank 1.2, beating GloGNN++ (rank 2.3). On fraud detection (YelpChi/Amazon), P²GNN scores 84.43/97.64 AUC-ROC, outperforming strong specialized baselines like PC-GNN (81.18/95.55) and GHRN (82.88/96.19). On proprietary e-commerce node recommendation, P²GNN achieves ~4% relative improvement over the production GCN on E-comm1 and ~1.5% on E-comm2 across Hits@K and MRR metrics. On fixed-split benchmarks (Table 11), P²GNN achieves an average rank of 2.0 across 8 datasets. Ablation studies confirm both prototype types contribute independently; adding either P_N or P_A to GCN, SGC, or ACM-GCN backbones consistently improves accuracy. Strengths: - True plug-and-play design: the framework is backbone-agnostic and requires only minor modifications to the adjacency matrix and layer computation, demonstrated on GCN, SGC, and ACM-GCN backbones - Class-independent prototypes avoid label leakage and scale to multi-class settings without linear growth in prototype count, unlike ProtoGNN which is limited to binary classification - Comprehensive empirical validation across diverse settings: small/large/fraud/proprietary graphs, node classification and recommendation tasks, with statistical significance testing Weaknesses / Limitations: - Introduces several new hyperparameters (K_N, K_A, λ_a, λ_d, λ_s) requiring careful tuning; while Bayesian search is used, it still adds tuning overhead compared to baseline GNNs - Performance on Squirrel and Chameleon is below SOTA due to known train-test data leakage from duplicate nodes in those datasets, limiting fair evaluation in those cases - Training time increases modestly but not dramatically (~33% overhead on large datasets per epoch), yet the paper does not report total wall-clock training time including hyperparameter search

#78 — Moderately Innovative (55)

Towards Understanding Hybrid Protein Language Model Design: A Systematic Ablation and Interpretability Study

openreview.net · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: A methodologically rigorous controlled ablation study that provides the first clean comparison of SSM, MoE, and hybrid architectures for protein language models. The finding that MoE creates a systematic local-task/global-task trade-off, explained by interpretable expert specialization patterns, is a useful contribution. However, the 20M parameter scale limits practical relevance, and the individual architectural components are all previously published.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Protein language models (PLMs) have rapidly diversified to incorporate mixture-of-experts (MoE) layers, state-space models (SSMs), and hybrid SSM-attention architectures. While each component has demonstrated compelling results individually, prior studies have been conducted at different scales, on different corpora, and with different evaluation protocols — making it impossible to attribute performance gains to specific architectural choices. This paper addresses the gap by providing the first controlled, parameter-matched ablation study of these components for PLMs, disentangling their individual and combined contributions. Methodology: All model variants are trained under a unified ESM-2-style masked language modeling (MLM) protocol on UniRef50, constrained to ~20M active parameters with hidden dimension 512, for 500K steps. Five architecture families are compared: (1) a dense Transformer baseline (ESM-2 style, 6 layers); (2) a pure bidirectional SSM (Hydra) replacing all Transformer layers; (3) hybrid SSM-Transformer models at two block ratios (4H:1T and 2H:2T); (4) MoE variants replacing FFNs with SonicMoE at two sparsity levels (64 experts/top-8 and 8 experts/top-2); and (5) hybrid MoE models combining SSM-Transformer hybrids with MoE at Transformer blocks only. Evaluation uses the full TAPE benchmark suite: stability, fluorescence, secondary structure (TS115, CB513, CASP12), remote homology (family/superfamily/fold), and contact prediction (medium/long-range P@L/5). An interpretability analysis probes expert specialization by computing P(secondary structure label | expert) across MoE layers. Key Results: Hybrid SSM-Transformer architectures are the most broadly effective non-MoE configurations. The 2H:2T hybrid achieves the best fluorescence (0.678), secondary structure overall (0.764), remote homology overall (0.616), medium-range contacts (0.420), and long-range contacts (0.410) among non-MoE models, outperforming both pure baselines. MoE introduces a consistent task-dependent trade-off: it improves stability (best: 4H:1T Hybrid MoE at 0.748 vs. ESM-2 baseline 0.717) and secondary structure (2H:2T Hybrid MoE achieves 0.795 on CB513), but degrades remote homology substantially (64:8 MoE drops overall remote homology to 0.586 vs. 0.605 for ESM-2) and long-range contact prediction. This pattern holds across all backbone configurations. Higher-sparsity MoE (64:8) outperforms lower-sparsity (8:2). Expert specialization analysis reveals that MoE experts develop clear helix- and strand-preferring specializations that deepen with layer depth; removing auxiliary load-balancing loss during fine-tuning sharpens this specialization and improves secondary structure performance further. Strengths: - Rigorous controlled experimental design: all architectures share identical training data, protocol, parameter budget (20M active), and evaluation suite, enabling clean attribution of performance differences to architectural choices rather than confounds. - Mechanistic interpretability analysis of MoE expert routing provides a compelling explanation for the observed local-task/global-task performance trade-off, connecting emergent expert specialization to task granularity. - Practical finding that selectively removing auxiliary load-balancing loss during fine-tuning can unlock task-specific gains for per-residue objectives, offering an actionable design recommendation. Weaknesses / Limitations: - All experiments are conducted at a small 20M active-parameter scale; it is unclear whether the findings generalize to the 650M–3B parameter range where PLMs are typically deployed in practice, leaving the most important scaling regime unvalidated. - The evaluation is limited to the TAPE benchmark suite, which may not capture performance on more challenging or application-relevant tasks such as fitness landscape prediction, protein-protein interaction modeling, or zero-shot mutant effect prediction.

#79 — Moderately Innovative (54)

Spherical double k-means: a co-clustering approach for textual data analysis

art.torvergata.it · karypis - new results
Why this rank: SDKM is a principled integration of cosine similarity into the double k-means co-clustering framework, with a formally proven monotonically non-decreasing objective. The method is computationally efficient and produces jointly interpretable term-document biclusters. However, it is fundamentally a combination of two well-established techniques (SKM and DKM) rather than a conceptual departure, and the evaluation is limited to text corpora where cosine similarity is already standard.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Standard k-means clustering fails on high-dimensional, sparse term-document matrices because Euclidean distance loses discriminative power as dimensionality grows and is biased by document length. Spherical k-means (SKM) addresses this by using cosine similarity but clusters only documents or only terms, not both simultaneously. Researchers often need to cluster both documents and terms into coherent groups for topic modeling, keyword extraction, and understanding thematic structure. The paper introduces Spherical Double K-Means (SDKM), which fills this gap by simultaneously co-clustering terms and documents under cosine geometry. Methodology: SDKM integrates SKM with Double K-Means (DKM), a framework that simultaneously partitions both rows and columns of a data matrix. Given a TF-IDF weighted term-document matrix X (N terms × J documents), SDKM seeks membership matrices U (N×K, terms to K clusters) and V (J×Q, documents to Q clusters) plus a centroid matrix Ȳ (K×Q) that maximizes the normalized cosine objective tr(X'UȲV') / sqrt(tr(X'X)·tr(UȲV'VȲ'U')). The algorithm performs block-coordinate ascent: alternately update U by assigning each term to the cluster with the nearest centroid (cosine), update Ȳ as the row-normalized block mean, update V by assigning each document to the nearest document centroid (cosine), and re-update Ȳ. Monotonic non-decrease of the objective is proven formally. Centroids are normalized by rows only (not columns) due to the inherent interdependence of the two clustering dimensions in co-clustering. A pseudo-F index adapted from Rocci & Vichi (2008) to spherical geometry guides selection of (K, Q). Multi-start with Rndst=20 random initializations eliminates local optima in practice. Key Results: In simulation, the pseudo-F index recovers the true cluster counts (K=4, Q=3) in 100% of 100 runs at low noise (ε=0.1), degrading to 13% at ε=0.5 and 0% at ε=0.9, where it tends to underestimate cluster counts. Partition recovery (500 runs) shows perfect ARI=1.0 for both U and V at ε≤0.50, declining to ARI≈0.48/0.35 at ε=1.75. With 20 random starts, local maxima frequency drops to 0% (vs. 71% with a single start). Runtime for 100 simulation runs averaged ~87 seconds on an Apple M1/8GB laptop. Applied to U.S. presidential inaugural addresses (59 documents, 1206 terms, 67.73% sparsity), SDKM identified K=3 thematic word clusters—"American Dream" (america, freedom, democracy), "Law and Order" (union, constitution, state), and "Politics, Economics, and Secession" (congress, law, business)—and Q=2 document clusters separating the pre-1865 founding era from the post-1865 modern era. Compared to DKM, the only document-level difference is Lincoln's 1865 address, which SDKM correctly assigns to the modern cluster by tracking relative word usage proportions rather than absolute term frequencies; word-cluster ARI between the methods is 0.225, reflecting finer distinctions in governance vocabulary. Against vMF mixtures, document-level ARI=0.932 (near-identical partition) and term-level ARI=0.360 (moderate divergence); vMF achieves slightly higher column ARI at high noise (0.912 vs. 0.840 at ε=1.10) but SDKM produces coherent biclusters with interpretable block-level centroids absent from per-mode vMF. On the 20 Newsgroups corpus (1278 terms, 2834 documents), SDKM cleanly separated religion/politics/sports from computing/for-sale content. Strengths: - Novel, principled integration of cosine similarity into a double k-means co-clustering framework, with a formally proven monotonically non-decreasing objective ensuring convergence - Computationally efficient via matrix operations; 100 simulation runs complete in ~87 seconds on consumer hardware, substantially faster than traditional SKM algorithms - Produces jointly interpretable term and document clusters linked through a shared centroid matrix Ȳ, enabling richer thematic analysis than single-mode clustering Weaknesses / Limitations: - The optimization is NP-hard and only converges to a local maximum; the pseudo-F index systematically underestimates the true number of clusters at moderate-to-high noise levels, requiring substantive judgment to override it - Hard (crisp) cluster assignments preclude representing terms or documents that naturally belong to multiple themes; the authors acknowledge fuzzy extensions as future work - Column normalization of the centroid matrix Ȳ is theoretically desirable but not enforced due to co-clustering interdependencies; while column norms remain empirically close to 1, this approximation lacks formal guarantees and could introduce subtle bias in highly imbalanced corpora

#80 — Moderately Innovative (53)

Regression Models Meet Foundation Models: A Hybrid-AI Approach to Practical Electricity Price Forecasting

Yunzhong Qiu, Binzhu Li, Hao Wei, Shenglin Weng, Chen Wang, Zhongyi Pei, Mingsheng Long, Jianmin Wang · arxiv.org · karypis - new results
Why this rank: FutureBoosting offers a clean and practical decomposition—delegating temporal modeling to frozen TSFMs and cross-variate correlation to lightweight regressors—with strong real-world deployment results. The approach is effective but methodologically straightforward: using one model's forecasts as features for another is a well-established stacking/ensembling pattern, and the gains over direct regression are modest (1-6%), suggesting the core contribution is more practical than conceptual.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Electricity price forecasting (EPF) is critical for energy market participants to optimize bidding, risk management, and resource allocation. However, electricity prices exhibit extreme volatility, non-Gaussianity, heavy tails, and non-stationarity — properties that make accurate forecasting exceptionally difficult. Real-world data from the Shanxi spot market in 2025 shows tail ratios (p99/p50) of ~5, excess kurtosis of ~5, and extreme price events (p > 1000) occurring ~5-6% of the time. Two dominant forecasting paradigms each have fundamental blind spots: time-series foundation models (TSFMs) excel at capturing temporal dependencies but underutilize cross-variate correlations and ignore future-available exogenous variables that are known at forecast time; regression models capture feature interactions well but cannot model historical temporal dynamics. No prior work bridges these two paradigms in a principled way for EPF. Methodology: The paper proposes FutureBoosting, a two-stage hybrid paradigm. Stage 1 uses a frozen, pretrained TSFM in zero-shot mode to generate forecasts of future-unavailable exogenous variables (e.g., system load, renewable generation) over the D+1 horizon. These TSFM-generated forecasts serve as augmented features encoding forward-looking temporal expectations. Stage 2 constructs an enriched feature set combining: (a) TSFM-generated forecasts from Stage 1, (b) future-available exogenous variables known at forecast time (e.g., weather forecasts, grid production plans), and (c) domain-knowledge-guided constructed factors such as thermal auction space (effective thermal capacity headroom) and renewable ratio (proportion of renewables in the generation mix). A lightweight tree-based regressor — LightGBM for the Chinese Shanxi dataset, linear regression for the European RealE benchmark — is trained on this enriched feature set to produce final price forecasts. The framework is model-agnostic: any TSFM (Chronos2, TimerXL, Sundial, TiRex, Moirai2, TimesFM) and any regressor can be plugged in. TSFM inference is cached to disk, making subsequent runs CPU-only with negligible memory overhead. Key Results: On the Shanxi dataset (12-month rolling evaluation, 2025), FutureBoosting reduces MSE/MAE by 45.43%/32.40% for day-ahead and 39.27%/24.98% for real-time relative to zero-shot TSFM baselines. Relative to direct regression on exogenous variables (LightGBM), FutureBoosting improves day-ahead MSE/MAE by 1.80%/4.56% and real-time by 6.17%/2.97%. The best single configuration (TimerXL + FutureBoosting) achieves day-ahead MAE of 94.78 and real-time MAE of 117.10. On the RealE European benchmark, FutureBoosting improves over zero-shot TSFMs by 16.92%/8.41% MSE/MAE for France and 36.52%/18.52% for Germany. FutureBoosting also outperforms Chronos2 fine-tuned with LoRA on the MAE metric (94.90 vs. 83.71 MAE for LoRA fine-tuning, with MSE of 34344 vs. 33335) while requiring only a single RTX 4090 GPU and ~152 seconds per monthly window, versus 4× RTX 4090 GPUs and ~8867 seconds for LoRA fine-tuning. SHAP analysis confirms that TSFM-derived features consistently rank among top predictors, validating their contribution. Strengths: - Principled paradigm that cleanly separates temporal generalization (delegated to TSFMs) from cross-variate correlation modeling (delegated to regression), with each component doing what it does best - Extremely lightweight and practical: TSFM inference is cacheable, making the framework CPU-only after the first run with only ~2 GB RSS, deployable on resource-constrained industrial IoT servers - Model-agnostic and plug-and-play: validated across six TSFMs and multiple regressors; improvements are consistent regardless of the backbone TSFM chosen - Strong interpretability via SHAP, with instance-level explanations showing how TSFM-derived and domain features drive corrections in both low-price and high-price extreme regimes - Real-world deployment validation on Xingzhixun's IoTDB production server in December 2025 Weaknesses / Limitations: - The framework relies on the availability of high-quality future-available exogenous variables (e.g., grid production plans, weather forecasts); performance may degrade in markets where such variables are scarce or unreliable - Feature set construction (thermal auction space, renewable ratio) requires domain expertise specific to electricity markets, limiting out-of-the-box transferability to other forecasting domains without expert-guided adaptation - On the RealE benchmark, zero-shot TSFMs already outperform direct regression baselines, suggesting that in some market regimes temporal modeling dominates and the cross-variate enrichment offers smaller marginal gains; the best regressor choice (LGBM vs. linear) is dataset-dependent and not automatically selected

#81 — Moderately Innovative (52)

FlexServe: A Fast and Secure LLM Serving System for Mobile Devices with Flexible Resource Isolation

Yinpeng Wu, Yitong Chen, Lixiang Wang, Jinyu Gu, Zhichao Hua, Yubin Xia · arxiv.org · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: FlexServe competently solves the contiguous-memory bottleneck of TrustZone with page-granular Flex-Mem and adds practical multi-model scheduling for mobile agents. The On-demand Protection mechanism eliminating idle-period overhead is a clean engineering contribution. However, the core techniques (S2PT manipulation, time-multiplexed NPU, layered caching) are established virtualization and systems concepts applied to a new deployment context rather than fundamentally new ideas.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Device-side LLMs offer privacy and availability advantages over cloud-side alternatives, but deploying them on mobile devices introduces serious security challenges. Model weights (worth millions of dollars to train) and sensitive user data (chat history, screen content) are attractive targets for attackers who may compromise the OS kernel. ARM TrustZone is the standard hardware isolation technology for mobile devices, but existing TrustZone-based approaches suffer severe performance penalties due to inflexible resource isolation: secure memory must be physically contiguous (allocating 8GB takes up to 6.44s on a loaded device), and the NPU cannot be dynamically switched between normal and secure worlds. State-of-the-art mobile agent applications further complicate matters by invoking multiple LLMs in sequence, making efficient multi-model memory management critical. Methodology: FlexServe introduces two core hardware abstraction primitives built atop ARM virtualization extensions (EL2 hypervisor mode): Flexible Secure Memory (Flex-Mem) and Flexible Secure NPU (Flex-NPU). Flex-Mem is a page-granular secure memory abstraction—any unprotected memory page can be dynamically switched to a protected Flex-Mem page and back, eliminating the contiguous allocation requirement. A Flex-Mem Daemon process in the normal world coordinates allocation/reclaim with the untrusted kernel via mmap, while the Flex-Monitor (running at EL2/EL3) enforces protection by configuring Stage-2 Page Tables (S2PT) and SMMU mappings. A Lazy Reclaim optimization defers zeroing freed pages until reuse. Flex-NPU operates on a time-multiplexing model: the NPU is switched into a protected mode exclusively for the secure world by remapping its MMIO region in the S2PT. An On-demand Protection mechanism disables S2PT overhead entirely when no secure resources are active. Building on these primitives, the FlexServe Framework (a TrustZone Trusted Application) provides: (1) a Secure Inference Pipeline that overlaps memory allocation, model loading, decryption, and NPU computation across consecutive layers to hide cold-start latency; (2) LLM-Aware Memory Management that organizes model weights in a layered cache with KV cache managed via a paged design similar to PagedAttention and cold KV pages spilled encrypted to flash; and (3) a Multi-Model Scheduler that prefetches the next model during the current model's decode stage, reclaims tail layers of model A while loading model B, and maintains a dynamic cache proportion policy for unknown workloads using LRU-based replacement. Key Results: Evaluated on a Rockchip RK3588 SoC (8-core CPU, 16GB RAM, 6 TOPS NPU) running Llama3 and Qwen3 series models (1.7B–8B parameters): Flex-Mem achieves 11.33× speedup over CMA for memory allocation and 12.6× faster reclaim. For single-model inference, FlexServe achieves an average 10.05× TTFT speedup over the CPU-only TrustZone strawman and 2.44× over the optimized strawman (Strawman-OPT) that already uses pipeline parallelism and secure NPU. Against the insecure normal-world baseline (NW-Base), FlexServe incurs only 4.41% average overhead. Under memory pressure (0–10GB background load), FlexServe maintains stable TTFT with up to 3.05× advantage over Strawman-OPT. For multi-model benchmarks with a 4GB cache constraint, FlexServe achieves 8.84× speedup over Strawman and 2.53× over Strawman-OPT. Across 10 real-world agent workflow scenarios, end-to-end response latency improves up to 24.30× over Strawman and 4.05× over Strawman-OPT. Virtualization overhead on SPEC CPU benchmarks is 2.46%, fully eliminated by On-demand Protection. Strengths: - The page-granular Flex-Mem abstraction elegantly solves the fundamental contiguous-memory bottleneck of TrustZone without requiring hardware modifications, enabling flexible LLM-specific memory management optimizations (layered caching, paged KV cache, lazy reclaim) that are impossible with CMA. - The On-demand Protection mechanism cleanly eliminates virtualization overhead during idle periods, making FlexServe nearly transparent to normal-world workloads (SQLite latency increase only 1.31× vs. 3.27× for Strawman). - Multi-model scheduler with workflow-aware prefetching and dynamic cache proportion adjustment addresses the realistic SOTA mobile agent use case that prior work (including the concurrent TZ-LLM) ignores entirely. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The evaluation platform's NPU is not state-of-the-art (6 TOPS), so the performance gap between FlexServe and strawmen would likely widen with a faster NPU—making current results a conservative lower bound rather than representative of production hardware. - The system cannot protect normal-world clients from having their plaintext input/output stolen by a compromised kernel; only model weights and KV caches are fully protected, which is a fundamental limitation shared by all TrustZone-based TEE systems. - The prototype only supports Llama3 and Qwen3 series; while the authors claim generalizability, no evaluation is provided for other architectures (e.g., Mixture-of-Experts models) or GPU-equipped mobile devices.

#82 — Moderately Innovative (52)

Learning to Rewrite Tool Descriptions for Reliable LLM-Agent Tool Use

Ruocheng Guo, Kaiwen Dong, Xiang Gao, Kamalika Das · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: The paper addresses a practical and underexplored bottleneck—tool description quality—with a well-engineered curriculum learning pipeline that bridges trace-rich training to trace-free deployment. The large-scale SFT dataset with real API validation is a useful contribution, but the methodology relies on standard techniques (SFT, curriculum mixing, LoRA fine-tuning) applied to a new domain rather than introducing fundamentally new methods.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: LLM-based agents depend critically on the quality of tool interfaces—particularly natural language descriptions and parameter schemas—to correctly select and invoke APIs. Existing tool descriptions are largely human-oriented, often vague, incomplete, or missing critical constraints (e.g., required call ordering, valid parameter formats). Prior approaches to improving descriptions rely heavily on execution traces collected at inference time, which are unavailable in cold-start, privacy-constrained, or newly-deployed API settings. These trace-dependent methods also optimize each tool independently, preventing generalization to unseen tools, and incur high inference cost due to iterative exploration pipelines. Methodology: The paper proposes Trace-Free+, a curriculum learning framework for training generalizable tool description generators that work without execution traces at inference time. The approach has three stages: (1) Agentic Seed Tool Annotation & Filtering — an LLM-based annotator interacts with 9,640 real ToolBench API providers in an action-observation loop to assess API health and collect representative call examples; 107 healthy providers (with all-good endpoints) are selected as seed tools. (2) Dependency-Aware User Query Synthesis — multi-step queries requiring inter-dependent API calls are synthesized by prompting an LLM with tool schemas and actual call histories, explicitly constructing dependency chains so queries cannot be solved by a single API invocation. (3) Two-Stage Tool Description Improvement — starting from original descriptions D0, a data-independent rewrite D1 is produced via best-practice guidelines; then execution traces from a tool-using agent are collected on the synthesized queries, and RIMRULE is used to extract generalizable failure rules that are incorporated into a final trace-aware description D2. An SFT dataset of ~6,900 queries across 5,576 tools is constructed. Qwen3-4B-Instruct is fine-tuned with LoRA on a curriculum that begins with trace-based examples and progressively shifts to trace-free examples, enabling knowledge transfer from trace-rich to trace-free inference. Both trace-free (no traces at test time) and trace-based (single-turn traces on test tools, but not test queries) inference settings are evaluated. Key Results: On StableToolBench (6 subsets, 49 categories), Trace-Free+ achieves an average subtask-level (SL) success rate of 70.1% and query-level (QL) of 54.0%, outperforming Trace-Free (67.8% SL / 51.6% QL), D1 (66.5% / 49.4%), D0 (67.3% / 48.0%), and EasyTool (63.5% / 43.7%). On cross-domain RestBench (TMDB and Spotify), Trace-Free+ achieves 88.1% SL / 74.9% QL on TMDB and 68.1% SL / 49.3% QL on Spotify, consistently outperforming all baselines. In the trace-based setting, Trace-Based achieves 70.3% SL / 54.6% QL overall on StableToolBench, the highest among all methods. In tool-level analysis, Trace-Free+ improves 6.8% of tools on F1 score with the best average delta (0.0211). Scaling experiments show Trace-Free+ is the most robust method as the candidate tool pool grows to 100+ tools, with the smallest degradation in query-level success rate. The curriculum mixing ratio of 10% trace-free in Stage 1 and 90% in Stage 2 is found optimal. Strengths: - Addresses a practically important and underexplored problem: tool interface quality as a bottleneck for agent performance, especially in trace-free deployment scenarios. - Curriculum learning design elegantly bridges the train/inference distribution gap, enabling knowledge transfer from trace-rich training to cold-start inference without requiring any test-time traces. - Large-scale, carefully validated SFT dataset (6,900+ queries, real API responses) with systematic schema correction enables clean, reproducible benchmarking and is a valuable research contribution in itself. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The approach is evaluated exclusively on RESTful APIs (RapidAPI/ToolBench ecosystem); generalization to other tool domains such as code execution environments, databases, or local function calls is asserted as future work but not validated. - Schema parameter fixing (Appendix B.2) is a significant preprocessing step required to make StableToolBench usable; this dependency on schema correction infrastructure may limit plug-and-play adoption on new benchmarks. - The fine-tuned description generator (Qwen3-4B) is evaluated only as a generator whose outputs are consumed by GPT-4.1 as the tool-using agent; the combined system's sensitivity to the specific agent model is not fully characterized.

#83 — Moderately Innovative (52)

ERASE--A Real-World Aligned Benchmark for Unlearning in Recommender Systems

Pierre Lubitzsch, Maarten de Rijke, Sebastian Schelter · arxiv.org · karypis - new results
Why this rank: ERASE fills a genuine gap by grounding recommender unlearning evaluation in practitioner-informed scenarios (sequential small deletions, domain-specific sensitive items) rather than artificial large-batch settings. The 600+ GB reusable artifact release and breadth of coverage (3 tasks × 9 datasets × 9 models) represent strong engineering, but the contribution is fundamentally a benchmark rather than a new method, and the key finding—that no existing algorithm meets real-world deployability thresholds—documents an open problem without advancing toward a solution.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Machine unlearning (MU) in recommender systems is critical for privacy compliance (GDPR right-to-be-forgotten), security (removing spam interactions), and liability mitigation. Existing benchmarks like CURE4Rec fail to reflect real-world deployment conditions: they focus exclusively on collaborative filtering (CF), simulate unrealistically large deletion requests (up to 5% of all interactions at once), ignore sequential unlearning dynamics, overlook domain-specific forget patterns (e.g., sensitive items for specific users), and treat efficiency as an afterthought despite practitioners reporting that retraining can take up to a day. ERASE addresses these gaps with a benchmark grounded in interviews with senior engineers at major European recommender platforms. Methodology: ERASE is a large-scale benchmark spanning three recommendation tasks—collaborative filtering (CF), session-based recommendation (SBR), and next-basket recommendation (NBR)—across nine public datasets (MovieLens, Food, RSC15, Books, 30music, NowP, TaFeng, Dunnhumby, Instacart) and nine state-of-the-art models (BPR, LightGCN, SGL, GRU4Rec, NARM, SASRec, SR-GNN, DNNTSP, Sets2Sets). The benchmark evaluates seven unlearning algorithms: three recommender-specific methods (SCIF, GIF, CEU) and four general-purpose methods from the NeurIPS 2023 competition (IDEA, Fanchuan, Kookmin, Seif), plus exact retraining as a reference. Two realistic unlearning scenarios are defined: (1) sensitive item unlearning, where a user's interactions with sensitive categories (e.g., alcohol, meat) are sequentially removed one user at a time, measured by Sensitive@k and RelItems@k; and (2) removal of poisonous/spam data, where injected adversarial interactions (1% of training set, 256 spam users) are removed in batches, measured by RelEff@k relative to retrained model nDCG@k. Unlearning requests account for only 0.01% of interactions per scenario, matching real-world scale. The benchmark generates over 600 GB of reusable artifacts including 1,069 pretrained model checkpoints, 769 unlearned model checkpoints, and experimental logs, enabling evaluation of new algorithms without re-running expensive training. Key Results: Effectiveness varies substantially across datasets, architectures, and scenarios—no single algorithm dominates universally. In sensitive item unlearning (RelItems@20): SCIF achieves +5.14 on Dunnhumby NBR (better than retrained), while general methods often score −10 to −50 (worse than retrained). GIF achieves +18.33 on Food CF but frequently diverges. In spam removal (RelEff@20): SCIF achieves +0.02 on TaFeng NBR; Kookmin achieves +0.49 NBR but degrades −42.34 on SBR. The recommender-specific SCIF is the most consistently robust method with lowest variance overall, exhibiting no divergences and maintaining utility across most settings. GIF and IDEA diverge frequently under sequential unlearning due to numerical instability in Hessian approximations. General methods (Kookmin, Seif, Fanchuan) work well for small numbers of requests but degrade as sequential batches accumulate. Efficiency analysis shows that most current algorithms fall short of real-world deployability: achieving a 3-orders-of-magnitude speedup over retraining (bringing latency to minutes) while maintaining effectiveness within 1% margin is the target threshold, and only SCIF consistently approaches this in some settings. Strengths: - Addresses concrete shortcomings of prior benchmarks with real-world motivated design choices (sequential small deletions, domain-specific scenarios, efficiency constraints validated through practitioner interviews) - Releases 600+ GB of reusable pretrained/retrained/unlearned model checkpoints, enabling the community to evaluate new algorithms without expensive GPU compute (13,000+ GPU hours consumed to generate artifacts) - Covers the broadest benchmark scope to date: 3 tasks × 9 datasets × 9 models × 7 unlearning algorithms × 2 scenarios × 5 random seeds, enabling systematic analysis of robustness, architecture effects, and algorithm generalization Weaknesses / Limitations: - The benchmark currently excludes exact unlearning methods (RecEraser, UltraRE) by design due to their training-set-size-dependent runtime, which may limit comparison with theoretical guarantees - Hyperparameter sensitivity is a persistent issue across most algorithms, and ERASE's published configurations may not transfer to new datasets or models without additional tuning effort; the benchmark does not prescribe a hyperparameter selection protocol for novel algorithms - The efficiency gap between approximate unlearning and real-world deployment requirements (3 orders of magnitude speedup needed) is identified but not resolved—no existing method consistently meets the deployability criterion across all tasks, leaving a critical open problem without a clear research path forward

#84 — Moderately Innovative (52)

{AdaCheck}: An Adaptive Checkpointing System for Efficient {LLM} Training with Redundancy Utilization

usenix.org · 2 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: Scores 52 as solid systems work with a well-designed tensor redundancy abstraction that generalizes across parallelism strategies, and the gradient-based incremental checkpointing cleverly exploits mixed-precision Adam's mathematical structure. However, checkpointing optimization for distributed training is a well-studied area, and while the reductions are large in absolute terms, the conceptual novelty is moderate—the core ideas of exploiting redundancy and incremental saving are natural extensions of existing approaches.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Large language model (LLM) training on thousands of GPUs is plagued by frequent hardware failures—LLaMA 3.1 training on 16K GPUs encountered 419 failures, roughly one every three hours. Checkpointing is the standard recovery mechanism, but existing systems are designed offline for specific parallelism strategies and model architectures. They fail to recognize that model states are heavily replicated across workers due to data, tensor, pipeline, and expert parallelism, causing them to save redundant copies unnecessarily. This inflates checkpoint size, limits how frequently checkpoints can be taken, and wastes GPU hours when rollback is needed. Existing systems also lack adaptability to new parallelism patterns (e.g., auto-generated by tools like nnScaler) and emerging architectures like Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) with multi-latent attention. Methodology: AdaCheck introduces the abstraction of *tensor redundancy*—a formal characterization of how each tensor in the model is distributed and replicated across workers under any combination of parallelism strategies. Using this abstraction, AdaCheck performs *offline redundancy utilization*: it categorizes model states into full redundancy, partial redundancy, and no redundancy, then constructs minimal checkpoints by storing only the intersection of parameter and optimizer state redundancy, avoiding saving states that can be recovered from another worker. To identify tensor redundancy efficiently without transferring entire tensors across workers (which would take over 30 days at 1,000+ workers), AdaCheck designs a *redundancy detector* using a hash-based consistency check combined with a ring-based communication algorithm. Hash values of tensors are packed and compared via two rounds of ring-allreduce-style communication, achieving accurate redundancy detection in under 3 minutes for 128 workers. For *online redundancy utilization*, AdaCheck introduces *gradient-based incremental checkpointing*: since mixed-precision Adam training computes half-precision gradients (size 2M for M parameters) each iteration, saving only gradients rather than full FP32 optimizer states (size 12M) reduces checkpoint size by 1/7. A remote CPU optimizer runs in parallel, updating full-precision states from received gradients without blocking training. The system is implemented atop PyTorch 2.0 with non-intrusive APIs compatible with DeepSpeed and nnScaler, and includes non-blocking full checkpointing for catastrophic failure recovery. Key Results: AdaCheck was evaluated on two clusters (32 A800 GPUs datacenter-class; 128 3090 GPUs commodity) across dense models (LLaMA-7B, LLaMA-30B, GPT-1.4B, GPT-7B) and sparse MoE models (DeepSeek-V2-Lite, GPT-MoE), with parallelism strategies including ZeRO-1/3, MiCS, EP, DP, and auto-generated parallelism. Checkpoint size is reduced by 6.00–896× compared to baselines (CheckFreq, GEMINI), with the largest reduction coming from cases with extensive parameter replication (e.g., ZeRO-1 and auto-generated parallelism). Checkpoint frequency increases by 1.46–111× over CheckFreq and 1.46–3.64× over GEMINI. Wasted time per failure is reduced by 12.1–88.93× compared to CheckFreq and 1.73–4.51× compared to GEMINI. Training throughput overhead is negligible (near zero), as checkpoint communication overlaps with computation via gradient-based incremental checkpointing. The redundancy detector completes in under 3 minutes for 128 workers, down from over 30 minutes without ring-based and hash-based optimizations. Offline redundancy utilization alone reduces checkpoint size by 1.30–240× versus GEMINI; online utilization adds up to another 7.09× reduction on top of that. Recovery probability exceeds 90% even in the worst-case simultaneous failure scenario for groups of size k=3 with n=32 workers. Strengths: - The tensor redundancy abstraction is genuinely general: it handles arbitrary combinations of DP, TP, PP, EP, ZeRO variants, MoE architectures, and even irregular auto-generated parallelism without any hardcoded assumptions, which prior systems all require. - The two-phase redundancy detection (hash-based consistency check + ring-based communication) is both practically efficient (sub-3-minute overhead at 128 workers, run only once at training start) and theoretically well-characterized with a formal time complexity analysis. - The gradient-based incremental checkpointing elegantly exploits the mathematical structure of mixed-precision Adam to achieve 1/7 checkpoint size with negligible recovery overhead by maintaining a shadow CPU optimizer, closing the gap to the theoretical 1S1C (one step one checkpoint) optimum. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The gradient-based incremental checkpointing has reduced reliability under simultaneous multi-worker failures: with group size k=3 and n=32 workers, 4 simultaneous failures can drop recovery probability below 90%, though the authors argue simultaneous failures at that scale are rare in practice. - The system is evaluated only with PyTorch-based frameworks (DeepSpeed, nnScaler); its integration overhead and compatibility with JAX-based or other non-PyTorch training stacks (common in some large-scale LLM efforts) are not addressed.
