#85 — Moderately Innovative (52)

A prescriptive generative AI maturity model for new product development processes

emerald.com · genai manufacturing - new results
Why this rank: The dual-maturity architecture enforcing NPD process discipline as a prerequisite for GenAI adoption is a meaningful conceptual contribution, and the LLM-powered prescriptive component generating budget-aware transformation roadmaps addresses a well-documented gap in the maturity model literature. However, maturity models are well-trodden ground, the four case studies are all Polish manufacturing firms limiting generalizability, and psychometric validation of both questionnaires remains incomplete.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Most existing AI maturity models are purely descriptive — they tell organizations where they currently stand but offer little actionable guidance on how to advance. They also lack cost-benefit analysis, alternative improvement pathways, and financial grounding, criticisms well-documented in the literature. Furthermore, no prior model conditions GenAI maturity assessment on a baseline level of New Product Development (NPD) process maturity, despite strong theoretical and empirical reasons to believe that disciplined development processes are a prerequisite for effective AI adoption. This paper addresses both gaps by introducing CLIMB2-OLIMP, a dual-maturity model that first assesses NPD process maturity before evaluating GenAI readiness, and embeds a prescriptive, LLM-powered component to generate budget-aware transformation roadmaps. Methodology: The model is developed using the Design Science Research (DSR) framework (Becker et al., 2009; Hevner et al., 2004), proceeding through problem definition, systematic literature review (13 articles from 494 initial results across Scopus, Web of Science, ACM, IEEE Xplore, and others), iterative model development, expert validation, and case study evaluation. The resulting model has two sequential components: (1) CLIMB2, an extension of the validated CLIMB maturity model adapted for NPD contexts, uses a 107-question questionnaire with strict level-locking scoring (all practices within a dimension must be met before advancing) across six dimensions — Activities and Flow, Decision-Making, KM Process, KM Techniques, Roles and Collaboration, Methods, Computerization and Software, Training — assessed on a five-point A–E scale; (2) OLIMP, a five-level GenAI maturity model (Organizing → Learning → Implementing → Managing → Pioneering) with eight dimensions — Technology and Infrastructure, Data, People and Competencies, Organization and Processes, Strategy and Management, Budget and Investment, Products and Services, Ethics and Regulations — assessed only if the organization achieves CLIMB2 level ≥ 2. The prescriptive component uses three LLM agents (OpenAI, Anthropic, Google Gemini) to evaluate questionnaire responses and generate alternative improvement pathways with explicit cost-benefit analysis for each target maturity level. The tool is open-sourced on GitHub. Four manufacturing organizations (two large multinationals, two Polish SMEs) served as case studies. Key Results: Across all four case organizations, NPD process maturity (CLIMB2 mean ≈ 2.86) consistently and substantially exceeded GenAI adoption maturity (OLIMP mean ≈ 1.93), confirming the theoretical proposition that NPD discipline is a prerequisite for GenAI readiness. The rank ordering was consistent across both models, with OrgB scoring highest on both dimensions. Expert evaluators rated the LLM-generated prescriptive reports at a mean of 7.25/10, comparing them favorably to outputs from major auditing firms like EY. Larger firms tended to favor multi-year transformation programs; mid-sized firms benefited more from stepwise plans targeting 2–3 priority areas, particularly People and Competencies and Organization and Processes. Purely hierarchical (threshold-based) scoring was found to conceal strong practices when a single low-scoring item blocked advancement; augmenting with weighted averages improved diagnostic clarity. Strengths: - Novel dual-maturity architecture that enforces NPD process discipline as a prerequisite for GenAI scaling, introducing a theoretically grounded sequencing logic absent from all prior AI maturity models. - Genuinely prescriptive: the LLM-powered tool generates multiple alternative transformation pathways per dimension, each with explicit cost-benefit and risk estimates, directly addressing the most cited gap in the maturity model literature. - Theory-grounded and empirically validated via DSR methodology, systematic literature review, nine-expert validation panel, and four organizational case studies — making it significantly more rigorous than most existing models. Weaknesses / Limitations: - All four case organizations are from the manufacturing sector in Poland or with Polish divisions, limiting generalizability to service industries, software-native firms, and non-European contexts. - Full psychometric validation (reliability and validity testing) of both the CLIMB2 and OLIMP questionnaires remains incomplete; budget estimates in prescriptive outputs are indicative and require local calibration. - The strict level-locking scoring rule (a single low-scoring practice blocks advancement) can produce misleading results by masking otherwise strong organizational capabilities, a tension the authors acknowledge but only partially resolve with hybrid scoring suggestions.

#86 — Moderately Innovative (52)

Investigation of Bcl-2 3'UTR-driven Plant Stress Tolerance Through MicroRNA and RNA Interference

eprints.qut.edu.au · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: Multi-level integration of transgenic phenotyping, NGS sRNA profiling, and transcriptomics to characterize a cross-kingdom non-coding regulatory mechanism is methodologically thorough. The custom bioinformatic pipeline achieving 34/34 miRNA prediction accuracy is notable. However, the mechanistic link remains correlational, and confinement to a model plant limits translational significance.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Climate change threatens global food security by intensifying drought and salinity stress on crops. While prior work showed that transgenic plants expressing the human B-cell lymphoma 2 (Bcl-2) 3' Untranslated Region (3'UTR) exhibit enhanced abiotic and biotic stress tolerance, the molecular mechanisms remained entirely uncharacterized. Because the 3'UTR lacks protein-coding capacity, its regulatory influence is hypothesized to arise from post-transcriptional RNAi mechanisms — specifically, the production of small RNAs (sRNAs) and microRNAs (miRNAs) that modulate endogenous plant stress-response genes. This PhD thesis investigates those mechanisms systematically, filling a critical gap between observed phenotype and molecular explanation. Methodology: Stable transgenic Nicotiana benthamiana lines were generated by Agrobacterium tumefaciens-mediated leaf disc transformation using the binary vector pOPT-EBX, in which 713 bp of the human Bcl-2 3'UTR was placed under the constitutive CaMV 35S promoter. Thirteen independent T0 lines were screened by PCR and Southern blot (EcoRI and PvuII double digestion), yielding four single-copy insertion events; two of these (lines 23 and 26) were confirmed by RT-PCR to transcribe the Bcl-2 3'UTR and selected for phenotypic analysis. Stress phenotyping used: (1) a floating leaf disc assay with 150 mM NaCl plus quantitative chlorophyll extraction to assess salinity tolerance; and (2) a controlled pot-based water deficit experiment (maintained at 10% volumetric water content for 2 weeks) measuring leaf relative water content (RWC), CO₂ assimilation, electrolyte leakage rate, and Evans Blue dye uptake. Mechanistic investigation employed next-generation sequencing (NGS) of total small RNA populations from stressed transgenic and non-transgenic plants, combined with a custom bioinformatic pipeline: the Bcl-2 3'UTR was fragmented in silico, secondary structures were predicted via the Vienna RNA Package (minimum free energy algorithm), candidate stem-loops were aligned to miRBase, and putative target genes in N. benthamiana were identified via psRNATarget. Differential gene expression (DGE) and Gene Ontology (GO) enrichment were performed on mRNA-seq data. A third experimental chapter used Agrobacterium-mediated transient GFP expression to probe the Bcl-2 3'UTR as a potential plant transcriptional terminator and transformation efficiency enhancer. Key Results: Transgenic lines 23 and 26 showed significantly enhanced salinity tolerance (P<0.05): leaf discs retained higher total chlorophyll (~56–58 µg/well vs ~54 µg/well for WT) under 150 mM NaCl. Under water deficit, line 26 maintained significantly higher leaf RWC (median ~83%) compared to WT (~68%; p<0.05). Plasma membrane integrity was markedly better in transgenics: line 26 showed electrolyte leakage reduced to half the WT rate (P<0.05) and Evans Blue dye uptake of 2.65 nmol/g vs 3.00 nmol/g in WT (P<0.05). CO₂ assimilation was equally suppressed in both transgenics and controls under water deficit (~0.7 µmol m⁻² s⁻¹), indicating that water retention benefits did not translate to photosynthetic advantage. NGS confirmed 197 distinct sRNAs derived from the Bcl-2 3'UTR in transgenics. Prior in silico prediction had identified 34 candidate miRNAs, all of which matched or nearly matched NGS results (with minor length discrepancies in nine). The Bcl-2 3'UTR did not strongly perturb the endogenous sRNA pool via endogenous target mimicry (eTM). DGE and GO analysis revealed upregulation of pro-survival pathways (ER quality control/ERQC, unfolded protein response/UPR, macroautophagy) and downregulation of pro-death pathways (apoptotic-like PCD, vacuole-dependent cell death). Regarding biotechnology applications, the Bcl-2 3'UTR functioned as a weak transcriptional terminator alone; placed upstream of the NOS terminator it disrupted terminator function, likely due to complex secondary structures triggering RNAi. Co-infiltration of a Bcl-2 3'UTR construct alongside a GFP expression vector significantly boosted GFP levels (p<0.05 vs WT), likely by alleviating Agrobacterium-induced cell stress. Strengths: - Multi-level experimental design integrating stable transgenic phenotyping, NGS-based sRNA profiling, and transcriptome-wide DGE to holistically characterize a complex regulatory non-coding element - Bioinformatic miRNA prediction pipeline built from first principles (no existing tool handles cross-species UTR-to-miRNA prediction) achieved high accuracy: 34/34 candidates confirmed by subsequent NGS - Use of two independent single-copy transgenic lines (23 and 26) with multiple complementary phenotyping assays (chlorophyll retention, RWC, electrolyte leakage, Evans Blue staining, CO₂ assimilation) strengthens causal claims Weaknesses / Limitations: - Study confined to a single model plant (N. benthamiana); direct translation to crop species relevant to food security (wheat, rice, maize) remains unvalidated - The mechanistic link between specific Bcl-2 3'UTR-derived miRNAs and the observed ERQC/UPR/autophagy transcriptional reprogramming is correlational — individual miRNA knockout or target validation experiments were not performed - Bcl-2 3'UTR did not improve photosynthetic rate under water deficit despite better water retention, suggesting partial and incomplete stress tolerance; yield-level field performance under realistic drought scenarios was not assessed

#87 — Moderately Innovative (51)

AutothinkRAG: Complexity-Aware Control of Retrieval-Augmented Reasoning for Image-Text Interaction

Jiashu Yang, Chi Zhang, Abudukelimu Wuerkaixi, Xuxin Cheng, Cao Liu, Ke Zeng, Xu Jia, Xunliang Cai · arxiv.org · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: The empirical observation that VLMs exhibit weaker logical reasoning than LLMs is interesting and the DPR architecture directly targets this gap, but the complexity-aware routing concept is well-trodden territory in adaptive inference. The framework stacks six models in a sequential pipeline that represents engineering integration rather than methodological innovation, and evaluation on only two benchmarks with unexplored router failure modes limits the contribution.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Information-intensive Document Question Answering (DocQA) over heterogeneous multimodal documents (PDFs, financial reports, technical diagrams) is hampered by long contexts and information overload. Existing multimodal GraphRAG frameworks suffer from two critical flaws: (1) Retrieval Rigidity — static retrieval strategies that apply uniformly large models regardless of query complexity, wasting compute; and (2) Reasoning Deficit — end-to-end VLMs exhibit significantly weaker logical reasoning than pure LLMs, producing errors of the form "correct visual recognition but incorrect answer generation." The paper empirically validates this gap: VLMs score only 27.7% overall on DocBench vs. 44.8% for LLMs, and 11.6% vs. 27.8% on MMLongBench. Methodology: AutoThinkRAG is a multi-model collaborative framework built around two innovations. First, the Query Complexity Router (QCR) uses a lightweight 3B Small Language Model (Qwen2.5-VL-3B) to analyze each incoming query along three feature axes — semantic intent, element count (entities, visual references, constraints), and cross-chunk dependency — and assigns a complexity label (Simple/Moderate/Complex) along with routing instructions. This enables adaptive selection between standard graph retrieval and hypergraph retrieval, avoiding unnecessary large-model invocations. Second, the Decomposition of Perception and Reasoning (DPR) architecture explicitly separates visual interpretation from logical inference: a small-scale VLM (Qwen2.5-VL-7B) acts as a zero-shot visual translator, converting retrieved image/table assets into dense structured text descriptions; a high-capacity LLM (Qwen3-32B-Instruct) then performs all logical deduction over this textualized evidence. The knowledge base is built via MinerU-based parsing into a hybrid Graph Knowledge Base (entity-relation graph with hard-matching disambiguation) plus a dense vector store (Qwen3-embedding-8B, 1024-dim, with bge-reranker-v2-m3 reranking). Text is chunked with 1,200-token sliding windows (100-token overlap); multimodal assets receive generated semantic descriptions concatenated with captions before information extraction. Key Results: On DocBench, AutoThinkRAG achieves 82.13% overall accuracy, outperforming the direct-VLM baseline (78.02%), the no-router ablation (79.37%), and RAGAnything (75.47%). The largest gain appears on Unanswerable queries: +28.45% over RAGAnything (81.25% vs. 52.80%), demonstrating strong hallucination suppression — the router detects information insufficiency and refuses to answer rather than fabricating responses. On MMLongBench (long-context multi-page documents), AutoThinkRAG achieves 51.29% overall vs. 44.86% for the baseline (+6.43%), with the largest gains in Administration (+10.34%) and Finance (+9.99%) domains. Ablations confirm both modules contribute: removing the router drops accuracy by ~2.76% overall, and replacing DPR with standalone VLM reasoning drops accuracy substantially, with the gap growing with document length (Figure 3 shows LLM+VLM consistently beating VLM-only across all page-range buckets from 0–50 pages to 200+). Strengths: - The VLM/LLM decoupling is empirically well-motivated: Table 1 provides a clear quantitative rationale, and the DPR design directly targets the identified gap rather than relying on a single monolithic model. - The complexity-aware router achieves resource efficiency without sacrificing accuracy — the 3B SLM router enables on-demand allocation, reducing hypergraph invocations for short documents while scaling up for complex multi-hop queries, as shown in the routing distribution analysis (Figure 2). - Strong performance on Unanswerable queries (+28.45%) is a practically important result, demonstrating that the framework can recognize when sufficient information is absent rather than hallucinating, a critical property for real-world deployment. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The framework stacks multiple models (3B router, 8B extractor, 8B embedder, reranker, 7B VLM, 32B LLM) in a sequential pipeline, creating significant latency and infrastructure complexity; the authors acknowledge this as a limitation and leave efficient coupling of parsing and encoding to future work. - Evaluation is limited to two benchmarks (DocBench and MMLongBench); no ablation studies measure the sensitivity of the routing classification accuracy itself (e.g., how often the SLM misclassifies query complexity and what the downstream accuracy cost is), leaving the router's failure modes unexplored.

#88 — Moderately Innovative (51)

OptiLeak: Efficient Prompt Reconstruction via Reinforcement Learning in Multi-tenant LLM Services

Longxiang Wang, Xiang Zheng, Xuhao Zhang, Yao Zhang, Ye Wu, Cong Wang · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: OptiLeak optimizes an existing attack vector (KV cache timing side channels) rather than discovering a new one. The automated DPO annotation scheme using token prediction likelihood is a clever mechanism, and the finding that small optimized models outperform large unoptimized ones is useful. But the contribution is fundamentally an efficiency improvement on a known attack, not a new paradigm.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Multi-tenant LLM serving frameworks (vLLM, SGLang) share Key-Value (KV) caches across user requests to improve inference efficiency. This creates a timing-based side channel: an adversary can probe whether their crafted queries hit the cache to infer other users' private prompts. Prior work identified this attack surface but focused on expanding attack vectors rather than optimizing attack efficiency, requiring impractically large numbers of requests and thereby underestimating the true privacy risk. The gap motivates a focused study on maximizing attacker capability through model optimization rather than broadening the attack surface. Methodology: OptiLeak is a two-stage fine-tuning framework for an adversarial local model that generates candidate token guesses. Stage 1 applies Supervised Fine-Tuning (SFT) on a domain-specific auxiliary dataset to initialize the model with domain vocabulary and query structure. Stage 2 applies Direct Preference Optimization (DPO) with an automated annotation scheme: the SFT model's token prediction likelihoods are used to identify "hard tokens"—domain-specific terms that are difficult to predict but carry sensitive information (likelihood rank above threshold γ). For each hard token, the actual token forms the preferred completion (s^win) and the SFT model's greedy-decoded alternative forms the dispreferred completion (s^lose), constructing preference pairs without manual labeling. The attack itself operates token-by-token: the adversary sends batches of candidate queries exploiting the Longest Prefix Match (LPM) scheduling policy of SGLang, observing Time-To-First-Token (TTFT) to detect cache hits and recover victim tokens one at a time. Key Results: Evaluated on MedQA, PubMedQA (medical), and FinanceBench (financial) datasets across Qwen-2.5 (3B, 7B, 14B) and Llama-3.1-8B models, OptiLeak achieves up to 12.48× reduction in Average Requests Per Token (ARPT) compared to the base LLM baseline. On MedQA with Qwen-2.5-3B-Instruct, OptiLeak reduces ARPT from 41.99 (base) to 21.60, versus 26.37 for SFT alone—an 18.1% further reduction over SFT. On FinanceBench, OptiLeak achieves ARPT of 19.29 versus 20.81 for SFT and 240.81 for base. Attack Success Rate at 1000 requests (ASR₁₀₀₀) reaches 98–100% across most settings. Notably, smaller optimized models (3B) consistently outperform larger unoptimized ones (14B), with Qwen-2.5-3B-Instruct achieving the lowest ARPT and highest ASR across benchmarks. Robustness experiments show that even with mismatched training domains, OptiLeak achieves 27.9% ARPT reduction over base; only completely unrelated domain training degrades performance. Strengths: - Novel automated DPO annotation mechanism eliminates manual labeling by using token prediction likelihood as an implicit reward signal, enabling scalable preference alignment for domain-specific attack optimization. - Comprehensive empirical validation across three datasets, four model scales, and multiple distribution-shift scenarios, demonstrating consistent gains and surfacing the counterintuitive finding that targeted small models outperform large unoptimized ones. - Dual-use framing as a proactive defense tool: OptiLeak can be deployed by service providers to simulate attacks and evict high-risk cache entries before real adversaries exploit them, with negligible overhead since lightweight 3B attacker models run independently of the production model. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The attack assumes the adversary has domain knowledge about victim queries (e.g., the general topic area), which may not hold in fully general multi-tenant deployments where victim query domains are unknown. - Evaluation relies on the SGLang LPM scheduling policy as the cache-hit signal; effectiveness on other scheduling mechanisms (FCFS, priority-based) is argued theoretically but not empirically validated, leaving open questions about real-world generalizability across diverse inference stacks.
Recommended 2602.20595 PDF
#89 — Moderately Innovative (51)

GP2F: Cross-Domain Graph Prompting with Adaptive Fusion of Pre-trained Graph Neural Networks

Dongxiao He, Wenxuan Sun, Yongqi Huang, Jitao Zhao, Di Jin · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: GP2F provides solid theoretical grounding (MSE minimization framework, Theorem 3.4) for why dual-branch fusion works in cross-domain graph adaptation, which is more principled than most GPL work. The adapter-based design is parameter-efficient and practical. However, dual-branch architectures and lightweight adapters are well-explored techniques in NLP and vision, and the transfer to graphs, while competent, represents standard methodological adaptation rather than a conceptual leap.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Graph Prompt Learning (GPL) has emerged as a paradigm for adapting pre-trained GNNs to downstream tasks, but recent work has shifted focus to cross-domain scenarios where source and target graphs differ substantially in data distribution. The key open question is *why* GPL methods remain effective under such distribution shifts. Empirically, the authors observe that two simple baselines—Linear Probing (LP, frozen encoder + linear classifier) and full Fine-Tuning (FT)—are often competitive with or superior to existing GPL methods in cross-domain settings, suggesting the effectiveness stems from the interplay of pre-trained knowledge and task-specific adaptation rather than from GPL's specific prompt mechanisms. Methodology: The paper first provides a theoretical analysis modeling the frozen GNN branch and an adapted branch as two noisy unbiased estimators of the true latent representation. Under a second-order error model, they prove via MSE analysis that an affine combination of the two branches achieves strictly lower estimation error than either branch alone (Theorem 3.4), with an optimal mixing weight λ* ∈ (0,1). This motivates GP2F, a dual-branch architecture: (1) a frozen branch using the pre-trained GNN unchanged to preserve source-domain knowledge, and (2) an adapted branch that runs the same backbone but with lightweight layer-wise adapters A^(l) inserted at each GNN layer—each adapter is a down-projection (d×r) followed by ReLU and an up-projection (r×d) with r ≪ d, plus a learnable scaling factor β^(l) for stability. The two branches are fused via a learnable scalar α: H_mix = α·H_pre + (1−α)·H_adp. Two auxiliary losses regularize the fusion: a cross-branch contrastive loss L_ctr treats the two branches as two views of the same graph and aligns them via InfoNCE with neighborhood-based positive pairs, and a topology-consistent fusion loss L_fus uses binary cross-entropy to align the mixed similarity matrix S_mix with the target graph's adjacency matrix A, ensuring structurally adjacent nodes remain similar in the fused representation. The overall objective is L = L_cls + λ1·L_ctr + λ2·L_fus. Key Results: GP2F is evaluated on 7 node classification datasets (Cora, CiteSeer, PubMed, Computers, Photo, CS, WikiCS) and 6 graph classification datasets (COX2, BZR, DD, PROTEINS, MUTAG, ENZYMES) in cross-domain few-shot settings (1-shot, 3-shot, 5-shot, 50-shot), with GNNs pre-trained using GRACE, DGI, and GraphMAE. In 1-shot node classification pre-trained on Cora, GP2F achieves 57.80% on Cora, 51.55% on CiteSeer, 53.05% on PubMed, 54.89% on Computers, 67.60% on Photo, 68.33% on CS, and 45.37% on WikiCS—best or second-best across all datasets, outperforming the prior state-of-the-art GraphLoRA-S by an average of 1.66%, with the largest gains on CiteSeer (+3.45%) and CS (+3.49%). In 50-shot graph classification pre-trained on PROTEINS, GP2F achieves best or second-best AUROC on 5 of 6 datasets, including 73.19% on MUTAG (+0.34% over best baseline). GP2F also outperforms Graph Foundation Models (GCOPE, MDGPT, MDGFM) on 1-shot node classification across Cora, CiteSeer, and PubMed (e.g., 64.62% vs. 46.84% for MDGFM on PubMed). On large-scale ogbn-arxiv and ogbn-products, GP2F achieves 68.76% and 76.91% respectively, outperforming all baselines including GraphLoRA-S (68.61%, 75.05%). Ablation confirms both L_ctr and L_fus contribute independently; removing either degrades performance across all datasets. Strengths: - Provides formal theoretical justification (MSE minimization framework, Theorem 3.4, Corollary A.2 on misclassification bounds) for why dual-branch fusion outperforms either branch alone, grounding the method design in principled analysis rather than empirical intuition. - The adapter-based adapted branch is parameter-efficient (r ≪ d) and avoids representation drift from aggressive fine-tuning, while the frozen branch preserves generalizable pre-trained knowledge—this balance is critical in 1-shot regimes. - Demonstrated robustness across three different pre-training strategies (GRACE, DGI, GraphMAE), both contrastive and generative, and across both node- and graph-level tasks, including large-scale graphs with millions of nodes. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The optimal adapter rank r requires dataset-specific tuning; the paper shows r varies significantly across datasets (smaller datasets prefer smaller r, larger prefer larger r), adding a non-trivial hyperparameter that may be costly to tune in practice. - The topology-consistent fusion loss L_fus is approximated on sampled subgraphs within mini-batches (O(B²d) complexity), which may not fully capture global structural consistency for very large graphs and introduces additional temperature hyperparameters (τ_ctr, τ_fus) that require separate tuning per dataset.

#90 — Moderately Innovative (51)

ChartEditBench: Evaluating Grounded Multi-Turn Chart Editing in Multimodal Language Models

Manav Nitin Kapadnis, Lawanya Baghel, Atharva Naik, Carolyn Rosé · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: ChartEditBench addresses a real gap by evaluating iterative multi-turn chart editing rather than single-shot generation, and the conversational protocol feeding each model's own outputs back reveals meaningful error accumulation dynamics. The hybrid evaluation combining programmatic assertions with grounded visual similarity is methodologically sound. The benchmark is entirely synthetic and matplotlib-specific, limiting generalizability to real workflows.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: While multimodal large language models (MLLMs) perform well on single-turn chart generation benchmarks, their ability to support real-world iterative data analysis workflows remains understudied. In practice, developers and analysts rarely create visualizations in a single step; they iteratively refine existing specifications across multiple turns, requiring models to maintain common ground, track prior edits, and adapt to evolving instructions. Existing benchmarks (ChartQA, PlotQA, MatPlotBench) address one-shot generation or question answering but fail to evaluate this incremental, stateful editing scenario. Additionally, standard evaluation approaches—LLM-as-a-Judge and CLIP-based similarity metrics—suffer from narrow score distributions, high variance, and inability to capture semantically meaningful visual discrepancies. Methodology: The authors introduce ChartEditBench, a synthetic benchmark for evaluating incremental chart code editing via two complementary tasks: (1) Code-to-Code Chart Modification, where a model receives original code and chart image plus a target modified image and must produce code generating the target; and (2) Natural Language Delta Modification, where a natural language instruction replaces the target image. Dataset construction uses a fully automated three-stage pipeline: initial chart generation (37 matplotlib chart types with controlled visual parameters), multi-turn modification generation (N=5 sequential turns with difficulty increasing from styling to structural to complex transformations), and VQA pair generation. The benchmark spans 35 modification types split between 17 style modifications (colors, typography, line styles) and 18 data modifications (filtering, aggregation, chart type conversion). Critically, the benchmark employs a conversational evaluation protocol where each turn uses the model's own previous prediction as input, exposing error accumulation effects. The evaluation framework combines execution-based fidelity checks, structured assertion-based scoring (instruction-following assertions and code quality assertions), and a grounded visual similarity metric using Chart-R1 (a Qwen2.5-VL fine-tune) to enumerate concrete visual differences under a fixed rubric. Instruction types are classified as programmatic (objectively verifiable, evaluated via assertion pass rates) or LLM-judged (semantic, evaluated via Chart-R1 on a 1–5 scale). Human verification was conducted on 400 uniformly sampled instances with two expert annotators. Key Results: Six models were evaluated: GPT-5-mini, Claude Haiku 4.5, Qwen3-VL (2B/8B/30B-A3B), and InternVL3-1B. Claude Haiku 4.5 achieves the highest overall score (2.187 on a 0–5 scale) with particularly strong visual similarity (1.937). GPT-5-mini leads on instruction following (1.623) but has weaker visual similarity (1.521). The open-source Qwen3-VL-30B-A3B achieves competitive overall performance (2.139) despite using only 3B active parameters via MoE. InternVL3-1B scores only 1.345 overall with instruction following of 0.480. All models show monotonic performance degradation across turns: Claude Haiku 4.5 drops 20% (2.46→1.97) from turn 1 to turn 5, while InternVL3-1B drops 33% (1.62→1.09). Visual similarity degrades the most steeply (~35% average drop) while code quality drops only ~8%, indicating accumulated errors primarily corrupt visual output rather than code structure. Style modifications (axis_style, series_label, axis_limit) score 2.2–2.8 for top models while data-centric modifications (rolling_average, data_transformation) score only 1.3–1.5 even for top models. Rolling average modifications exceed 80% low-score rates for most models except Claude Haiku 4.5 (58%). Proprietary models achieve 85–90% rendering success while open-source models range from 46% to 90%. Strengths: - The conversational benchmarking protocol—feeding each model's own predictions as input for subsequent turns—is a methodologically sound approach that reveals error accumulation effects invisible to single-turn evaluations, more faithfully reflecting real deployment conditions. - The hybrid evaluation framework combining programmatic assertion checking, execution validation, and grounded LLM-based visual similarity is more reliable and interpretable than pure LLM-as-a-Judge or CLIP metrics, with fine-grained failure diagnosis. - The difficulty-controlled data generation pipeline (37 chart types, 35 modification categories, 4 difficulty tiers) with rigorous 4-step code validation and human verification on 400 instances provides a well-calibrated, diverse benchmark. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The benchmark is entirely synthetic (matplotlib-generated), which may not capture the full diversity of real-world chart editing workflows involving different plotting libraries, enterprise tools, or domain-specific visualization idioms. - The reliance on Chart-R1 as the primary visual similarity evaluator introduces a dependency on a single model's quality and biases; while it outperforms CLIP-based metrics, its own failure modes (e.g., on novel chart types not in its fine-tuning data) are not thoroughly analyzed. - The fallback mechanism (reverting to last successful render when code fails) partially decouples instruction-following scores from the actual multi-turn degradation phenomenon, potentially masking the true severity of error cascades in models with poor rendering reliability.

#91 — Moderately Innovative (51)

Multimodal multi-instance learning for cardiopulmonary exercise testing performance prediction

nature.com · Fei Wang, FAMIA, FIAHSI, FACMI - new articles
Why this rank: The multi-instance learning reformulation for TTE studies is a sensible methodological contribution that properly mirrors clinical workflows, and the attention-based multimodal fusion with two-step training is well-motivated for small datasets. However, MIL and attention fusion are individually well-established techniques, and the contribution is primarily in their careful combination and application to CPET prediction rather than in fundamentally new methods.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Cardiopulmonary exercise testing (CPET) is the gold standard for assessing functional capacity and predicting survival in heart failure (HF), which affects ~7 million Americans with prevalence projected to exceed 10 million by 2040. Peak oxygen consumption (peak VO₂), a critical CPET-derived metric, stratifies patients for advanced therapies such as heart transplantation or LVAD. However, CPET requires specialized equipment, trained personnel, and dedicated infrastructure not widely available in community or rural settings. Prior AI work (Alishetti et al.) demonstrated feasibility of predicting CPET outcomes from transthoracic echocardiography (TTE) and EHR data, but used a simple ensemble of independently trained unimodal models and a single-instance learning paradigm that ignored the multi-instance structure of TTE studies and cross-modal interactions. Methodology: The authors propose a multimodal multi-instance learning (MIL) framework. Each TTE study contains multiple imaging modalities—2D cine loops, M-Mode recordings, and Spectral Doppler—each with multiple samples per patient. Rather than treating each image/video as an independent sample (single-instance paradigm), a separate MIL module is constructed for each imaging branch, aggregating information across all instances within a modality to produce a single modality-specific patient representation. For structured EHR data, TabPFN (a tabular foundation model) is used to extract feature representations. The modality-specific representations are integrated via attention-based multimodal fusion to form a comprehensive patient-level embedding. Training uses a two-step strategy: modality-specific branches are trained and frozen first, then the multimodal fusion module is trained on top—empirically superior to end-to-end training given the limited dataset size. An additional patient embedding regularization term inspired by proximal regularization (Wu et al. 2018) is applied during training to stabilize representations and improve generalization. Model interpretability is provided through HiResCAM saliency maps for imaging branches and permutation importance scores for EHR features. The dataset comprises 1,000 development patients from one NYP-affiliated hospital and 127 external validation patients from three additional sites, with a 64%–16%–20% train/val/test split. Key Results: On the held-out development test set, the full multimodal MIL model achieves R² = 0.603 for peak VO₂ regression (vs. R² = 0.529 for prior work; RMSE 4.853 vs. 5.280; MAE 3.667 vs. 4.021) and AUROC = 0.849 for high-risk patient identification (peak VO₂ ≤ 14 mL/kg/min threshold) vs. AUROC = 0.836 prior. On the external validation cohort (no fine-tuning), the model achieves R² = 0.541 vs. 0.395 and AUROC = 0.870 vs. 0.797. Ablations confirm each modality contributes: 2D cine alone gives R² = 0.396/AUROC = 0.780; adding M-Mode + Spectral Doppler improves to R² = 0.469/AUROC = 0.804; EHR alone gives R² = 0.567/AUROC = 0.794; full multimodal fusion is best. The MIL reformulation alone improves per-modality R² substantially (e.g., 2D cine: 0.384→0.396; M-Mode: 0.208→0.263). Patient embedding regularization provides further gains (R² 0.585→0.603 internally; 0.493→0.541 externally). KNN evaluation on learned embeddings achieves AUROC 0.784–0.847 for high-risk classification, confirming clinically meaningful representation structure. Subgroup analysis shows consistent performance by sex but notable age disparity: younger patients (18–59) R² = 0.571 vs. older (≥60) R² = 0.249, attributed to dataset imbalance. Strengths: - The MIL reformulation properly mirrors clinical image interpretation workflows where clinicians holistically review multiple images, yielding more stable and informative modality representations compared to single-instance approaches. - Attention-based multimodal fusion explicitly captures cross-modal interactions, outperforming the naive score-averaging ensemble of prior work and demonstrating that complementary information exists across TTE modalities and EHR. - Strong external validation across three distinct hospital sites without fine-tuning demonstrates genuine generalizability, with larger performance gains over the baseline on the external cohort than internally. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Retrospective study design using patients already referred for CPET introduces confounding by indication; performance on unselected HF populations may differ, and prospective validation is needed. - All four sites are academic hospitals in the New York metropolitan area, limiting geographic and institutional diversity; generalizability to community or rural settings remains undemonstrated. - The external validation cohort is small (n=127), and the model shows notable age-related performance disparities (R² = 0.249 for patients ≥60), likely reflecting underrepresentation of older patients in training data and their higher comorbidity burden.

#92 — Moderately Innovative (48)

Improving TabPFN's Synthetic Data Generation by Integrating Causal Structure

Davide Tugnoli, Andrea De Lorenzo, Marco Virgolin, Giovanni Cinà · arxiv.org · karypis - new results
Why this rank: The paper provides a clear mechanistic explanation of why autoregressive tabular generators produce spurious correlations at collider variables, which is a useful insight. However, the proposed fix — conditioning on causal parents instead of all predecessors — is a straightforward application of causal reasoning, and the discovered-CPDAG variant fails to improve consistently. The approach requires no retraining but also represents a relatively minor modification to TabPFN's inference procedure.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: TabPFN (Tabular Prior-Data Fitted Network) is a foundation model for tabular data that generates synthetic samples autoregressively — each feature is conditioned on all previously generated features in column order. This ordering-dependent generation is fundamentally at odds with causal structure: when the column order conflicts with causal dependencies (e.g., a collider variable appears before its parents), the model conditions on descendants while generating ancestors, inducing spurious correlations. This is a critical problem for domains like healthcare and drug development where synthetic data must faithfully preserve causal effects for use in treatment effect estimation, clinical simulation, and privacy-preserving data sharing. While TabPFN averages over random permutations of the conditioning set at inference time to mitigate local order sensitivity, this does not fix the global ordering problem when features are generated in a causally incorrect sequence. Methodology: The paper proposes two complementary causal conditioning strategies for TabPFN's autoregressive generation. First, DAG-aware conditioning: given a known Directed Acyclic Graph (DAG) G, each variable x is conditioned only on its causal parents rather than all predecessors in the sequence — C_DAG(x) = {x_j : x_j → x in G} — with variables generated in topological order. Second, CPDAG-based conditioning for the more realistic setting where only partial causal knowledge is available: a Completed Partially Directed Acyclic Graph (CPDAG) is used, applying DAG-aware conditioning to variables with at least one directed edge and falling back to sequential conditioning for fully undirected variables. CPDAGs are either constructed as minimal (orienting only V-structures from the true DAG) or discovered via the PC-stable causal discovery algorithm. Experiments compare vanilla TabPFN (original column ordering), vanilla TopPFN (topological ordering), DAG-aware, and both CPDAG variants across three evaluation metrics: Correlation Matrix Difference (CMD, structural fidelity), k-Marginal Total Variation Distance (kMTVD, pairwise distributional alignment), and Nearest-Neighbor Adversarial Accuracy (NNAA, privacy preservation), plus Average Treatment Effect (ATE) preservation. Benchmarks include a custom 4-variable collider SCM, six Microsoft CSuite datasets (4–12 nodes, various causal structures), and a 38-variable SimGlucose T1DM simulator. Training sizes range from N=20 to N=1000 with 100 resampling iterations per configuration. Key Results: DAG-aware conditioning significantly improves CMD in 24 of 35 dataset-training size configurations vs. vanilla TabPFN with original ordering, with only 2 degradations (on CSS at N=20 and N=100). When both methods use the same topological ordering, DAG-aware still achieves 20 significant CMD improvements with no degradations, confirming that causal parent conditioning contributes independently of ordering. For ATE preservation, DAG-aware generation achieves 26 significant improvements across 42 configurations with 2 degradations vs. vanilla; on the custom collider SCM, it reduces absolute ATE error by approximately 1.23 units at N=20. Minimal CPDAG shows 15 significant CMD improvements and 3 degradations vs. vanilla; for ATE preservation, it achieves 23 significant improvements with 2 degradations. Discovered CPDAG (via PC algorithm) shows no consistent improvements and produces 4 CMD degradations, primarily on CMC where PC orients edges with low direction precision (≤0.19 at N=500). Topological column ordering alone improves CMD in 26 of 40 configurations and ATE preservation in 18 of 42 configurations vs. original ordering; reverse topological ordering predominantly degrades both. On the 38-variable SimGlucose dataset with partial causal knowledge, topological ordering improvements increase with training size, indicating the approach scales to higher-dimensional realistic settings. Robustness checks at σ=10^{-2} noise confirm benefits extend beyond near-deterministic regimes. Strengths: - Provides a clear mechanistic explanation of why vanilla TabPFN produces spurious correlations (collider conditioning during autoregressive generation) with controlled experimental validation using a custom SCM where ground-truth independence is known - DAG-aware conditioning is a lightweight modification requiring no retraining — it simply changes the conditioning context at inference time, making it immediately applicable to the existing TabPFN ecosystem - Evaluates both the idealized (full DAG known) and practical (partial CPDAG) settings, with the conservative CPDAG strategy gracefully falling back to vanilla conditioning for undirected edges to avoid propagating causal discovery errors Weaknesses / Limitations: - The CPDAG-based approach's effectiveness is highly sensitive to the quality and position of discovered edge orientations: incorrect orientations (as seen with PC on CMC) actively degrade quality, and direction recall rarely exceeds 0.32 across datasets and training sizes with PC-stable, meaning most edges remain undirected - The study focuses exclusively on ATE as the causal estimand; other interventional quantities (conditional ATEs, dose-response curves, counterfactuals) may respond differently to causal conditioning, and the framework has not been validated for downstream tasks beyond ATE estimation - The approach is evaluated only on TabPFN and not tested on other neural autoregressive architectures (e.g., TabularARGN), limiting generalizability claims about causal conditioning for autoregressive tabular generators more broadly

#93 — Moderately Innovative (48)

Metrics for Artificial Intelligence in Medicine: A Reference Resource

pubs.rsna.org · semantic integration ontologies - new results
Why this rank: A well-executed standardization effort that formalizes 207 AI performance metrics into a machine-interpretable OWL ontology with rich annotations, resolving genuine terminological ambiguity in medical AI evaluation. The breadth across modalities and task types is impressive, but the contribution is fundamentally cataloging and taxonomy construction rather than methodological innovation — it organizes existing knowledge rather than generating new insight.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: The effective integration of AI systems into clinical medicine requires comprehensive and transparent performance evaluation, yet the field lacks standardized, widely accepted metrics terminology. This gap undermines reproducibility, hinders clinical translation, and enables hidden biases to go undetected. As medical AI has expanded beyond simple binary classification to complex tasks spanning structured health data, medical images, audio signals, and unstructured text, the diversity of evaluation metrics has grown without corresponding standardization. Without a shared vocabulary, developers, regulators, purchasers, and clinicians cannot communicate effectively about AI system performance, and inconsistent reporting practices make cross-study comparison unreliable. Methodology: The authors developed a comprehensive, machine-interpretable taxonomy of 207 AI performance metrics as a module within the Radiology Ontology of AI Datasets, Models and Projects (ROADMAP). The taxonomy was constructed through systematic literature review starting from widely-used metrics (sensitivity, Dice coefficient, AUROC), expanded via PubMed/Google Scholar searches using AI/ML terminology, and augmented with snowball citation sampling. Metrics were additionally drawn from literature on LLM evaluation, fairness, readability, and model drift. The ontology is built on Web Ontology Language (OWL) and uses two anchor entities: *model performance metric* (ROADMAP:03642) and *performance criterion* (ROADMAP:04043). Metrics are classified into three structural types — 11 graphical metrics (e.g., ROC curve, Kaplan-Meier estimator), 3 matrix metrics (confusion matrices), and 193 scalar metrics (e.g., Brier score, F1 score). Each metric is annotated with preferred and alternate labels (including multilingual variants), formal definitions, mathematical formulas, numerical bounds, and citations to authoritative sources using RDFS, SKOS, and Dublin Core schema. Logical axioms link each metric to one or more of 18 performance criteria (classification, calibration, fairness, image segmentation, text analysis, model drift, etc.), enabling automated reasoning, completeness checks, and SPARQL queries. ROADMAP integrates with established biomedical terminologies including SNOMED CT and RadLex. Key Results: The taxonomy formalizes 207 metrics spanning graphical (11), matrix (3), and scalar (193) types, mapped across 18 performance criteria. Representative coverage includes classification metrics (accuracy, F1, Matthews correlation coefficient), calibration metrics (Brier score, calibration slope/intercept, expected calibration error), image segmentation metrics (Dice similarity coefficient, Hausdorff distance, average symmetric surface distance), text generation metrics (BLEU, ROUGE variants, BERTScore, METEOR, MAUVE), fairness metrics (equalized odds, demographic parity, counterfactual fairness, individual fairness), and model drift metrics (multimodal concordance index). The ontology resolves terminological ambiguity — for example, formally identifying "sensitivity" and "recall" as alternate names for *true positive rate*. The complete ontology is publicly accessible via the National Center for Biomedical Ontology (BioPortal). The taxonomy complements existing decision-tree tools (Metrics Reloaded, MIDRC-MetricTree) by providing the standardized underlying representation those tools reference. Strengths: - Breadth and comprehensiveness: 207 metrics spanning all major modalities and task types (classification, segmentation, regression, NLP, fairness, survival analysis, model drift) in a single machine-interpretable resource. - Machine-interpretable OWL formalization enables automated reasoning, SPARQL queries, completeness checking, and integration with broader biomedical ontology ecosystems (SNOMED CT, RadLex). - Rich annotations per metric — multilingual labels, synonyms, mathematical formulas, numerical bounds, and authoritative citations — directly address the terminology fragmentation problem and reduce semantic ambiguity. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The taxonomy is a reference resource rather than an empirical study; there are no quantitative validation experiments demonstrating that its adoption improves reproducibility or reduces reporting errors in practice. - Coverage of emerging LLM-specific and multimodal evaluation metrics may lag behind the rapidly evolving field; the ontology requires ongoing community curation to remain current, and a community submission platform is only planned, not yet operational. - Comparison with the Intelligence Task Ontology (ITO), which covers 1,995 AI-related properties, is acknowledged but not resolved — harmonization between ROADMAP and ITO remains future work, leaving potential redundancy and gaps unaddressed.

#94 — Moderately Innovative (48)

LUMAX: A LUT-Based Mixed-Precision Accelerator for LLM Inference on the Edge

dspace.lib.ntua.gr · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: The ¼-LUT strategy—storing only even-weight products and reconstructing odd-weight products via addition—is a technically elegant trick enabling 96% DSP reduction. The RISC-V RoCC integration with parameterized design space exploration shows good hardware/software co-design. However, the approach operates within a well-explored design space, and evaluation on a 15M parameter model limits practical relevance.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Deploying large language models (LLMs) on edge devices requires inference hardware that is simultaneously energy-efficient, flexible across precision configurations, and practically integratable into real processor ecosystems. While weight quantization reduces memory and compute costs, mixed-precision operations—where activations remain at higher precision (e.g., INT16/INT8) while weights are aggressively quantized (e.g., INT2/INT4)—remain inefficient on both general-purpose CPUs/GPUs and fixed-precision systolic array accelerators like Gemmini. Lookup Table (LUT)-based methods precompute products to eliminate runtime multipliers, but prior LUT accelerators suffer from excessive on-chip memory overhead (storing all possible products), limited hardware/software integration, and difficulty adapting to varying bitwidth combinations. LUMAX addresses all three gaps with a reconfigurable, fully integrated design. Methodology: LUMAX is a LUT-based mixed-precision GeMM accelerator implemented as a tightly coupled RocketChip Co-processor (RoCC) within the Chipyard/RISC-V ecosystem, developed in the Chisel hardware description language. Its core computational approach is a five-stage dataflow: (1) Load Activations — a window of activation elements is fetched via DMA over TileLink into an internal register file; (2) Product Generation — for each activation element, all possible products with every even weight value in the supported bitwidth range are precomputed using only additions (no multipliers) and stored in synchronous single-cycle read memories (SyncReadMem); (3) Load Weights — weight columns are streamed column-wise from external DRAM via ping-pong buffers; (4) Select & Accumulate — each weight value serves as an index into the precomputed product memory, retrieving the appropriate partial product and accumulating it into the output; and (5) Store Outputs — completed output vectors are written back via DMA. To reduce LUT memory overhead, LUMAX employs a quarter-size LUT (¼-LUT) strategy: only products for even weights are stored, and odd-weight products are reconstructed at runtime by adding the product of the nearest even weight to a scaled activation value, eliminating half the storage without accuracy loss. The architecture is highly parameterized via a hardware generator (x_slice, y_slice, Row Factor, bankMergeFactor, DMA_Ids), enabling design space exploration across memory block counts, parallelism levels, and DMA bandwidth. Variable precision is supported at runtime for activations (INT4–INT16) and weights (INT2–INT8), with memory blocks partitioned both vertically (rows for different weight precision depths) and horizontally (columns for multiple element packing at lower precision). A caching mode for low-precision activations precomputes a full joint activation-weight LUT, avoiding redundant product generation. The hardware is integrated with a C software API (preload, start, fence functions) that offloads control parameter computation to software, simplifying the hardware control path. Key Results: Prototyped on a Xilinx ZCU106 Zynq-based FPGA and evaluated against a Gemmini 4×4 PE systolic array baseline (the state-of-the-art open-source GeMM accelerator) using LLaMA2 inference on the TinyStories-15M dataset. LUMAX reduces FPGA LUT utilization by up to 33% and DSP usage by up to 96% (Gemmini consumes 197 DSPs even for a small 4×4 array; LUMAX uses only 8 DSPs regardless of configuration). In cycle-count comparisons for integer vector-matrix multiplication, LUMAX achieves up to 79% fewer cycles than Gemmini. End-to-end speedup reaches up to 4.7× on LLaMA2 for the (8-bit activation, 2-bit weight) configuration with XS=8 and Row Factor=4. Power consumption across all tested LUMAX configurations is consistently 0.3×–0.6× that of Gemmini, translating to up to 70% improved energy efficiency. Sensitivity analysis shows that reducing weight bitwidth from 8 to 2 bits provides the largest performance gain (up to 4.73× in some configurations), while increasing XS (number of memory blocks) and Row Factor provide diminishing returns once the design becomes memory-bound on DMA bandwidth. Strengths: - The ¼-LUT strategy is technically elegant: storing only even-weight products and reconstructing odd-weight products via a single addition nearly halves LUT memory with zero accuracy penalty, directly enabling the 96% DSP reduction over Gemmini. - Tight integration as a RISC-V RoCC coprocessor provides genuine hardware/software co-design, with a C API that handles control parameter computation in software—an unusually practical approach compared to standalone accelerator designs. - The parameterized generator enables systematic design space exploration across memory organization, DMA bandwidth, precision ranges, and parallelism dimensions, with a cycle-accurate analytical model validated against Verilator simulation. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Evaluation is limited to a tiny 15M parameter LLaMA2 model on a single forward pass workload; no end-to-end latency or throughput results on realistic large LLMs (e.g., 7B+ parameter models) are reported, making it difficult to assess practical deployment benefits. - The baseline comparison is exclusively against Gemmini; no quantitative comparison with other LUT-based accelerators (FIGLUT, LUT Tensor Core, CARAT) is provided, leaving LUMAX's relative advantage within the LUT-accelerator design space unclear. - High weight bitwidths (8-bit weights) substantially degrade LUMAX's performance advantage because product generation requires more cycles and the activation window shrinks, suggesting the architecture is best suited to very aggressive quantization (2–4-bit weights) rather than the INT8 regime common in deployed models.

#95 — Moderately Innovative (47)

KGHaluBench: A Knowledge Graph-Based Hallucination Benchmark for Evaluating the Breadth and Depth of LLM Knowledge

Alex Robertson, Huizhi Liang, Mahbub Gani, Rohit Kumar, Srijith Rajamohan · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: KGHaluBench offers a useful engineering contribution by dynamically generating hallucination evaluation questions from Wikidata and decomposing errors into breadth-of-knowledge vs. depth-of-knowledge hallucinations (Halu_BOK/Halu_DOK), which provides more actionable diagnostics than single-metric benchmarks. However, the core techniques—KG-based question generation, embedding similarity filtering, NLI-based verification—are standard components assembled in a straightforward pipeline, and the hallucination benchmarking space is already crowded. The finding that factual depth errors persist even in large models is informative but not surprising.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Large Language Models frequently generate hallucinations—plausible but factually incorrect content—that undermine their trustworthiness. Existing hallucination benchmarks suffer from two key limitations: they rely on static, pre-constructed question sets that quickly become outdated and fail to challenge advancing models, and they use closed or multiple-choice question formats that probe only surface-level knowledge. Additionally, most benchmarks collapse results into a single accuracy metric, obscuring which aspects of LLM knowledge (breadth vs. depth) are responsible for failures. KGHaluBench addresses these gaps by dynamically generating challenging, multifaceted open-ended questions grounded in a knowledge graph and providing decomposed hallucination metrics. Methodology: KGHaluBench consists of two core components built on top of Wikidata (~118 million entities). The Question Generation Module dynamically samples focal entities from the KG, balancing across entity types (Very Common, Common, Uncommon) to avoid popularity bias. For each entity, it retrieves KG triples, entity statistics, and a Wikipedia description, then constructs a compound open-ended question requiring the LLM to recall three specific relational facts simultaneously. Question difficulty is estimated via a modified sigmoid formula combining entity popularity and relation complexity (derived via a TPOT-trained ML pipeline), yielding a continuous difficulty score used to compute a fairness-adjusted Weighted Accuracy metric (W_a). The Response Verification Module employs a two-stage pipeline: an Entity-Level Filter first screens responses for abstentions and conceptual hallucinations using semantic similarity (Jina-Embedding-V3 cosine similarity) and lexical overlap (Fuzzy Set Ratio), combined 70:30; responses passing this filter then undergo a Fact-Level Check via a tri-stage pipeline (RoBERTa-Large-MNLI for NLI entailment → Llama-3.1-8B for LLM entailment → Mistral-7B expert decision), evaluating each of the three facts independently. This decomposition yields two novel hallucination rates: Halu_BOK (breadth-of-knowledge hallucinations caught at entity level) and Halu_DOK (depth-of-knowledge hallucinations caught at fact level). The entity-level filter achieves 79.19% agreement with human judgment; the fact-level pipeline achieves 87.74% agreement, outperforming GPT-3.5-Turbo as automated judge (79.18%) by 8.56%. Key Results: 25 frontier models (15 open-source, 10 proprietary) were evaluated across 10 runs of 150 questions each. GPT-5 achieves the highest W_a at 65.60%, demonstrating that even the best model answers correctly less than two-thirds of the time. Proprietary models substantially outperform open-source models (average W_a 55.94% vs. 48.32%), though GLM-4.5 (open-source, 335B) achieves 54.35% W_a, narrowing the gap. Halu_BOK drops dramatically from ~54.75% for small 8-32B models to ~11.91% for large proprietary models, indicating that larger models rarely fail at entity recognition. In contrast, Halu_DOK decreases only modestly (~24.59%) across the same range, revealing that even the strongest models struggle with precise factual recall. Approximately 39.74% of responses from larger models still contain at least one hallucinated fact. High abstention rates are identified as a confounding factor: Claude Sonnet 4, GPT-5-Mini, and GPT-oss-20B decline to answer 49.40%, 49.67%, and 55.93% of questions respectively, artificially suppressing their hallucination rates. Strengths: - Dynamic question generation from a live KG (Wikidata) ensures the benchmark remains current and scalable, addressing the staleness problem of static benchmarks - The decomposition of hallucination into Halu_BOK and Halu_DOK provides interpretable, actionable diagnostics distinguishing entity-recognition failures from fact-recall failures - The difficulty-weighted accuracy metric W_a statistically corrects for entity popularity bias, producing fairer cross-model and cross-assessment comparisons with reduced variance (std dev reduced from 2.57% to 2.45%) Weaknesses / Limitations: - Exclusive reliance on Wikidata limits coverage to KG-indexed entities, with uneven representation across topics, cultures, and languages (non-English and marginalized entities are underrepresented), and excludes domain-specific knowledge not captured in general KGs - The automated verification pipeline, despite outperforming GPT-3.5-Turbo judges, still makes errors (entity-level recall of 73.17% at the strictest threshold; fact-level pipeline at 87.74%), meaning some valid responses are rejected and some hallucinations are missed, requiring further improvements to soundness before the benchmark can serve as a fully reliable automated evaluator
Recommended 2602.19643 PDF
#96 — Moderately Innovative (47)

Perceptual Compute Offloading: A Hypothesis for Sub-Millisecond Robotic Perception via Hash-Indexed Spatial Kinematic Blueprints

iaeternum.ai · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: The dual-process architecture formalizing the semantic/sensorimotor split is a genuinely interesting design vision, and the zero-GPU System 1 critical path with hash-indexed SKB lookup is a creative departure from the dominant neural-network-for-everything paradigm. The paper is unusually rigorous for a hypothesis paper, with explicit falsification criteria. However, the complete absence of physical experiments means all claims remain projections, and the restriction to quasi-static template environments significantly narrows the applicability of the approach.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: State-of-the-art Vision-Language-Action (VLA) models achieve remarkable generalization but are fundamentally incompatible with real-time robotic control due to what the authors term the Inference-Control Discrepancy (ICD): RT-2 infers at 1–3 Hz, OpenVLA at 5–10 Hz, and even optimized architectures like π₀-FAST at 50–200 Hz, while industrial manipulation demands 500–1000 Hz control loops. The workaround of action chunking (predicting trajectory chunks at low frequency) introduces open-loop vulnerability, jerky transitions, and temporal inconsistency. The paper identifies that every high-performing robotic system independently converges on decoupling semantic reasoning from sensorimotor execution—a dual-process architecture—but does so ad hoc. PCO aims to formalize this architectural pattern into a principled, mathematically rigorous system. Methodology: PCO is a dual-process architecture consisting of System 1 (Fast Path) and System 2 (Slow Path). System 1 runs entirely without neural network inference: a Kinematic ROI Isolator performs depth-gated background subtraction and connected-component tracking (<0.5 ms total) to extract a clean crop of the target object, which is then hashed using a CompositeHasher computing five complementary perceptual hashes (64-bit dHash, 64-bit pHash, 256-bit pHash-256, 4×4 color hash, and 64-bit zHash from depth maps). The composite hash indexes a registry of Spatial Kinematic Blueprints (SKBs)—four-layer structured schemas encoding Provenance, Semantic Topology (object type, mass, friction), Affordance (13 canonical action primitives with SE(3) target poses and force profiles), and Safety (Control Barrier Function parameters, hazard classes, regulatory certifications). Registry lookup uses BK-Trees or Multi-Index Hashing for O(1) sub-millisecond retrieval. System 2 activates only on hash miss or low confidence, invoking full VLA inference (200–2000 ms) to generate a new SKB. Write-Back Augmentation then pre-computes hashes for ~30 geometric, photometric, and occlusion transformations (rotations ±15°, scale ±20%, specular perturbations, synthetic gripper silhouettes), mapping all to the same SKB, so subsequent System 1 encounters of the same object succeed immediately. Object-Conditioned Hash Ensembling uses inverse-variance weighting (computed during Write-Back) to adaptively trust the most stable hash types per object—automatically down-weighting specular-sensitive dHash on machined metal parts in favor of lighting-invariant zHash. Safety is enforced via Control Barrier Functions derived from SKB constraints and enforced at 1 kHz via a Quadratic Program safety filter at the RTOS level, providing formal guarantees that even a catastrophic false-positive hash match cannot cause unsafe motion. Key Results: This is explicitly a hypothesis paper with no completed physical experiments. All quantitative claims are projections: the authors expect <6 ms fast-path latency (ROI isolation + hash + lookup + verification) vs. 500–2000 ms VLA inference, representing a 97–99.7% latency reduction; System 1 hit rate >95% in a populated Template Environment after convergence; grasp success within 2% of the VLA baseline that generated the SKBs; visual convergence in ≤1 trial via Write-Back Augmentation; kinematic convergence in ≤8 trials via proprioceptive feedback and SKB versioning; and fleet-wide SKB propagation within 100 ms via the Zenoh pub/sub protocol. The paper provides a worked example: a bin-picking hash miss triggers System 2 (1835 ms VLA inference), generates a new SHAKE_BIN_HORIZONTAL primitive SKB with 20+ augmented hash keys; on the next entanglement, System 1 resolves it in 0.8 ms. The paper grounds its analogy in the Cognitive Nutrition experiment (prior work) showing that dense structured metadata (+160% semantic coverage, +8.9% CogBench) versus sparse metadata (−54.4% CogBench, +330% hallucination rate) on vision-language models validates the hash-to-metadata principle. Strengths: - The zero-GPU, zero-neural-network System 1 critical path is a genuinely differentiated design point: O(1) hash lookup runs on a $10 FPGA at <2W, enabling edge deployment on battery-constrained mobile manipulators where GPU-based Generation 2 VLAs (requiring RTX 4090-class hardware at 300+ W) are physically impractical. - The multi-layered safety architecture (geometric gating via point-cloud dimension validation, CBF-QP at 1 kHz RTOS, impedance compliance as mechanical fuse, proprioceptive pre-tensioning) provides defense-in-depth with a compounded false-execution probability of ~10⁻⁹, placing the system within SIL 2 thresholds—a certification pathway probabilistic VLA models cannot achieve. - The paper is unusually rigorous for a hypothesis paper: it provides explicit falsification criteria with quantitative thresholds (p₉₉ fast-path latency >10 ms, False Acceptance Rate >0.1%, grasp degradation >2% vs. VLA baseline each individually falsify the hypotheses), commits to publishing negative results, and releases the codex_lab_kit as open-source to enable external replication. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The paper presents zero physical experimental results—all performance numbers are projections or theoretical analyses. The core hypotheses (H1: ≥90% latency reduction with equivalent success rate; H2: visual convergence in ≤2 trials; H3: no novel failure modes) remain entirely unvalidated, making the paper more of a technical marketing document and collaboration solicitation than a research contribution in the traditional sense. - The architecture is explicitly scoped to quasi-static Template Environments where objects are stationary or slow-moving; the authors acknowledge it degrades to continuous System 2 operation for fully dynamic scenarios (tumbling objects, human handover). The proposed PCA canonicalization extension for SO(3)-invariant hashing is reserved for future work and is noted to be unstable for symmetric objects. This severely limits applicability to the high-entropy domestic robotics domain where VLA generalization is most valuable.

#97 — Moderately Innovative (45)

Improving LLM Performance Through Black-Box Online Tuning: A Case for Adding System Specs to Factsheets for Trusted AI

Yonas Atinafu, Henry Lin, Robin Cohen · arxiv.org · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: The paper's most valuable contribution is the empirical finding that speculative decoding monotonically degrades p99 latency and goodput under tight SLOs — a counterintuitive result with practical implications for operators. However, the core methodology is a straightforward deterministic hill-climbing controller with fixed step sizes, which is a well-understood optimization technique applied without significant algorithmic innovation. The evaluation on only small models (≤4B parameters) with synthetic single-prompt workloads substantially limits the generalizability and impact of the findings.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Interactive LLM serving is dominated by tail latency, yet default serving-stack configurations either underutilize GPUs or push systems past queueing thresholds where p99 latency spikes — harming a minority of users even while average metrics look acceptable. Existing tuning approaches target average throughput or require internal instrumentation; speculative decoding parameters are exposed as flags but never tuned automatically against an explicit SLO. The broader gap is that AI Factsheets — documents intended to certify responsible AI deployment — omit system performance and sustainability metrics entirely. Methodology: The paper introduces SLO-Tuner, a black-box online controller that maximizes *goodput* (throughput of requests satisfying a p99 SLO) using only end-to-end measurements, without instrumentation. It operates via deterministic hill-climbing over a small knob vector: client concurrency, max batch size (max_num_seqs), and speculative decoding width. The score function is S(K) = goodput(K) − λ·max(0, p99(K) − SLO) − hw_cost(K), with λ=5.0 penalizing SLO violations heavily over throughput gains. Neighbor evaluation uses fixed step sizes (concurrency ±2, batch ±3, spec width ±4, plus spec on/off toggle); the controller moves only when improvement exceeds δ=0.02 or when the current config violates the SLO. A co-designed lightweight discrete-event simulator models FCFS queueing, prefill/decode phases, and speculative decoding dynamics (draft width W, verifier cadence k) for cheap pre-deployment stress-testing and trend guidance. A thin adapter maps logical knobs to stack-specific flags, enabling portability across vLLM, MLX, TGI, and Triton without code changes. Key Results: On TinyLlama-1.1B served via vLLM on an NVIDIA L40S GPU under a 1.2 s p99 SLO, SLO-Tuner reduces p99 from ≈1.36 s to ≈0.70 s (49% reduction) while increasing goodput from ≈8.1 rps to ≈15.0 rps (85% increase) in 8 hill-climb iterations (~40–45 min total). The key finding is that speculative decoding is monotonically harmful for this SLO: spec width 0 achieves 13.87 rps at p99 0.77 s, width 8 drops to 9.77 rps at p99 1.21 s, and width 16 degrades to 4.53 rps with p99 2.00 s. Concurrency shows a sharp knee: goodput peaks around 8–10 threads then collapses (0.27 rps at 16 threads) as SLO violations dominate. Batch size benefits plateau around 11–13 sequences before queueing inflates p99 beyond 1.2 s. Simulator and live vLLM results agree directionally on knob rankings, though absolute latencies differ. A portability check on Apple Silicon (MLX, Qwen-4B) confirms the simulator correctly captures directional trends across hardware. Strengths: - Purely black-box approach (standard APIs only) enables deployment across serving stacks without modification, with a thin adapter pattern that cleanly separates controller logic from stack-specific flags - Exposes the counterintuitive result that speculative decoding — widely assumed beneficial — can severely harm p99 and goodput under tight SLOs, a practically important insight for operators - Simulator–live alignment strategy divides labor effectively: cheap trend guidance from simulation, correctness from online measurement, avoiding the cost of full grid search Weaknesses / Limitations: - Evaluated only on small models (TinyLlama-1.1B, Qwen-0.6B/4B) with a synthetic single-prompt workload and steady closed-loop arrivals — real deployments have mixed prompt lengths, heterogeneous request types, and time-varying burstiness that may invalidate the tuned configuration - First-order hill-climbing with 8 fixed steps can get trapped in local optima on non-convex SLO surfaces; no comparison against random search, Bayesian optimization, or bandit methods under the same segment budget is provided, making it hard to assess search efficiency - Requires restarting the vLLM server for every evaluation segment (not hot-swappable), making the approach operationally expensive and potentially infeasible under strict uptime requirements or multi-tenant production settings
