#112 — Incremental (37)

One Supervisor, Many Modalities: Adaptive Tool Orchestration for Autonomous Queries

Mayank Saini, Arit Kumar Bishwas · arxiv.org · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: The centralized orchestration framework is a competent systems integration effort combining known components (RouteLLM, YOLO, CLIP, LangGraph) under a single supervisor. The Couplet Framework's pairing of perceptual models with SLM coordinators is a reasonable architectural choice, but the conceptual novelty is limited—the contribution is primarily in engineering composition rather than methodological innovation.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Modern AI deployment faces a fundamental tension between handling diverse multimodal queries autonomously and maintaining cost-efficiency at scale. Monolithic LLM deployments (e.g., routing everything to GPT-4) incur prohibitive costs since most queries do not require frontier-model reasoning. Conversely, hierarchical routing systems with predetermined decision trees fail catastrophically on edge cases—when queries fall outside anticipated patterns, these systems cannot degrade gracefully and instead force full pipeline restarts or require users to manually reformulate requests. The authors' own prior work on conditional orchestration suffered from this brittleness, motivating a shift to adaptive, learned routing. Methodology: The paper introduces a centralized orchestration framework built around a single Supervisor LLM that dynamically decomposes queries, selects tools, and synthesizes results without following fixed decision trees. The architecture has three processing paths based on query type: (1) text-only queries use RouteLLM for learned strong/weak model routing—a two-stage classifier first predicts whether a query needs a strong model (threshold P > 0.4), then subclassifies weak queries into coding, summarization, math, or general subcategories for fine-grained model selection; (2) non-text queries go through the Couplet Framework, which pairs domain-optimized perceptual models (YOLO for object detection, CLIP for retrieval, Tesseract for OCR, Whisper for transcription) with lightweight SLM coordinators that translate natural language to structured model inputs and contextualize outputs; (3) complex multi-step queries use LangGraph StateGraph for parallel execution with dependency analysis and local repair mechanisms. Query routing uses a two-stage decomposition: heuristic attachment analysis (file extensions, MIME types) followed by a Phi-3.5-mini-instruct SLM classifier that assigns one of eight execution flags (audio, video, vision, imagen, document, routellm, moe, complex). A three-tier cost knob (trad_couplet at $0.15–0.25/M tokens, open_src at $0.30–0.50/M tokens, closed_src at $2.50–5.00/M tokens) enables explicit cost-performance tradeoffs. A five-layer hierarchical memory architecture maintains short-term (circular buffer, last 5 turns), full history, modality-specific vector stores (Qdrant HNSW with 1536-dim embeddings), relevant retrieved context, and LLM-compressed summaries, combined via weighted scoring: C_final = 0.6·C_short ⊕ 0.3·C_relevant ⊕ 0.1·C_compressed. Key Results: Evaluated on 2,847 queries across 15 task categories against a matched hierarchical baseline, a monolithic GPT-4 baseline, and multi-agent frameworks (AutoGen, LangGraph). The centralized framework achieves: 72% median reduction in time-to-accurate-answer (TTA: 4.2s → 1.18s, IQR 65–77%, p<0.001); 85% reduction in conversational rework rate (23% → 3.4%, p<0.001); 67% reduction in cost per query ($0.15 → $0.05, p<0.001); 20% improvement in throughput (45 → 54 q/s, p<0.01); accuracy parity within ±1% (99.8% vs 99.2%, not statistically significant). Performance is consistent across modalities: text 73%, image 68%, audio 71%, document 65%, video 69%, mixed-modal 77% TTA reduction. Perceptual task latency drops from 2.4s/frame (LLM vision) to 180ms/frame (YOLO via Couplet). Ablation studies show memory layers contribute 28% TTA impact, parallel execution 42% TTA impact, and the Couplet Framework 45% TTA impact when removed. Predetermined routing fails on 23% of edge cases that the adaptive system handles successfully. Strengths: - The composite optimization target (time-to-accurate-answer rather than pure cost or raw latency) is well-motivated and captures real deployment economics more faithfully than prior routing work focused on single metrics - Local repair mechanisms that recover from individual tool failures without global pipeline restarts address a genuine architectural weakness of hierarchical systems, demonstrated concretely with the handwritten-notes edge case study - The Couplet Framework's approach of pairing specialized perceptual models with SLM coordinators achieves both cost and latency efficiency (13× speedup for vision tasks) while maintaining a clean typed interface abstraction that enables plug-and-play extensibility Weaknesses / Limitations: - The evaluation dataset is partially constructed by the authors using "curated multimodal prompts" following their own prior workload construction principles, raising questions about benchmark independence and potential optimization toward self-constructed test distributions - The Couplet Framework supports only a fixed catalog of traditional models (YOLO, CLIP, Tesseract, ResNet), and adding new perceptual capabilities requires manual integration—the paper acknowledges this but offers no automated solution beyond future meta-learning directions - LLM-based orchestration introduces inherent latency overhead for very high-throughput scenarios, and the routing system exhibits over-conservative escalation to expensive models in ambiguous cases, partially undermining the cost efficiency claims for workloads with high query ambiguity

#113 — Incremental (37)

Unifying Ontology Construction and Semantic Alignment for Deterministic Enterprise Reasoning at Scale

preprints.org · semantic integration ontologies - new results
Why this rank: This is a technically competent generalization of known 2D universal word shortening results to arbitrary dimension d, with the row-independent incomparable elements being a genuinely useful refinement. However, the core approach closely follows the Kitaev–Potapov–Vajnovszki framework, and the problem domain is narrow with limited cross-pollination potential.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: A universal word (u-word) for d-dimensional permutations of length n is a 2-dimensional matrix with d−1 rows such that every consecutive window of n columns covers exactly one d-dimensional n-permutation (in reduced form), and all such permutations are covered. The naive ("trivial") u-word has length (n!)^(d−1) + n − 1. The central question is: how much shorter can u-words be made? For the classical 2-dimensional case (d=2), Kitaev, Potapov, and Vajnovszki (2019) showed that u-words of length n! + n − 1 − i(n−1) exist for a range of i values, using incomparable elements. This paper extends that result to general d ≥ 2, addressing the non-trivial obstacles that arise because direct product constructions for d-dimensional universal objects do not work in general. Methodology: The authors introduce a refined notion of incomparable elements applied independently per row of the multi-dimensional matrix, rather than to entire columns simultaneously. This yields a richer family of compressed u-words than the column-level approach. The core proof technique involves three main components: (1) d-dimensional analogues of twin permutations — pairs of d-dimensional n-permutations that differ only in a final element that can be made incomparable — classified by "type i" based on how many of their d−1 rows are non-monotone; (2) the clustered graph of overlapping d-dimensional n-permutations, which has ((n−1)!)^(d−1) clusters and is shown to admit an Eulerian cycle (proved via balance and strong connectivity); (3) a compression procedure that selects cycles in this clustered graph formed by 2^i parallel edges corresponding to i-twin classes, removes n−1 edges from each such cycle (by introducing a repeated element in row j to serve as an incomparable pair), and counts the maximum achievable compression. The maximum compression sums over all i from 1 to d−1 using the formula for the number of such disjoint cycles (Lemma 3.3), yielding a total reduction of (2^(d−1))/(n−1) · [(1+(n−1)!)^(d−1) − (1+(n−1)!/2)^(d−1)] steps. Key Results: The main theorem (Theorem 1.1) establishes that u-words of length (n!)^(d−1) + n − 1 − i(n−1) exist for d ≥ 2 and all integers i in [0, (2^(d−1))/(n−1) · ((1+(n−1)!)^(d−1) − (1+(n−1)!/2)^(d−1))]. For d=2 this recovers the Kitaev–Potapov–Vajnovszki result exactly. For d=3, n=3, the theorem gives u-words as short as length 18 (compared to the trivial length 26 = (3!)^2 + 2), demonstrated with an explicit construction. The paper also exhibits a u-cycle (cyclic version) for 3-dimensional 3-permutations of length 16. A lower bound of 2^(number of removed cycles) is noted for the number of distinct shortened u-words achievable by this method. The paper also raises open questions: whether u-cycles of the same family of lengths exist (Question 1), what other lengths are achievable beyond those in Theorem 1.1 (Question 2), and what good upper bounds exist on the count of u-words (Question 3). Strengths: - Cleanly generalizes a nontrivial 2D result to arbitrary dimension d, overcoming genuine obstacles (e.g., products of u-cycles do not directly yield d-dimensional u-cycles) - The proof is constructive: an explicit algorithm transforms any Eulerian path in the compressed clustered graph into a concrete u-word matrix, with worked examples for d=3, n=3 - The framework of row-independent incomparable elements is strictly more powerful than the column-level approach, yielding many more achievable lengths Weaknesses / Limitations: - The achieved compression may not be optimal; the paper itself notes that allowing incomparable elements at distances smaller than n−1 could yield even shorter u-words not covered by Theorem 1.1 (e.g., for n=4, d=2 a u-word of length 17 exists, shorter than the theorem's minimum of 21) - The lower bound on the number of distinct u-words produced by this method is acknowledged to be very far from the true count, and a tight enumeration via the BEST Theorem is noted as computationally intractable even for small n and d

#114 — Incremental (37)

HisGraphRAG: GraphRAG for Vietnamese Historical question answering

aclanthology.org · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: HisGraphRAG is a competent domain adaptation of GraphRAG with some clever practical additions, particularly the answer candidate filtering mechanism inspired by human test-taking strategy. However, the overall approach is an incremental extension of existing GraphRAG pipelines with domain-specific modules (entity alignment, temporal DB), evaluated on a narrow benchmark of 240 multiple-choice exam questions.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Large Language Models struggle with factual accuracy and hallucinations in domain-specific historical QA, particularly for less-documented contexts like Vietnamese history. Standard RAG fails to capture complex inter-entity relationships and temporal structures inherent in historical narratives. While GraphRAG addresses some of these limitations, it introduces new problems in historical domains: (1) redundant/variant entity representations fragment the knowledge graph (e.g., "Soviet" vs. "Soviet Union"); (2) temporal grounding is lost, making time-sensitive questions harder; and (3) excessive retrieved information can paradoxically suppress LLM reasoning ability, causing GraphRAG to underperform a naive LLM in some cases. Methodology: HisGraphRAG is a six-step pipeline extending GraphRAG with three targeted enhancements. During indexing: (1) Section-aware document chunking preserves semantic coherence of history textbook chapters, with a VLM used to extract text from visual elements; (2) Domain-specific entity/relationship extraction targets historical concepts (events, figures, locations, organizations, strategies) stored in a Neo4j graph database plus a separate SQL temporal database; (3) Entity alignment uses an LLM with extended context to batch-merge semantically equivalent entities into canonical forms, reducing graph nodes by 11.09% and relations by 25.11%. During querying: (4) Answer candidate filtering scores each multiple-choice option via LLM confidence and discards options below a 15% threshold, reducing noisy retrieval from wrong answers; (5) Graph retrieval (cosine similarity via Milvus) combined with temporal retrieval (±1 year window around query's time expression) form a union context set; (6) LLM-guided reranking selects the top-15 most relevant context items before final answer generation. The system uses GPT-4o mini as the primary LLM and text-embedding-3-small for dense retrieval. Key Results: Evaluated on Vietnamese University Entrance Exam datasets (VUEE-2017 and VUEE-2018, 120 questions each from 3 official exam sets), HisGraphRAG achieves 81.7% accuracy on VUEE-2017 and 70.8% on VUEE-2018, compared to: LLM-only (68.3%/50.8%), NaiveRAG (72.5%/54.2%), and standard GraphRAG (77.5%/60.0%). Answer candidate filtering alone is the single most impactful component (80.8%/63.3%), while temporal retrieval + reranking in isolation actually hurts performance (73.3%/56.7%) due to context overload. Entity alignment alone yields mixed results (-0.8% on 2017, +1.7% on 2018). The full system trades accuracy gains for computational cost: 2.2M tokens and ~977s response time vs. 920k tokens and 210s for baseline GraphRAG. Strengths: - The answer candidate filtering mechanism is a novel, practically effective contribution—inspired by human test-taking strategy—that significantly reduces retrieval noise from distractor options and provides the largest single accuracy boost. - The dual-database architecture (graph DB for relational context + SQL temporal DB) cleanly separates structural and chronological retrieval, enabling targeted temporal windowing that standard GraphRAG lacks. - Ablation study is thorough, evaluating each component individually and in combination, revealing non-obvious interactions (e.g., temporal retrieval hurts in isolation but helps in the full system). Weaknesses / Limitations: - Evaluation is narrow: only 120 questions per dataset from a single knowledge source (Grade 12 Vietnamese history textbook), making generalizability to other historical domains or languages uncertain. - Computational cost is severe—~977 seconds and 2.2M tokens per 40-question exam—making real-time or large-scale deployment impractical without significant optimization. - Entity alignment requires LLM+human validation of canonical names, introducing a manual step that limits full automation and scalability.

#115 — Incremental (37)

RBCorr: Response Bias Correction in Language Models

Om Bhatt, Anna A. Ivanova · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: RBCorr applies a straightforward mean-normalization of token log-probabilities using a small calibration set — a simple and effective calibration technique but not methodologically novel. The paper's main value is its unusually comprehensive experimental coverage (12 models × 10 datasets × 3 prompt formats) and the useful negative result that correction terms do not transfer across configurations, though neither finding is surprising given prior calibration literature.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Language models (LMs) evaluated on fixed-response questions (yes/no, multiple choice, NLI) exhibit systematic response biases — tendencies to favor certain answer tokens regardless of content. This inflates or deflates measured accuracy, making benchmark scores unreliable proxies for true model capability. Prior mitigation efforts using LogProbs manipulations lacked systematic evaluation across diverse experimental configurations (models, datasets, prompt formats), and did not track bias separately from accuracy. The paper addresses this gap by proposing a principled, low-overhead correction method and characterizing how bias varies across these axes. Methodology: RBCorr is a calibration-based bias correction applied directly to token log-probabilities (LogProbs) before response selection. The key steps are: (1) extract single-token response LogProbs from the model's final layer (pre-softmax) for all answer options; (2) sample a small class-balanced calibration set (as few as 100 items) from the dataset; (3) compute the mean LogProbs for each answer token across this calibration set; (4) subtract these means from each item's LogProbs at evaluation time. This mean-normalization forces the model's average output distribution to be uniform across options, directly counteracting label preference bias. Bias is measured via Total Variation Distance (TVD) between the uniform ground-truth label distribution and the model's empirical response distribution. The method is tested on 12 open-weight LMs from three families (Falcon3, Gemma3, Llama3.1, each with 3B–70B base and instruction-tuned variants), across 10 datasets spanning 2-choice (ARITH, BABI, COMPS, EWoK), 3-choice NLI (SNLI, MNLI), and 4-choice MCQ (MMLU-STEM/Humanities/Social Sci/Others) question types, and three prompt formats (zeroshot, instruction-only, fewshot). Key Results: RBCorr consistently reduces TVD bias and maintains or improves accuracy across nearly all model-dataset combinations. The largest accuracy gain observed is +28.9% on the BABI dataset for Llama3.1-8B. Comparing against Contextual Calibration (CC) and Batch Calibration (BC) on Llama3.1 models, RBCorr generally achieves the best accuracy gains for 2-choice and 3-choice datasets (e.g., +11.7% on SNLI vs. +11.8% for CC and +4.3% for BC), while BC performs better on 4-choice MMLU tasks where RBCorr occasionally slightly reduces accuracy. Against PriDe (which carries 15% overhead compute), RBCorr achieves comparable bias reduction (e.g., −77.55% vs. −76.09% RStd on llama-2-7B) and accuracy improvement (+12.44% vs. +12.29%) at zero additional compute. Smaller models benefit most from correction. Critically, bias correction terms do not transfer reliably across models (10.42% success), prompt formats (10.42%), or datasets (21.93%), confirming that LogProbs-based bias is highly configuration-specific. Strengths: - Zero overhead at inference: calibration requires only baseline LogProbs from a small held-out set (≥100 items), no extra forward passes needed during evaluation. - Systematic experimental design covering 12 models × 10 datasets × 3 prompt formats simultaneously tracking both bias (TVD) and accuracy, providing unusually comprehensive characterization. - The non-transferability finding (Experiment 4) is a substantive negative result that informs practitioners against reusing pre-computed correction terms across configurations. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Restricted to open-weight models where LogProbs are accessible; closed-API models require approximate workarounds (high-temperature sampling) that may be less reliable. - Evaluation limited to transformer models up to 70B parameters; behavior on larger models or non-transformer architectures is unverified. - RBCorr underperforms Batch Calibration on 4-choice MMLU tasks and occasionally slightly hurts accuracy on already low-bias instruction-tuned models, suggesting the method is not universally dominant.

#116 — Incremental (37)

Towards Hyper-Personalized Travel Planning: A Multimodal AI Agent with Integrated Neural Rendering for Immersive Itineraries

mdpi.com · karypis - new results
Why this rank: Creative architectural integration of agentic LLM planning with real-time 3D Gaussian Splatting visualization for travel planning. The Pydantic schema validation as hallucination mitigation is a practical design choice. However, the purely qualitative evaluation at TRL 4, reliance on pre-reconstructed local assets, and failure on the very hotel aesthetics (minimalist glass/mirrors) most relevant to the target market make this a feasibility demonstration rather than a validated contribution.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: The travel planning industry suffers from two compounding problems: data fragmentation across platforms (flights, hotels, activities require navigating separate services) and the absence of immersive "try-before-you-buy" visual validation. While LLM-based travel chatbots can synthesize information conversationally, they operate in a visual vacuum—they can recommend a "luxury suite with a sea view" but cannot show it. Static 2D photo galleries fail to convey spatial context or realistic lighting, leaving users without a meaningful way to visually verify accommodation quality before booking. The paper addresses this gap by coupling conversational AI planning with real-time photorealistic 3D visualization of hotel facilities. Methodology: The system integrates two distinct subsystems. The first is a conversational AI agent built on Gemini 2.5 Flash, implemented in Python 3.11 using LangChain for agentic orchestration and Chainlit for the multimodal chat interface. The agent uses an OOP architecture separating an Engine class (reasoning, conversation history, system prompt) from Tool classes (deterministic logic functions). Tools are structured via Pydantic schemas, which act as validation logic gates—blocking invalid inputs like negative night lengths or malformed dates and forcing re-prompting cycles. Tools include itinerary structuring tools (flights, hotels, activities, car rental), an itinerary compiler, and critically, a 3D Visualization Bridge that contextually triggers the neural rendering pipeline. The agent also processes image screenshots (e.g., of flight search results) to populate itinerary fields via Gemini's multimodal capabilities. The second subsystem is a modular SfM→3DGS reconstruction pipeline: raw smartphone video is preprocessed via FFmpeg at 2 fps with Laplacian variance blur filtering, then fed into one of five interchangeable SfM backends (COLMAP, OpenMVG, GLOMAP, Hloc, PixSfM) that produce sparse point clouds and camera poses. A Data Format Harmonization Layer standardizes outputs to a unified JSON schema. The sparse cloud initializes 3D Gaussian Splatting (3DGS) optimization using the gsplat library (PyTorch 2.0/CUDA 11.8), training with a composite L1 + D-SSIM loss (λ=0.2) and adaptive density control (pruning, cloning, splitting every ~100 iterations). The final optimized .ply files are served via a WebGL-based Gaussian Splatting viewer embedded directly in the chat interface, enabling >30 fps interactive navigation. Key Results: The system is presented as a TRL 4 proof of concept validated on three Gran Canaria hotel facilities (Lopesan Corallium Dunamar, Kumara Serenoa, HD Parque Cristóbal). Evaluation was qualitative rather than quantitative benchmark-oriented. Key demonstrated behaviors: (1) robust slot-filling—the agent correctly identifies missing mandatory Pydantic fields and pauses execution to request them; (2) stateful multi-turn itinerary building—components (flights, hotels, activities, car rental) are maintained as persistent structured dictionaries modifiable across conversation turns; (3) multimodal input processing—the agent successfully parsed tabular flight data from screenshot images and correctly inferred per-flight prices from aggregate totals; (4) contextual 3DGS triggering—when a user asks "Can I see the pool?", the agent queries its local asset database and mounts a WebGL viewer inline in the chat. 3DGS training converged structurally at ~1k iterations with fine view-dependent detail refinement continuing to 10k iterations; interior scenes required ~500k Gaussians while large exterior facilities required significantly more. The pipeline failed on featureless surfaces (white walls, mirrored surfaces, glass) where SfM feature matching collapses. Strengths: - Novel architectural integration of agentic LLM planning with real-time neural rendering, demonstrating a working end-to-end multimodal pipeline rather than just a conceptual proposal - Pydantic schema validation as a structural hallucination mitigation mechanism is a practically sound design—it enforces data integrity at the tool boundary rather than relying on LLM output correctness alone - Modular SfM backend design with a harmonization layer enables systematic future benchmarking and easy substitution of reconstruction methods without downstream changes Weaknesses / Limitations: - Purely qualitative evaluation with no quantitative metrics (PSNR, SSIM, user study data, task completion rates) and no comparison to baselines—the paper explicitly frames itself as a feasibility proof at TRL 4, making it difficult to assess practical viability - Critical environmental bias: the SfM pipeline fails on featureless modern architecture (minimalist white walls, mirrored surfaces, glass), which characterizes many luxury urban hotels—precisely the high-value market segment where visual verification would matter most - The system uses a constrained local database of pre-reconstructed Gran Canaria hotel assets rather than live booking APIs or a scalable asset pipeline, severely limiting generalization and leaving the path to commercial deployment (GDS API integration, automated capture protocols, RAG grounding) entirely to future work

#117 — Incremental (36)

Addressing Zoning Abbreviation Inconsistencies in the Greater Toronto Area (GTA) through a Universal Zoning Ontology

yorkspace.library.yorku.ca · semantic integration ontologies - new results
Why this rank: Addresses a real and well-documented municipal fragmentation problem with a clean dual-anchor ontology design, and the practitioner-grounded competency questions ensure practical relevance. However, the approach applies standard ontology engineering methodology (LOT, OWL-DL, SPARQL CQ testing) to a narrow geographic domain with only 2 of 25 municipalities covered, limiting both methodological novelty and representativeness.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Urban development in the Greater Toronto Area (GTA) is hindered by severe fragmentation in zoning terminology across its 25 municipalities. Each municipality independently maintains its own zoning by-law with distinct abbreviations, definitions, and classification systems. Identical-looking codes carry different meanings (e.g., "R2" in Toronto versus "R2-S" in Markham refer to zones with different permitted uses and development standards), while functionally equivalent zones bear entirely different labels across jurisdictions. This semantic fragmentation creates legal ambiguities, duplicative analyses, and delays in permitting workflows — directly contributing to Ontario's housing supply crisis, where only 81,300 units were produced in 2024 against a provincial target of 125,000 annually. Existing urban ontologies (LBCS-OWL2, iCity, OntoURBAN, CityGML, LandInfra) address land use categorization or infrastructure, but none models zoning by-law terminology, cross-municipal equivalences, or conditional permissions in a unified, machine-readable structure. Methodology: The thesis introduces Zonology, a zoning ontology for the GTA developed using a five-stage Linked Open Terms (LOT) methodology adhering to FAIR principles (Findable, Accessible, Interoperable, Reusable). Stage 1 (Specification) involved cross-municipal comparison of four GTA municipalities (Toronto, Markham, Aurora, Ajax) to document zoning abbreviation inconsistencies and formalize an Ontology Requirements Specification Document (ORSD) with four competency question (CQ) categories: general definitional queries, cross-jurisdictional comparisons, constraint-based regulatory queries, and use-specific activity queries. Stage 2 (Conceptualization) used a hybrid top-down/bottom-up modeling approach, producing two core ontology anchors: Zone (the physical spatial unit) and ZoningDesignation (the abstract regulatory category). The class hierarchy includes approximately 60+ ZoningDesignation subclasses, 150+ LandUse subclasses, and supporting classes for Authority, Document, ZoningCharacteristic, and ZoningIndex (quantitative standards: height, FSI, frontage, lot coverage, parking). Stage 3 (Refinement) standardized naming, reused elements from LBCS, iCity, and CityGML where possible, and enforced OWL-DL compliance. Stage 4 (Implementation) encoded the ontology in OWL 2 using Protégé, with SPARQL-queryable object properties including hasPermittedLandUse, hasAbbreviation, isEquivalentToDesignation, regulatedBy, and hasMaxHeight. Cross-municipal linkages (e.g., Toronto's CL ≡ Markham's COM; Toronto's R1 ≡ Markham's TR) were encoded as owl:equivalentClass or rdfs:subClassOf axioms. Stage 5 (Evaluation) used 13 SPARQL competency question tests in Protégé, DL reasoning with HermiT, expert practitioner workshops, and qualitative comparison against manual by-law lookup. Key Results: Quantitative evaluation over 13 SPARQL competency queries yielded precision = 0.83, recall = 0.91, and F1 = 0.87. Execution accuracy was approximately 0.77 (3 of 13 queries required manual adjustment). DL reasoning with HermiT confirmed: no unsatisfiable named classes, global ontology consistency, no domain/range property violations, and no hierarchy collapse from equivalence axioms — all four checks passed. Three notable failure cases were identified and corrected: (1) over-strict owl:equivalentClass between Industrial and Employment zones that conflicted with a disjointness axiom, resolved by relaxing to rdfs:subClassOf; (2) lz:hasPermittedLandUse accidentally declared as a datatype property in the Employment branch, causing SPARQL binding failures; (3) missing conjunctive someValuesFrom restrictions on the MU class prevented Mixed-Use zones from being retrieved by conjunctive residential-and-commercial queries. After corrections, all queries executed successfully. Qualitative comparison showed ontology-backed retrieval offers higher repeatability, harmonized terminology, formalized traceability, and lower cognitive load for cross-jurisdictional tasks compared to manual by-law lookup. Strengths: - First ontology specifically designed to harmonize GTA municipal zoning terminology, filling a clear gap unaddressed by LBCS-OWL2, iCity, OntoURBAN, CityGML, and LandInfra - Dual-anchor design (Zone vs. ZoningDesignation) cleanly separates spatial footprint from regulatory category, enabling a single designation to apply to many zones and zones to shift designation as by-laws change — a pattern that keeps the ontology in OWL-DL and makes reasoning predictable - Grounded in practitioner-derived competency questions, ensuring every class and property traces back to a real planning task; validated by planning professionals in structured workshops Weaknesses / Limitations: - Coverage restricted to only two of the GTA's 25 municipalities (Toronto and Markham); unique practices in the other 23 (e.g., Vaughan employment lands, Oakville heritage overlays) are absent, limiting representativeness - Legal zoning language contains conditional exceptions, site-specific provisions, and interpretive clauses (e.g., "except where abutting a major arterial road") that cannot be fully encoded without oversimplification, meaning ontology outputs must be treated as decision-support information rather than authoritative legal determinations - Execution accuracy of ~0.77 (3/13 queries requiring manual fixes) reveals that axiomatization errors — over-strict equivalences, property type misspecification, missing conjunctive restrictions — are a persistent risk that requires careful ontology engineering discipline as coverage scales

#118 — Incremental (34)

ArcLight: A Lightweight LLM Inference Architecture for Many-Core CPUs

Yuzhuang Xu, Xu Han, Yuxuan Li, Wanxiang Che · arxiv.org · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: ARCLIGHT correctly identifies cross-NUMA memory access mismatch as the root cause of many-core CPU inference degradation and applies well-known NUMA-aware allocation techniques to solve it. The tensor_ptrs bundle abstraction and asynchronous subgraph execution are useful systems contributions, but the core optimization strategy is a standard application of established NUMA programming principles to a new workload.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Existing CPU-based LLM inference frameworks (primarily llama.cpp) fail to exploit the full computational potential of many-core CPU platforms deployed in web servers and high-end networking devices. These platforms use Non-Uniform Memory Access (NUMA) architectures where CPU cores and memory are partitioned across multiple nodes, and remote memory access can be ~4x slower than local access (measured at ~102 GB/s local vs. ~23 GB/s remote on a 4-NUMA ARM system). Current frameworks like llama.cpp distribute threads across NUMA nodes but do not bind tensors to specific nodes, causing systematic cross-node memory access mismatches during GEMM operations that compound across transformer layers, forming a sustained performance bottleneck. Additionally, mature frameworks have accumulated legacy code that makes fine-grained optimizations difficult. Methodology: ARCLIGHT is a lightweight C++ LLM inference framework (~10 source files) built ground-up for many-core CPUs with five core modules: memory manager, thread manager, tensor library, forward graph builder, and graph computation scheduler. The key technical innovations are: (1) NUMA-aware memory management that allocates separate memory buffers in the local memory of each NUMA node (vs. llama.cpp's monolithic UMA buffer), with a double-buffering mechanism for activations to reduce memory overhead. (2) Multi-view thread organization that logically partitions a single thread pool into dynamically reconfigurable groups, enabling both single-graph and multi-subgraph parallel execution. This introduces a two-level barrier system: local barriers within thread groups and global barriers across all groups. (3) Cross-NUMA tensor parallelism (TP) applied to transformer weight matrices: Wq, Wk, Wv, Wgate, Wup are row-partitioned across NUMA nodes while Wo and Wdown are column-partitioned, with Scatter and Gather operators managing transitions between serial and parallel subgraph execution. Crucially, the paper introduces asynchronous subgraph execution (Sync B) where synchronization is confined within thread groups except at TP boundaries, reducing thread idle time. ARCLIGHT reuses llama.cpp's operator kernels (GEMM, FlashAttention) rather than reimplementing them. Key Results: Evaluated on a 192-core machine (4 NUMA nodes × 48 HUAWEI Kunpeng-920 ARMv8.2 cores) running Qwen3-4B quantized at Q4_0. In the single-NUMA-node case (up to 48 threads), ARCLIGHT achieves slightly higher decoding throughput than llama.cpp (~28 vs. ~25 tok/s at 48 threads), as NUMA-aware local allocation outperforms OS-managed page distribution. In the multi-NUMA case, the advantage is substantially larger: with 4 NUMA nodes at 96 threads, ARCLIGHT achieves ~38 tok/s vs. ~8 tok/s for llama.cpp (N=4), representing the up-to-46% headline throughput improvement. Asynchronous subgraph execution alone contributes ~5 tok/s additional gain. For prefill throughput with 300-token prompts, ARCLIGHT still outperforms llama.cpp but the advantage is smaller since prefill is compute-bound rather than memory-bandwidth-bound. Strengths: - Precisely identifies and addresses the root cause of many-core CPU inference degradation (cross-NUMA memory access mismatch) with a principled, measurable solution rather than heuristic tuning - Clean modular architecture (~10 files) makes the framework transparent and extensible; the tensor_ptrs bundle abstraction elegantly preserves unified module interfaces across serial and TP execution modes without requiring model-specific TP rewrites - Asynchronous subgraph execution (Sync B) is a subtle but impactful optimization that reduces cross-group barrier overhead within TP regions Weaknesses / Limitations: - Evaluated exclusively on ARM (Kunpeng-920) hardware; x86 support is declared future work, limiting immediate applicability to the dominant server CPU architecture - The Scatter and Gather operator implementations are described as preliminary, with unresolved memory overhead and parallel efficiency gaps; the TP advantage for prefill (compute-bound phase) is significantly smaller than for decoding, suggesting the optimization is narrowly targeted at memory-bandwidth bottlenecks - Experiments use only one model (Qwen3-4B Q4_0) and one quantization format, leaving open questions about generalization to different model scales, architectures (e.g., MoE), or quantization schemes

#119 — Incremental (34)

Parallelization Strategies for Dense LLM Deployment: Navigating Through Application-Specific Tradeoffs and Bottlenecks

Burak Topcu, Musa Oguzhan Cim, Poovaiah Palangappa, Meena Arunachalam, Mahmut Taylan Kandemir · arxiv.org · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: This paper provides a thorough empirical characterization of well-known parallelization strategies (TP, PP, hybrid) using a validated simulator, but the core insights — TP reduces latency, PP improves throughput — are widely understood in the systems community. The contribution is primarily in systematic quantification across workloads rather than in novel methods or surprising findings.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Dense large language models (LLMs) — those activating all parameters per token — face severe deployment challenges due to their enormous memory footprints and the inherent tension between inference latency and throughput. Serving models like Llama 3.1-405B in FP8 requires ~405GB just for weights, far exceeding single-GPU memory. While parallelization strategies such as Tensor Parallelism (TP) and Pipeline Parallelism (PP) are widely used, the literature lacks systematic analysis of how these strategies interact with batching preferences, input characteristics, and application-specific SLA objectives. This gap hinders practitioners from making principled deployment decisions. Methodology: The authors develop and validate an in-house analytical simulator that models end-to-end LLM inference workloads across different GPU configurations, parallelization schemes, and input characteristics. The simulator models individual transformer kernels (QKV projection, attention via GQA, FC-1/FC-2 GEMMs, all-reduce operations) with variable hyperparameters, and cascades them to represent full model stacks. It is validated against AMD MI325x/MI355x silicon, achieving >90% accuracy for prefill and >86% for decode on Llama 3.1-70B. Experiments span Llama 3.1-70B (8-bit, MI325x, 8 GPUs) and Llama 3.1-405B (4-bit, MI355x, 8 GPUs), across five datasets (LongAlpaca, MLPerf, BBH, GSM8K, HumanEval) covering both long-context and short-sequence regimes. Parallelization strategies evaluated include pure TP (degrees 2–8), pure PP (depths 2–8), and hybrid TP×PP combinations. Metrics are time-to-first-token (TTFT), time-per-output-token (TPOT), and output tokens-per-second (TPS). Key Results: TP substantially reduces both TTFT and TPOT compared to PP and no-parallelism baselines, with deeper TP providing further latency reductions. For Llama 3.1-70B with batch size 256 on LongAlpaca, TP8 achieves 1.87× lower TTFT and 3.01× lower TPOT compared to TP4, and 3.61× lower TPOT compared to TP2. However, TP degrades system throughput (TPS) relative to PP because all-reduce overhead grows with TP degree and is not offset proportionally by compute savings. PP improves throughput by enabling larger effective batch sizes through reduced per-GPU memory pressure: PP8 on Llama 3.1-405B increases maximum nano-batch size from 32 (no parallelism) to 512, achieving 13.8× and 44× TPS improvements over no-parallelism on MLPerf and combined short-sequence datasets respectively — though with only a 1.35× gain over pure data parallelism on MLPerf due to its long-ISL-dominated prefill. Hybrid TP×PP configurations provide a tunable balance: TP controls latency flexibility, PP controls throughput scaling. All-reduce operations incur an unavoidable latency overhead regardless of interconnect speed — doubling link speed from 128 GB/s to 608 GB/s reduces TTFT by only 34% for TP8 on the 405B model. P2P communication overhead in PP remains below 0.5% of TTFT even at 1/5th of datasheet link speed (32 GB/s), confirming PP's latency impact is negligible intra-node. Strengths: - Comprehensive empirical sweep across all relevant deployment axes (model size, parallelism type and degree, batch size, short vs. long sequences) using a validated simulator, enabling insights impractical to obtain on real hardware alone. - Clear and actionable separation of TP vs. PP roles: TP for latency-sensitive SLAs, PP for throughput-oriented batch workloads, with hybrid configurations as a principled middle ground. - Simulator validation against real AMD Instinct silicon with quantified error bounds (>90%/86% accuracy) gives the findings credibility beyond purely analytical models. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Analysis is restricted to single-node (intra-node) deployments with all-to-all GPU interconnects; multi-node scenarios — where inter-node bandwidth is far lower and would dramatically amplify TP's all-reduce overhead — are discussed qualitatively but not experimentally evaluated. - The simulator exhibits higher error for the 405B decode phase (~17.25% average prefill error for TP8 on MI325x), attributed to dynamic voltage-frequency variations and suboptimal kernel performance on actual hardware, limiting precision for very deep parallelization regimes. - The study covers only dense transformer architectures; Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) models, which introduce all-to-all expert routing communication on top of standard TP all-reduces, are discussed as future work rather than evaluated.

#120 — Incremental (34)

SEMANTIC OPPOSITION AND LEXICAL MEANING: EPISTEMOLOGICAL REFLECTIONS ON LANGUAGE AND KNOWLEDGE

cyberleninka.ru · semantic integration ontologies - new results
Why this rank: A competent integrative synthesis bridging structural linguistics, cognitive science, and cross-cultural semantics under an epistemological framing of opposition. However, it presents no original empirical data or novel methodology—its contribution is reorganizing well-known ideas (Saussure, Jakobson, Lakoff) into a unified narrative, and the cross-cultural evidence (two languages plus brief references) is too thin to support the universality claims advanced.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: This paper addresses the question of how semantic opposition—the structuring of meaning through contrast (e.g., true/false, hot/cold, buy/sell)—functions not merely as a lexical device but as a foundational epistemological mechanism. The gap it targets is the fragmented treatment of opposition across structural linguistics, cognitive science, and philosophy of language: prior accounts either formalize contrast syntactically (Saussure, Jakobson) or ground it in embodied cognition (Lakoff, Johnson) without integrating these into a unified epistemological framework. The paper argues that opposition is the core infrastructure by which language constructs, organizes, and transmits knowledge. Methodology: The paper is a theoretical synthesis drawing on four intellectual traditions: (1) classical ontology (Plato's dialectic, Aristotle's categories, Lévi-Strauss's binary classification, Cassirer's symbolic forms) to establish that opposition is a structuring principle of reality itself; (2) structural linguistics (Saussure's differential value, Jakobson's markedness theory, Trier's semantic field theory) to show how contrast is encoded in lexical architecture; (3) cognitive linguistics (Lakoff and Johnson's conceptual metaphor, Rosch's prototype theory, Eve Clark's contrastive mapping in first-language acquisition, Vygotsky's sociocultural theory) to ground opposition in embodied experience and development; and (4) cross-cultural/linguistic relativity analysis (Sapir-Whorf hypothesis, Winawer et al.'s Russian color study, Uzbek moral binaries, Chinese yin/yang) to demonstrate that while oppositional form is universal, semantic content is culturally mediated. No original empirical data are collected; the paper synthesizes and reframes existing evidence. Key Results: The paper's principal findings are theoretical rather than quantitative. It establishes a typology of opposition (gradable antonyms like wide/narrow, complementary pairs like dead/alive, converses like buy/sell, reversives like open/close) and argues each encodes a distinct logical and epistemic relation. The Russian color term distinction (goluboy vs. sinij) is cited as empirical support: Russian speakers discriminate shades of blue faster when the boundary crosses the lexical divide, showing that lexically encoded opposition sharpens perceptual categorization (Winawer et al., 2007, PNAS). The Uzbek halol/harom binary is analyzed as an ideologically loaded opposition that functions as a normative epistemic tool, not merely a descriptive one. The paper concludes that markedness asymmetry (Jakobson) embeds cultural hierarchies directly into linguistic structure (e.g., normal/abnormal, able/disabled). Strengths: - Broad integrative scope: successfully bridges classical philosophy, structural linguistics, cognitive linguistics, and cross-cultural semantics into a coherent epistemological argument - Concrete cross-linguistic examples (Russian color terms, Uzbek moral binaries, Chinese yin/yang) ground abstract claims in culturally specific data - The typological treatment of opposition types (antonyms, complementaries, converses, reversives) adds analytical precision often absent in philosophical accounts of contrast Weaknesses / Limitations: - The paper is entirely a literature synthesis with no original empirical data; claims about how opposition shapes cognition rest on cited studies rather than new evidence - The argument conflates distinct senses of "epistemology" (individual knowledge acquisition, cultural worldview, formal logic) without clearly distinguishing which claims apply to which level - Cross-cultural analysis is selective and anecdotal—two languages (Russian, Uzbek) plus brief references to Chinese thought—insufficient to support the broad universality-vs-cultural-specificity thesis advanced

#121 — Incremental (33)

An epoch-weighted privacy budget allocation framework for fine-tuning large language models

scis.scichina.com · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: The core insight that earlier training epochs tolerate more noise is intuitive and empirically validated, but the two-stage budget allocation is a relatively incremental refinement of existing DP-SGD scheduling ideas. The autoencoder-based dimensionality reduction is a reasonable engineering contribution but raises its own unanalyzed privacy concerns. Evaluation is limited to BERT-base on three classification tasks with modest gains (1.8–2.8%) over baselines, leaving generalization to larger models and generative tasks unclear.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Fine-tuning large language models (LLMs) on private data exposes sensitive information to adversaries via membership inference, extraction, and attribute inference attacks. Existing differentially private fine-tuning methods (e.g., DP-SGD) suffer from three key limitations: (1) they rely on centralized privacy paradigms requiring trust assumptions; (2) they use uniform privacy budget allocation across all training epochs, ignoring the fact that model sensitivity to noise perturbations increases as training converges; and (3) they suffer from the curse of dimensionality, where high-dimensional embeddings require large noise magnitudes that overwhelm useful signal. No prior work has systematically addressed the interaction between training stage dynamics and privacy budget scheduling for LLM fine-tuning. Methodology: The paper proposes EW-FT (Epoch-Weighted Fine-Tuning), a local differential privacy framework built around two modules. The first is the Epoch-Weighted Privacy Budget Allocation (EWPBA) algorithm, which divides fine-tuning into two stages—a rapid exploration stage (first half of epochs, where lower precision is needed and more noise can be tolerated) and a precision adjustment stage (second half, where gradient sensitivity is higher and less noise is injected). EWPBA uses a binary search procedure over noise multipliers to construct a per-epoch noise schedule that respects the global (ε, δ)-DP budget while front-loading noise in early epochs and preserving budget for later ones. Privacy accounting uses the PRV Accountant via a modified ComposePRV′ algorithm that handles lists of heterogeneous noise multipliers. The second module addresses dimensionality by inserting a stacked autoencoder between the pre-noise and post-noise layers: hidden embeddings are compressed to a low-dimensional space (e.g., 768→64), Gaussian noise is injected in this reduced space, and the embeddings are then reconstructed. Noise is applied to the forward-pass embeddings (not gradients), enabling a local DP paradigm suited to distributed settings. Sensitivity is controlled by normalizing and clipping embeddings to a fixed F-norm. Key Results: EW-FT is evaluated on SST-2, IMDB, and QNLI using BERT-base-uncased at ε ∈ {2, 4, 8} (δ = 1e-5). Against the primary baseline DP-forward, EW-FT achieves average accuracy gains of 2.8% on SST-2, 2.2% on IMDB, and 1.8% on QNLI at matched privacy budgets. At ε=4 on SST-2, EW-FT reaches 86.93% vs. DP-forward's 84.17%. On IMDB at ε=8, EW-FT achieves 78.37% vs. 76.01%. Ablation studies confirm that EWPBA alone consistently improves performance across all layers, while the autoencoder provides complementary gains especially in shallower encoder layers. EW-FT also outperforms DP-SGD (a centralized CDP method) in accuracy while maintaining local DP guarantees, and the non-private ceiling ranges from 92.89% (SST-2) to 89.93% (QNLI). Strengths: - The two-stage epoch-weighted allocation is empirically motivated (Table 1 demonstrates an 8.58% accuracy drop from identical noise injected at epoch 1 vs. epoch 2), making the core insight concrete and well-grounded. - The stacked autoencoder integration addresses the curse of dimensionality with user-controllable depth and output dimension, offering a practical knob for the privacy-utility tradeoff. - The framework operates in the local DP paradigm, avoiding the need for a trusted aggregator—making it directly applicable to federated and distributed fine-tuning scenarios. Weaknesses / Limitations: - Experiments are limited to BERT-base-uncased on three classification benchmarks; it is unclear how well EWPBA generalizes to larger models (GPT-scale), generative tasks, or regression settings where epoch dynamics may differ substantially. - The autoencoder itself is trained on user data and its privacy implications are not fully analyzed—training the dimensionality reduction component could potentially leak information if not handled carefully. - The performance gap relative to the non-private baseline remains large (e.g., ~6 points on SST-2 at ε=8), and gains over DP-forward, while consistent, are modest (1.8–2.8%), suggesting room for further improvement especially at tight privacy budgets (ε=2).

#122 — Incremental (31)

Revisiting Deep Knowledge Tracing: A Comparative Analysis of RNN and Transformer Models on Interaction Encoding and Sequence Variations

scholarworks.calstate.edu · karypis - new results
Why this rank: This is a reproducibility and ablation study that clarifies a genuine source of confusion in the DKT literature, which has practical value. However, the contributions—resolving an encoding ambiguity, proposing a simpler integer encoding, and running hyperparameter sweeps—are incremental and do not introduce new models, problems, or insights beyond what systematic benchmarking reveals.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Knowledge Tracing (KT) aims to model a student's evolving knowledge state from their learning interaction history, enabling personalized and adaptive education. The seminal Deep Knowledge Tracing (DKT) model by Piech et al. (2015) has suffered from poor reproducibility in subsequent work, with reproduced AUC scores varying from 0.73 to 0.86. A key source of confusion is the ambiguous description of input encoding in the original paper—specifically, whether interactions encode question IDs or skill IDs. This paper seeks to clarify these misunderstandings, validate reproducibility, and systematically study how encoding strategies and sequence parameters affect LSTM vs. Transformer performance on KT. Methodology: The authors re-implement the original DKT RNN in PyTorch, closely following the original Lua/Torch hyperparameters (e.g., batch size of 100), evaluated on ASSISTments 2009. They also implement a TensorFlow LSTM with embedding, dropout, and layer normalization, and a Transformer with positional encoding and causal masking to prevent label leakage. They clarify the original one-hot encoding scheme: each interaction (skill_id, correctness) is encoded as a 2M-dimensional vector (M = number of skills), with the two halves representing incorrect and correct responses respectively. As a lighter alternative, they propose two encodings: (1) Tuple Encoding—a simple (SkillID, Correctness) pair, and (2) Integer Encoding—a single integer computed as 2×SkillID + Correctness. Experiments on ASSISTments 2017 systematically vary encoding method, sliding-window stride (1, 5, 10), and sequence length (10, 20, 30) to assess their impact on AUC. Key Results: On ASSISTments 2009, the re-implemented RNN achieves AUC 0.84 vs. the original's 0.86, outperforming prior reproductions (0.73–0.82). LSTM with one-hot encoding achieves AUC 0.85, while the Transformer reaches only 0.79. The proposed Integer Encoding matches LSTM one-hot performance (AUC 0.84) while reducing training time by 28% (31 vs. 43 mins) and parameter count by 20.6% (155,324 vs. 195,524). On ASSISTments 2017, encoding choice has marginal impact (LSTM: 0.82/0.80/0.79 AUC for one-hot/integer/tuple). Stride strongly affects performance: stride 1 yields AUC 0.80 for LSTM vs. 0.67–0.68 at strides 5–10. Sequence length has the largest effect: LSTM AUC rises from 0.80 at length 10, to 0.93 at length 20, to 0.97 at length 30; Transformer improves from 0.74 to 0.90 over the same range, suggesting self-attention requires longer contexts to be competitive. Strengths: - The integer encoding proposal is practically valuable: it achieves near-identical accuracy with significantly reduced computational cost (28% faster training, 20.6% fewer parameters), making it attractive for resource-constrained EdTech deployments. - The systematic ablation across encoding types, stride values, and sequence lengths on the understudied ASSISTments 2017 dataset yields clear, actionable insights about the sensitivity of KT models to these hyperparameters. - The paper resolves a genuine and documented source of confusion in the DKT literature (skill ID vs. question ID encoding), providing an explicit, illustrated explanation that aids reproducibility for future researchers. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The experimental scope is narrow: only two datasets (ASSISTments 2009 and 2017) are used, both from the same platform and domain (mathematics), limiting generalizability to other subjects or interaction structures. - The Transformer baseline appears relatively weak and undertuned—no ablation is performed on Transformer-specific hyperparameters (number of heads, layers, learning rate schedule), making the LSTM vs. Transformer comparison potentially unfair and the conclusion that "LSTMs outperform Transformers" overstated.

#123 — Incremental (31)

Построение динамической гибридной модели рекомендаций и исследования пользователей

cyberleninka.ru · 10 new citations to your articles
Why this rank: This paper combines well-established techniques — user-based collaborative filtering, matrix factorization, k-means++ clustering, and time-decay weighting — without introducing new algorithmic ideas. The engineering contribution of an hourly-refresh real-time pipeline is practical but standard for modern recommendation systems.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Traditional library book recommendation systems rely on users actively searching for titles by keyword, producing large numbers of matching results with recall rates below 35%. Users face cognitive overload when selecting from these results. Existing recommendation approaches largely overlook real-time performance requirements — in practice, user interests shift rapidly and latency directly impacts user experience quality. This paper addresses both the accuracy gap (keyword dependency, cold-start, sparse rating matrices) and the scalability gap (real-time responsiveness under high concurrent load) for library book recommendation. Methodology: The paper proposes a dynamic weighted hybrid model combining user-based collaborative filtering (UserCF) with matrix factorization (MF) via adaptive weight coefficients. The rating matrix R ∈ ℝ^(m×n) encodes explicit ratings (1–5 stars) and implicit feedback (clicks, reading time). User similarity is computed via bias-adjusted cosine similarity (eq. 2), and Top-K neighbors (K=20–50) are selected for cross-recommendation. The predicted rating for a target user is a weighted average of neighbor rating deviations (eq. 8), using similarity as weights. A time-decay clustering algorithm with decay factor λ=0.85 is applied to multi-source behavioral data to capture evolving user preferences; k-means++ clustering (optimal at k=8, achieving average intra-cluster cosine similarity 0.79, which is 41% higher than standard k-means) groups users for targeted recommendations. A real-time incremental update mechanism refreshes the rating matrix R hourly and merges streaming computations to keep latency under 300ms. The system stack is React + Spring Boot + Apache Spark, processing ~1.2 TB of behavioral logs per day via embedding points. Key Results: The proposed model outperforms the traditional hybrid recommendation (HR) algorithm by 18.7% on MAE. At K=25 neighbors, MAE reaches its optimal balance at 0.18, and recommendation diversity increases 37% compared to K=5. The improved CF algorithm consistently achieves lower standard deviation than HR across all tested neighbor counts (e.g., at K=5: 0.38 vs. 0.46; at K=25: 0.18 vs. 0.21). The time-decay clustering achieves a 41% increase in user similarity detection and 35% improvement in new book discovery. Response time remains stable under 5 seconds for up to 3300 concurrent users (300ms per request). Parallel throughput reaches 1800 requests/second, with feedback latency always below 500ms (6–50ms gap between receive and feedback speeds), supporting over 100,000 daily recommendation requests from university libraries. Strengths: - Combines dynamic time-decay clustering with hybrid CF+MF, directly addressing the staleness problem that most prior library recommender work ignores - Real-time incremental update mechanism (hourly matrix refresh + streaming merge) provides a practical, scalable architecture validated at 3300 concurrent users - Comprehensive empirical evaluation covers both accuracy (MAE, standard deviation across neighbor counts) and system performance (response time, parallel throughput) Weaknesses / Limitations: - Evaluation is conducted on an internal university library dataset without comparison against publicly available benchmark datasets (e.g., BookCrossing, LibraryThing), limiting reproducibility and generalizability - The paper does not report cold-start performance separately — it is unclear how the system handles new users or newly added books with no rating history, which is a critical practical scenario for libraries

#124 — Incremental (29)

ArabicNumBench: Evaluating Arabic Number Reading in Large Language Models

Anas Alhumud, Abdulaziz Alhammadi, Muhammad Badruddin Khan · arxiv.org · Recommended articles
Why this rank: ArabicNumBench identifies a real dissociation between accuracy and structured output reliability that matters for production Arabic NLP, and the structured-output metric is practically motivated. However, the benchmark is a self-described pilot with only 210 test cases, covers only everyday contexts, and the core finding — that few-shot CoT dramatically improves structured output — is consistent with well-established prompting literature rather than surprising.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Arabic NLP presents a unique numerical challenge: two distinct numeral systems coexist — Eastern Arabic-Indic numerals (٠١٢...) and Western Arabic numerals (0-9) — used across different regions and contexts. Existing Arabic benchmarks (ArabicMMLU, BALSAM, AraTrust) evaluate semantic understanding and factual knowledge but measure only whether answers are correct, not how they are generated or whether models follow structured output formats. This gap matters for production deployments where consistent, parseable responses are critical. Methodology: The authors construct ArabicNumBench v3, a benchmark of 210 test cases evenly split across six semantic categories: pure Eastern Arabic numerals, pure Western Arabic numerals, contextual addresses, dates, quantities, and prices (35 cases each). They evaluate 71 LLMs from 10 providers (Qwen, Mistral, OpenAI, Meta, Google, Anthropic, Cohere, DeepCogito, Microsoft, MoonshotAI) via the OpenRouter API at temperature 0.0, using four prompting strategies: zero-shot, zero-shot CoT ("think step by step"), few-shot (3 examples), and few-shot CoT (3 examples with explicit Arabic reasoning chains). A hierarchical answer extraction pipeline distinguishes structured output (GSM8K #### delimiter, Arabic CoT pattern markers, Arabic keyword markers) from fallback output (last-number heuristic, failed). Two novel metrics are introduced: Structured Output % (fraction of answers extracted via structured methods) and Format Preservation (numeral system consistency in correct answers). Total evaluations: 281 model-strategy combinations, 59,010 individual test cases. Key Results: Accuracy ranges widely from 14.29% to 99.05% across all model-strategy pairs. Few-shot CoT achieves 80.06% average accuracy vs. 28.76% for zero-shot — a 2.8x improvement — and produces structured output in 34.12% of cases vs. 7.14% for few-shot without CoT (4.8x increase). A stark capability divide emerges: only 6 models (8.5%) achieve ≥90% structured output under optimal few-shot CoT — Qwen3 Max (100%), Llama 3.1 405B (100%), Command A (99.52%), GPT-4o-mini (98.57%), Claude 3 Opus (98.10%), and Qwen3 235B (95.24%) — while 44 models (62.9%) remain below 10% structured output despite identical prompting. Critically, top-accuracy models are not the same as top-structure models: Gemini 2.5 Pro achieves 99.05% accuracy under few-shot prompting yet produces 92.86% unstructured (fallback) output. At the provider level, Microsoft (Phi-3) and Cohere show the most consistent cross-model behavior; Google and Qwen have highest peak accuracy but high variance. The Arabic CoT conclusion marker appears in 29.45% of few-shot CoT outputs but only 0–1.24% in all other strategies. Strengths: - Introduces two practically motivated metrics (structured output %, format preservation) that expose a real dissociation between numerical accuracy and production reliability, challenging the accuracy-centric benchmarking paradigm. - Large-scale systematic evaluation across 71 models, 4 prompting strategies, and 10 providers yields actionable model selection guidance for Arabic NLP practitioners. - The hierarchical extraction pipeline with method-level tracking provides fine-grained insight into how models produce answers, not just whether they are correct. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The benchmark is small (210 test cases, 35 per category), limiting statistical power and granular per-category analysis; the authors themselves call it a pilot dataset. - Coverage is restricted to everyday Arabic contexts (addresses, dates, prices); scientific, legal, and dialectal Arabic numerics are absent, reducing generalizability. - Only four prompting strategies are tested; the broader prompt engineering space (system prompts, structured output forcing via JSON mode, self-consistency) is unexplored.

A Semantic Approach for Digital Twin Cross‑Platform Interoperability: Challenges and Resolutions in AAS Data Integration

hal.science · semantic integration ontologies - new results
Why this rank: A useful engineering report documenting seven concrete AAS integration challenges and their resolutions, providing direct value to practitioners. The bidirectional conversion pipeline is competently implemented but represents standard semantic mediation work with no quantitative evaluation and behavioural portability explicitly deferred.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: As Digital Twin (DT) ecosystems grow in scale and heterogeneity, enabling seamless interoperability across platforms with different proprietary data models, communication protocols, and integration patterns has become a critical challenge. Existing approaches largely address data format and API standardization but fall short on enabling full digital twin portability — including behaviour, execution logic, dataflows, and contextual adaptation. The paper targets the foundational semantic and structural prerequisites for cross-platform DT interoperability, using the IEC 63278 Asset Administration Shell (AAS) standard as an anchor, and asks: how can a configuration be imported that enables the integration of a fully functional "digital twin" rather than merely a "digital model"? Methodology: The paper proposes a semantic, model-driven interoperability approach centered on a pivot ontology grounded in the AAS 3.1 meta-model ontology. The core technical contribution is a bidirectional conversion pipeline between AAS JSON (the format used by the JNI platform, an open-source AAS v3-compliant DT platform built on BaSyx Java SDK) and RDF TriG (the format used by Orange's Thing'in platform, a graph-of-objects semantic platform). The pipeline is implemented as a Dockerized Python mediation service using a customized and extended version of the `py-aas-rdf` library, updated to comply with AAS ontology v3.1. In the JNI→Thing'in direction, AAS shells, submodels, and concept descriptions are extracted from AAS JSON, converted to RDF, and uploaded to Thing'in via its API. In the reverse direction, a TiQL (Thing'in Query Language) query retrieves all AAS-namespace-prefixed graph elements, which are then converted back to AAS JSON and uploaded to JNI. The authors document seven concrete integration challenges encountered and their resolutions, including AASX package parsing bugs, AAS 3.0/3.1 namespace mismatches in `py-aas-rdf`, ontology design flaws (overly restrictive `xsd:string` type constraint on `aas:Property/value`), disconnected RDF subgraphs, deprecated property handling (`aas:index`), incorrect object/data property mappings for multilingual strings, and blank node scoping issues requiring the switch from Turtle to TriG format. Key Results: The prototype successfully demonstrates lossless round-trip bidirectional conversion between AAS JSON (JNI) and RDF TriG (Thing'in) for a real industrial use case. The system enables semantic augmentation of AAS-based digital twins within Thing'in's graph, supporting inference and enriched relationship reasoning. No quantitative benchmarks (latency, throughput, fidelity metrics) are reported; the contribution is primarily a feasibility demonstration and engineering report. The authors claim the shared AAS ontology pivot guarantees lossless round-trip fidelity. Future directions include LLM-based agentic approaches for automated semantic alignment and transformation configuration, working toward a "Plug-and-Play Digital Twin" vision. Strengths: - Practically grounded: the paper catalogs seven specific, concrete integration challenges with AAS 3.1 and `py-aas-rdf` and provides actionable resolutions, offering direct value to practitioners building AAS interoperability layers. - Addresses a real gap: semantic technologies are underused in operational DT interoperability; grounding the pivot in the official AAS ontology provides standards alignment and reusability. - Bidirectional symmetry: the design explicitly supports both JNI→Thing'in and Thing'in→JNI flows, enabling genuine cross-platform synchronization rather than one-way data export. Weaknesses / Limitations: - No quantitative evaluation: the paper provides no metrics on conversion fidelity (beyond claiming losslessness), performance, or scalability, making it impossible to assess real-world viability at scale. - Narrow scope — data layer only: behavioural portability, execution logic, dataflow synchronization, and context adaptation are explicitly deferred to future work, meaning the gap between a "digital model" and a fully functional "digital twin" remains unaddressed by this contribution.

#126 — Incremental (29)

LLM -Based Optimized Adaptive Threat Monitoring Framework for Malicious Domain and Adversarial URL Detection Process

iapress.org · inference optimize llm faster inference throughput - new results
Why this rank: Comprehensive integration of five techniques (meta-learning, adversarial RL, hierarchical classification, temporal modeling, federated learning) into a unified threat detection pipeline. However, the unnamed baselines ([3], [8], [18]) make results impossible to contextualize against established methods, and the kitchen-sink architecture lacks ablation studies demonstrating that each module is necessary rather than additive complexity.
Show full summary & analysis
Problem & Motivation: Traditional malicious domain and URL detection systems rely on static feature-based or signature-based approaches that fail against zero-day attacks, adversarial perturbations (homoglyphs, typosquatting, Unicode encoding, DGA-generated domains), and long-term evolving attack campaigns. Existing systems also lack granular threat attribution (classifying only benign vs. malicious rather than specific attack types), cross-organization intelligence sharing (due to privacy constraints), and time-series modeling of domain behavior. These gaps leave modern cyber defenses unable to adapt to fast-flux networks, obfuscation techniques, and stealthy botnet infrastructure. Methodology: The paper proposes a five-module pipeline framework built on a BERT-Base transformer backbone (110M parameters) pretrained on 3.2B general tokens and 180M cybersecurity-specific tokens. (1) ML-SSDG (Meta-Learned Self-Supervised Domain Generalization) applies contrastive self-supervised pretraining over a 10M-record URL dataset, then uses MAML-style episodic meta-learning with inner/outer gradient updates to generalize to unseen malicious domains without retraining. (2) RL-AdvTrain (Reinforcement Learning-Augmented Adversarial Training) models an RL-based attacker that maximizes an evasion reward function to generate adversarial perturbations (FGSM, PGD, DeepFool), while the defender minimizes classification loss on those perturbations in a closed adversarial loop. (3) HMT-TC (Hierarchical Multi-Task Threat Classification) uses multi-label binary cross-entropy with a hierarchical dependency matrix to simultaneously classify URLs across phishing, malware, botnet C2, and cryptojacking categories. (4) TMAT-STD (Temporal Memory-Augmented Transformer for Sequential Threat Detection) encodes sequences of domain activity via a memory-augmented Transformer (concatenating current input with prior memory state), computing anomaly scores as squared deviation from historical mean embeddings to detect fast-flux and DGA campaigns over 6-month DNS logs. (5) FPPTIL (Federated Privacy-Preserving Threat Intelligence Learning) aggregates local models from decentralized organizations via weighted federated averaging with Gaussian differential privacy noise (σ=0.1), enabling collective threat intelligence without raw data sharing. All five module outputs are combined via an ensemble prediction (Eq. 15) into a unified risk score driving block/monitor/alert decisions. Key Results: Evaluated on VirusTotal, OpenPhish, PhishTank, DNS-BH, CIC-IDS2018, URLhaus, and Farsight passive DNS datasets. Against three baselines (Methods [3], [8], [18]): malicious URL detection reaches precision 0.96, recall 0.93, F1-score 0.94 (vs. best baseline 0.91/0.86/0.88). Adversarial URL detection achieves 92.4% FGSM, 89.1% PGD, 90.7% DeepFool detection rates (vs. best baseline 80.2%/76.8%/79.5%). Hierarchical threat classification yields avg. F1-score 0.92 with per-category accuracy: phishing 95.2%, malware 93.5%, botnet C2 90.7%, cryptojacking 91.9% (vs. best baseline 89.8%/88.3%/85.6%/87.2%). Time-series detection achieves 91.7% recall with 3.1s detection time and 30% speed improvement over the reference baseline. Federated learning yields 89.3% global threat detection with 0.0% data leakage risk (vs. baseline best of 76.9% with 5.3% leakage). Average detection latency is 12.8ms (35% reduction vs. baseline). ML-SSDG achieves 92.3% zero-shot accuracy on novel domains. RL-AdvTrain reduces adversarial evasion rate from 33% to 8%. Strengths: - Comprehensive integration of five complementary techniques (meta-learning, adversarial RL training, hierarchical multi-task classification, temporal memory modeling, and federated privacy) into a unified pipeline that addresses distinct failure modes of prior systems simultaneously. - Strong adversarial robustness: the closed RL attacker-defender loop with continuous retraining on new adversarial samples keeps evasion rates below 10% even against GAN-generated and polymorphic URLs. - Privacy-preserving federated design achieves zero data leakage risk while improving collective detection by 30% over isolated models, enabling cross-organization collaboration that was previously impractical. Weaknesses / Limitations: - The experimental baselines ([3], [8], [18]) are not clearly identified by name or publication, making it difficult to contextualize improvements against well-known state-of-the-art systems like URLNet, LSTM-based DGA detectors, or production blocklists. - The federated evaluation uses only 5 simulated decentralized nodes, which is far smaller than real-world deployment scenarios; scalability claims (plateauing above 25 nodes) are based on simulation rather than actual multi-organization deployment. - The 10M-record dataset and NVIDIA A100 (80GB VRAM) training infrastructure represent significant compute requirements, and while model distillation to 38-45M parameters is discussed, the edge deployment results (28-35ms latency on Raspberry Pi 4) remain preliminary without full end-to-end validation of the compressed model's federated and temporal components.
